THE RED PAGE.
MY MUSE
Sometimes she moves with gracious ease
Demure, sedate, serene ;
Sometimes she lifts her draperies
And frolics like a quean.
Sometimes she comes in sad attire,
Pale-faced, prim-lipped, a prude ;
Sometimes, to set my blood on fire,
My wanton seeks me nude.
Sometimes all rouged and corseted
She comes, with perfumed breath ;
And then, while all the world’s abed,
We shake a leg with Death.
Sometimes she’s tender, trustful, true.
Maidenly, quiet, wise;
Sometimes the Lampsacene leers through
Her passion-fevered eyes.
Sometimes she feeds, in fickle mood,
Alternate hopes and fears ;
Sometimes she heats me with her blood,
Then cools me with her tears.
* Sometimes with dainty delicate
Soft hands she fondles me ;
Sometimes she spurs me with her hate
Apd cold effrontery.
Her eyes are soft with joy and pain,
And warm with life and sin—
Like twin brown lakes of autumn rain
With flecks of gold therein.
Her voice is softer than Spring’s breath
In groves of Arcady ;
But grander than the voice of Death,
And more peremptory.
She hath that girdle round her waist
Dead Venus once did use ... -
Ah ! —cruel, tender, wanton, chaste—
My light last love—my Muse !
Tas.
FRANK MORTON.
Ruskin.
CURRENT obituary notices refer -to
Ruskin’s death in his 81st year as a loss
to English letters—which it plainly is
not. It is Ruskin’s works which
establish his literary claim ; and these re-
main uninfluenced by his death, as they
would have been uninfluenced had he lagged
another score of senile years—a surpefiuous
veteran.
Ruskin the man was born rich, and he
made a good and unselfish use of his riches.
He was a liberal patron of art, spent much
in hopeless attempts to create a social
Eutopia, and gave, gave, gave to hundreds of
men and public insti t utions that seemed to him
deserving. Naturally, he failed to agree
with his wife: either she was too small to
embrace his large scheme of art and life, or
his scheme was too large to permit him to
embrace her in the fashion commonly
demanded, or -—any of the reasons which
prevent a man with his head in the clouds
from attending to the practical domestic
creature at his feet. Speculation is as use-
less as unnecessary.
Ruskin’s head was always in the clouds,
like his writings. The best excuse for his
dithyrambs is that he lived them. He was a
literary Gladstone, an aesthetic Whitefield—
a vast wave of emotion which dashed sud-
denly upon you from a vague expanse,
retreated, and lost itself in the expanse
again. The wave had no particular intellec-
tual reason or justification: its cause
was as obscure as its consequences; but
it made a furious noise, shone with
prismatic colours in the sunshine, and, in a
general way of speaking, was an impressive
natural object. When the wave fell back,
you noticed the ground was a little wet.
The patch of moisture left by the Ruskin
wave is rapidly drying. Nobody nowadays
pays any intellectual attention to Ruskin.
He was a fine emotional force in his day and
generation : for emotional people he is still
an emotional force. But intellectually he
does not count : he never did count.
How was it possible that Ruskin should
count ? He referred everything to the
Glory of God. That was the end of all art,
the aim of all human effort. Ruskin did not
know what God is, or the Glory of God.
All that he could do was to marry the
Hebrew Scriptures with a Turner sunset,
and decide that the Glory of God was the
offspring. Then he described the offspring
in an ecstasy of sonorous alliterations, and
invited you to worship. You might wor-
ship ; but you were doubtfully convinced.
Was. that the Glory of God ? . . .
The Glory of God was the end, and
the Glorification of Beauty was a
means j but the Ruskin idea of Beauty
was intellectually as incomprehensible as the
Ruskin idea of God. For consider : Ruskin
Beauty is divided into typical beauty and
vital beauty. Typical beauty is that external
quality of bodies which typifies some divine
attribute. Vital beauty is the appearance
of felicitous fulfilment of function in liv-
ing things. The forms of typical beauty
are—(l) Infinity, the type of divine in-
comprehensibility. (2) Unity, the type
of divine comprehensiveness. (3) Repose,
the type of divine permanence. (4) Sym-
metry, the type of divine justice. (5)
Purity, the type of divine energy. (6)
Moderation, the type of government by law.
The forms of vital beauty are
But that will do. Imaginative gibberish,
It seems ? Imaginative gibberish it is. So
far as theories of art were concerned,
Ruskin wrote nothing but imaginative
gibberish. But when his theories did
not come in the way foe was a s! rjwd
critic of art, and he was for a long time the
chief appreciator of art in England. The emo-
tionwhich he felt he could often give. His
vision seems sc\metimes the inspired vision
of a prophet: the eyes in that head-in-the-
clquds saw things which ordinary men
groped all their lives without perceiving:
sometimes the angel-wing of Beauty did
hash before Raskin's eyes, and sometimes
he could brilliantly describe a prismatic
feather. You must browse over Ruskin as
a horse browses over a meadow, welcoming
what is succulent and sustaining, and pass-
ing many an arid patch and many a patch
too dangerously green and shining.
_ Ruskin wrote rhetorically, oratorically :
his prose style is too continually in the
clouds where his head was The energy of
it is remarkable ; the beauty of it is some-
times remarkable ; it is frequently turgid,
and its artifices are monotonous. When a
thought can be expressed in two plain words
you do not continually desiderate a glitter-
ing fifty. Every intelligent reader of Ruskin
must pine for a diminished torrent and a
greatly-diminished spout.
When Whistler commented in 1878 on the
famous cause Whistler v. Ruskin—verdict
for plaintiff : damages one farthing—he
wrote that Ruskin’s writing was art, and his
art was unworthy his writing. It was only
latter statement which was debatable in
IKnva days we would debate both.
Ihe Ruskin wave has spent itself. And if
you look closely, you may perceive that the
ground is a little wet.
>
Gr. W. Steevens is really a loss, for he was no
extinct crater: at 30 one conceives his best work
was still to do. What he did is a series of bril-
liant flashlights upon manifestations of modern
lif e the American character, the country of
India, the recent Greek and Egyptian wars, and
so on. And there was a clever little book to hint
the author s University education—“ Monologues
of the Dead, epitomising classic characters in the
Imaginary Conversations ” way. In Steevens
a very bright eye was matched by a very
bright style. He saw instantly the pictorial or
dramatic points of a man or a scene, and flashed
them at you in words full of life and colour-
words which, to many unobservant persons, gave
better images than the things they stood-for
would have given. All brain, of course—the
biain which the local war-correspondents
lack including the power of vision, the
power of comparison, the sense of words
and the power over style. The local war-
correspondence—so unillumined, so dreary,
so absolutely inefficient—gives amazing views of
the men who write and the papers which sent them
to write. An exception is A. B. Paterson, who is
readable, at all events. Yet when The Sydney
Mail sent Paterson away there was real indigna-
tion among the tribe of local reporters. He
had no journalistic experience, no journalis-
tic training ”—that is, his mind had not been
muddled in the stupid routine of a stupid
daily-paper (which, mind you, gives its audience
what they like), and he brought a fresh eye and
an original intelligence to his work. He does
better than the others because he is better than
the others : he has a livelier brain, a livelier pen.
The mill-horse round of daily journalism is not
experience-which-teaches unless the man is teach-
able. You receive in proportion as you bring;
and the man who does n’t rise superior to his
environment is inevitably crushed by it—like the
dreary local war-correspondent.
♦
Arthur Adams’s “Maoriland and Other Verses”
receives minute verbal criticism from the energetic
and rather pedantic person who edits The Triad
(M.L.). It seems there are still people more con-
cerned with the letter which killeth than with
the spirit which giveth life. And, to give it its
due, The Triad scores^—sometimes from Adams,
sometimes from itself. Example—
Here are a few specimens of his rimes : Pansy, fancy;
furied, lurid; furrow, sorrow ; builded, unshielded;
sombre, encumber; heaven, even ; mist, as, vistas, and so
on. Mr. Adams also has a curious fancy for certain words:
Trickling, neutral, vista 3, parturient, reluctant, sudden,
iconoclast.
True: some of those rhymes (only the pedants
spell it rimes, defying overwhelming Custom, that
final arbiter) are abominable ; our poets should
take more pains. And if Adams and other
youngsters find a strange, sonorous word like
“ parturient ” they are apt to drag it in regardless
of sense—as when “ axe and torch
To the grim depths of forest fastnesses
Brought the parturient light.”
Clearly, the light is the agent;—its function is to
beget, to generate; and the forest fastnesses are
parturient, being the subject of the act. But
when The Triad remarks
The line from “ The Reaper” :
The world is drowsy, the winds asleep,
is ungrammatical. “ The winds is asleep ” !
you have an example of The Triad's ignor-
ance of grammar, and can incidentally observe the
unenlightened standpoint from which it regards
poetry. Nevertheless, the review contains much
that it would be profitable for the local poet to
consider. Poetic skill is to a considerable extent
shown by “ playing the game by the manner in
which the poet distils from uncouth words the
essence of melodious emotion. You should
conquer the canons, not disregard them or dodge
them —and a good end does not always justify bad
means. *
~
The psychology of contributors to the various
Australian war funds is interesting. It is based
on the principle of the rolling stone, which requires
considerable force to move it from rest, and com-
paratively small force to keep it in motion. There
is always more or less brain inertia to reckon with.
Many people will pass three fruit-shops displaying
grapes at 3d. per lb., and buy from a fourth shop
which sells the same grapes at 4d. : because the
repeated stimulus decides the purchase. Some
commercial travellers think it an advantage
to have a man with the same line of
goods canvass a town before them. He
awakens attention, and sets people thinking
of possible profits from handling the goods:
then the second man comes along and secures the
orders. “ The brain is to a certain extent like a
soil in which things must germinate and ripen
before they'can produce results.” So the N.S.W.
Bushmen’s Contingent Fund, starting some weeks
after the so-called Patriotic Fund, has consider-
ably surpassed it. There was needed six weeks
beating of the war - drum before the brains of
local millionaires were stirred sufficiently to over-
come the reluctance to open their money-bags.
Not till the London Dangar subscribed .£lOOO did
the Sydney Dangar subscribe £5OO. He had just
as much reason to subscribe three months ago;
but reason (in the sense of conscious intellectual
argument) has little to do with the average man’s
decisions. He is swayed rather by a' tide of
unconscious suggestions, which rises till it bursts
the barriers to action.
+
The blind ardours and furies of a crowd exhibit
a similar absence of argumentative process. A
crowd has the affectability of a woman, the incon-
sequence of a child, the destructive instincts of a
savage. When animated by a strong emotion it
exerts upon its members a distinct hypnotic
influence which sends brains toppling like card-
houses. The average man has little reasoning
capacity at any time: the average woman has
less. They are dominated by instincts and im-
pressed by images. The alleged cruelties to
Cubans might never have stirred America to war
with Spain; while the sinking of the Maine
roused the popular imagination and forced the
Government to war. The annual slaughter of
thousands of Australian infants through their
parents’ ignorance passes unnoticed by a multitude
of persons who were profoundly thrilled when
three or four babies were found in the Makins’
back-yard. And does anyone believe that the
hysterical crowd which watches the_ passage of
Transvaal contingents is acquainted with the
causes and objects of the war, or has reasoned
regarding its ethical right or wrong ?
' ——-—■— -
People are accustomed to say that man is a
rational animal. In the main he is not. The
small power of individual judgment which the
average man acquires inevitably deserts him
in emotional crises. Under the pressure of
an insistent image, or the hypnotism of
a crowd, he is as effectively derationalised
as if he were made insensible by a blow
on the back of the head. For illustration:
An article in Dec. Wide World Mu azine tells
the effect of Noise in ending a plague of tree-
caterpillars in Eastern U.S.A. The caterpillars
attack apple, maple, sugar, and plum trees, and
kill them in a year. They are not to be dislodged
by arsenic or kerosene, and before Mrs. Martin’s
discovery had to be tediously picked-off by hand.
It was Mrs. Martin who, blowing a horn to call
her husband to dinner, saw with astonishment
that the caterpillars fell from an infested tree.
Then the local band turned out—
“ Now, then,” cried the leader, ‘“Auld Lang- Syne’—
softly.” As the band slowly breathed forth the Scotch
song, the caterpillars began to move their bodies in per-
fect time to the music. The band played faster ; faster
moved the caterpillars ; and then, ere the last note died
away, they began to fall from the tree by hundreds.
The theory is that the local band and
kindred noises stupefy the caterpillars, whose
nervous system has not been tuned tp
endurance of gross sound-vibrations : conse-
quently they shiver to their fall and the final fire.
And in precisely similar fashion the blare of
military bands, the spectacle of flags waving and
men marching, the insistence on pictorial ideas
like that of “the Empire calling,” stupefy the
reasoning centres of the average person with an
undeveloped cortex to his brain. When he hears
“Soldiers of the Queen ” he begins to move his
brain in perfect time to the music. The more it
is played, the faster he moves. Then, like the
U.S.A. caterpillar, he “falls off his perch.”
The evil our friends do lives after us. Of
Stevenson’s letters, edited by Colvin, The Saturday
Revietv says that “Stevenson is a writer who has
every title to commiseration, and the appearance
of the volumes before us may be said to mark the
climax of his misfortunes.” The fullest truth
rarely consists with epigrams ; but in this epigram
there is truth enough to leave a sting. Two bio-
graphers are especially to be dreaded—your worst
enemy, and your best friend.
H. : “In Ruskin’s descriptive prose, De
Quincey may be traceable, as Christopher North
in De Quincey. John Wilson, Christopher
North, was an originator of this luscious, exuberant
writing, and some of it pours, all over the bucket,
into the unintelligible, ■’-'t not the sublime unin-
telligible of De Quincey. I take it that De
Quincey is the greatest of the trio, writing prose
simply as music, a symphony from Beethoven.
Swinburne’s magnificent prose, another form of
the “Marlowe mighty line,” is not well enough
known, and yet he cannot achieve pure poetry
in prose like De Quincey, the Shelley of that
field. Buskin, as the great pen painter, must
always have some objective kernel, whereas De
Quincey darts right away, and can even commence
building from the top. He is like the Indian
fakir, who throws up a tape, and a boy comes
down a ladder. Nobody has arisen even to emulate
him.”
“Hodgson’s Tales of Medical Students by Dr.
Ralph Hodgson of Sydney 1899 ” —a 400-page
volume with a London imprint—makes one
wager the author is a fine upstanding fellow, full
of energy and without the literary brain. His
sentences and stories flow clearly and quickly—
and give no picture, transfer no particular im-
pression, mildly interest and never arrest. The
writer fails to get his vitality into his language.
’T is a curious case; but by no means uncommon.
They are crusading in England against the split-
infinitive crusaders—and it was quite time. Not
only do most of the masters of English split; but
all have reason to. Dexterously to split your in-
finitives and indicatives is often to make your
style more lucid, more vivid, and more eloquent.
Points of Theodore Watts (and bother the
Dunton). 1. Tiny man. 2. Deafish. 3. Heavy
moustache. 4. Has slashed many authors. 5.
Particularly hates to be slashed himself.
An anonymous English writer: before 18th
century—
He or she that hopes to gain
Love’s best sweet without some pain,
Hopes in vain.
Oupid’s livery no one wears
But must put on hopes and fears,
Smiles and tears.
And, like to April weather,
Rain and shine together,
Both or neither.
During his current season at Sydney Opera
House, Howard Vernon is to produce Brunton
Stephens’s light opera “ Fayette, ” hitherto given
only in Brisbane. The score is by G. B. Allen,
a clever old musician who died in Brisbane some
two years ago, neglected and unknown.
“ George Bernard Shaw [writes The Labour
Leader ] is the greatest inventor of the age. He
invented George Bernard Shaw.”
The new Sydney quarterly, United Australia, is
an eminently solid and respectable publication;
but (in the charming American phrase) it does n’t
out ice.
Harry Morant, that hard-case bushman known
as a verse-writer over signature of “ The Breaker,”
was reported as definitely leaving Australia with
S.A. Transvaal contingent. The contributed
comment is more personal than literary—
An English weekly alleges that on the broad
beam of the mantel in an old farmhouse near
Windsor, which dates back to 1204, are these
lines—
Life is mainly froth and bubble. Two things stand lyke
stone:
Kindnesse in another’s trouble, Courage in your own.
So this also, it seems, may have to be accounted
with A. L. Gordon’s plagiarisms.
The Bookfellow.
Whatever they may say,
You ’re a fighter all the way
(Good-bye, Breaker !)
Let us put your faults behind,
Let us put your better qualities before,
For we ’re glad to hear you’ve signed, and we ’ll drink the
red night blind
To another bull-dog - fighter for the war 1
Whoever may deny ,
You ’re a plucky sort, say I,
(Good luck, Breaker !)
We have seen you go ahead
In the rattling polo-rally and the hunt;
So we ’ll hope you dodge the lead, and we ’ll fill the wine-
cup red,
To another reckless devil for the front !
W. H. O.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O Hearts Aglow!
O, Hearts aglow !
Ye fall, indeed, O Hearts, on evil days,
The World is weary grown in devious ways,
Our song-birds fly a-near with flagging wing,
Our laugh hath mocking malice in its ring,
The brows are cold and pale that loved you so
0, Hearts aglow!
O, Hearts aglow!
There lies a chilling frost upon our time,
The cheery bells of old are out of chime
Your heroes sleep among the mighty slain,
Ye seek a faith and find a fallen fane,
While Freedom’s sacred fires are dim and low
O, Hearts a-glow !
O, Hearts a-glow !
Let no such mournful music be your theme,
’Tis better far to dream your noble dream,
To hold with steady eye the fixed star
Than beat your breast at ev’ry squalid bar,
While all unheeded shine the peaks of snow
O, Hearts a-glow !
O, Hearts a-glow !
The darkest deep hath caves of fairy sheen,
The marble lies below the mud terrene,
Through cloven mountain-chasm dark and dumb
The lava-tides in lambent glory come,
From blackest clouds the floods of blessing flow—
0, Hearts a-glow !
O, Hearts a-glow !
Though Right be crushed a-while and overborne
Each glorious Dead hath Resurrection Mom,
And yet the tranced Years that sleep in thrall
Shall hear the pealing clarion call and call,
Shall leap to Life as leapt the Years Ago—
O, Hearts a-glow !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
The Boer’s Prayer.
Lord God of Battles, hear us,
Harken to our prayer,
The dreadful day draws near us
Of failure and despair.
The shriek of the war-stallions,
The cheering of the foe,
Lord of the big Battalions,
O, hear us, ere we go.
We fight and know the ending,
We fight the losing fight,
Our country’s plains defending,
We know that we are right.
We know our fate is written
Our best blood will be shed
To dye our lands for Britain
Their thrice accursed red.
Now that our bright days darken
A debt on us is laid,
Lord God of Battles, hearken
And see that it is paid ;
When those that now beset us
Shall be beset full sore,
Lord God, do not forget us,
’Tis all that we implore.
C.P.Q.C.
Mid-Forest Fear.
She is standing at the gate,
Tall and sweet,
And although the hour is late
She will greet
Me, her lover,
Smiling over
Absent mind and tardy feet.
“Rest,” I’ll say to her, “ and more rest,”
As she wraps her love around me,
And I’ll tell her of the forest,
Of the strange, fear-haunted forest,
Where the fleshless beings found me.
For I trod a rock-strewn rude way
Thinking only of my lover,
When the moonlight on the woodway
Made a weird-way of the woodway,
And a place where demons hover.
For the leaves that had been sleeping
On the sodden soil-bed lying,
Took a motion and ’gan creeping,
Like a thousand small feet creeping,
And there rose a distant sighing.
Why the trees did droop their tresses,
Weeping leaves for something under,
And what bode in dim recesses,
Feline-lurked in dim recesses,
Paled my cheeks and heart to ponder.
Had I feet I would have hurried,
But the moonlit forest chained me,
Soul and body grasped and worried,
With frost-fingers gripped and worried,
Till, half-stayed, my hurt heart pained me.
“ Rest,” I’ll say, “ my Love, and more rest,
Things unseen have life and motion
And they haunt the moonlit forest—
Soul-affronting haunt the forest,
And men meet them on the ocean.
She will look so grave and kind,
Saying “ Rest —
Rest is here for heart and mind
On this breast—
Put aside all
Fancies idle,
I will shield you— Love is best.
RODERIC QUINN.

SATURDAY, FEB^^l9oo^
The Empire, Right or Wrong.
It has obviously come to that now. There
was a time when Britain might have backed
out of the Transvaal war without making an
unusually large dint in the Ten Command-
ments or (what counts for a lot more than
the Ten Commandments) in its own in-
terests ; but that is all over. After being
held at bay by two flyspeck states for four
months—after raking up its best generals
and collecting troops from England, Scot-
land, Ireland, Wales, Canada, India, and all
the seven Australias—after the defeats,
petty as they were, of Magersfontein and
Nikolson’s Nek and Spion Kop and Tugela
River—after revealing the fact that its
Intelligence Department didn’t know enough
to provide itself with reliable maps of its
own province of Natal—it is evident that
Britain has got to win, even if all the
Decalogue goes overboard and the Sermon
on the Mount is publicly suspended as a
beautiful theory that doesn’t suit the
times and the circumstances. The world
has already set down the British mili-
tary authorities as an ass, by reason of
their perpetual unreadiness, and though
the outside world is mostly the same kind
of ass when the same conditions arise, that
doesn’t in the least alter the value of its
judgment. If Britain doesn’t win now the
world will set it down as not even a valiant
and dangerous ass, and there are terrible
possibilities about such a conclusion. The
merits of the original case remain where
they were, but Spion Kop was the last straw
which materially altered, the circumstances
of it. The spectacle of the hugely rein-
forced British army, after spending weeks
in finding out exactly where it was, scram-
bling forward under all its collection of pet
generals to make the same old blunder, has
created an impression abroad that Britain
hasn’t the leaders it' once had. No empire
can afford to inquire what is right or what is
wrong after that impression has got abroad.
The only thing left is to do whichever pays
best with all possible vigour.
• * * •
As a land Power the British empire lives
on bluff. It has a reliable white population
not much larger than that of France, no
larger than that of Germany, only about half
that of Russia. It has more frontiers to
defend than all three of them, and more
strange niggers and aliens to keep down
than all three of them twice over. It has
an army in actual training that the smallest
of the three could outnumber four times
over. In every great war for the last two
centuries, that with the revolted American
colonies alone excepted, it has had valuable
allies, but no country can depend on
always having allies. And as the spread
of railways makes outlying British territories
more accessible by land and less defensible
by sea, the bluff grows bigger. A nation with
such a stupendous amount to do and so little
preparation for doing it has to keep up a
reputation for invincibility, even if it sells
its immortal soul to do it. The present war
is a very small affair, but through strange
blundering in high places it has been very
unlucky for its size. And if it left off now a
great part of the world would think it had
been really a large affair, and it would pro-
bably become necessary very soon after to go
and pick a quarrel with somebody else in
order to demonstrate that the Empire isn’t
decrepit after all. It has become a genuine
case of “The Empire, right or wrong.” The
necessity for Britain to win a complete vic-
tory has become so absolute that it doesn’t
matter in the least now whether the Empire
is right or wrong.
* * • •
Presumably every person of British
descent hopes in his heart that the empire
will win. This great Christian nation
is just plain, ordinary heathen at
bottom—just as heathen as if it worshipped
a stuffed animal with a glass eye on
some coral island. It doesn’t love its
neighbor as itself. It doesn’t judge his case
as it would its own. It doesn't believe that
the meek shall inherit the earth—it knows
better than that. When the crisis comes
it forgets all about its alleged gos-
pel of peace; its churches and cathedrals
represent only 1900 years of wasted brick
and profitless mortar; its preachers are
just as human as the priests of Baal
in old Assyria. From one end to
another the clannish tie of common blood
and a common language asserts itself above
the veneer of a bogus Christianity. And if
the pretence that this country takes any in-
terest in the merits of the case were only
dropped—if it were only announced that
Christianity was regretfully suspended and
the Law and the Gospel hung up under
compulsion till the war was over, and that a
Power which was going to be an honest,
hard-fighting heathen till further orders
was going in to win, right or wrong—
things would be a lot easier.
* • *
Britain must needs take the Transvaal
and the Orange State now and kill a very
large number of Boers, in order to prove that
Tugela and Spion K«»p and Magersfontein
were mere accidents and small ones at that,
and that the empire isn’t in the least de-
crepit after all. If it doesn’t prove this in
South Africa the inevitable result will be
that it will presently have to prove it some-
where else against a much larger enemy, and
kill a much larger number of people in the
process. Something must be run into, and
it has become a case of running into the
cheapest thing available. The only thing
that can be done to alleviate the heathenism
of a hard situation is to give these dead
Boers a decent tombstone and a kindly men-
tion in history. A people whose empire is
built up of such a vast straggling multi-
tude of strange races and unknown
quantities as the British empire is, is held
together by its prestige, and it dare
not show even the appearance of de-
feat by a foe so very much less in point of
size. Every military nation has to keep
up the great military bluff. The enormous
effort required to down the two republics is
itself a disaster. Not to down them after
making the effort would be worse disaster.
* * * *
As things are now the necessity of carrying
the war to ar-successful conclusion at any cost
is sufficient explanation without saying any-
thing about the morality of it. One
necessity is worth a hundred moralities. The
cant of the Christian Jingo is a sickening
CHADBAND-ism, but if there is an opening
for any kind of Jingoism which doesn’t mix
up the patter of piety with the necessity
for doing . impious things, The Bulletin
doesn’t mind making one. It is not a
case of blood being thicker than water
—it is a case of blood being thicker
than Christianity. If the blessing of the
church could be lifted off the British army
in South Africa—if preachers and other
weary characters in black hats and black
coat-tails would give up trying to fit in
their theoretical gospel of peace and non-
resistance with the practical heathenism
which makes war and big guns and conquest
unavoidable, and would leave off pro-
fessing to manufacture a square circle
and a globular triangle then Jingo-
ism would become respectable. For Britain
to back out of the Transvaal war now with-
out gaining everything it has ever demanded,
and possibly a great deal more, would be
strictly in accordance with the teachings of
that divine sermon which is the epitome of
its alleged religion, but it would almost cer-
tainly bi'ing abouc such disaster in later
years that the British nation would wish it
had never heard of its religion. And but
for the habit of trailing a wholly unsuit-
able creed at the tail-end of a now unavoid-
able errand of destruction, the errand would
look quite respectable.
The Common, Sordid Bill.
There are many aspects of patriotism*, and within
the last few months Australia has seen all of them
save one. And as that One is about the most
important of them, all, The Bulletin proposes to
bring the Want of it under public notice. So far
Australia has done everything on Britain’s behalf
that it really liked to do—everything. that was
pleasant—everything that was a relief in the dull-
brown and sage-green monotony of life, and a
joyful stirring up of the dregs of daily existence.
For the sake of Britain many adventurous young
men have gone off on a pleasant voyage, in a
pleasant ship, a,t a very decent wage, and gone on
an errand that is probably no more deadly than
facing the fevers and spears of Papua While
gold-seeking at no wage at all. For Britain’s
sake many people have had sundry pleasant
holidays, seeing the contingent off, and many
have kissed the nice-looking young men Of
the contingents, and the contingents have
kissed many nice girls ; and some few people have
had very excellent victuals and drink at patriotic
banquets; and almost everybody has had great
and pleasant excitement reading the war news in
the papers ; and the papers have made more
money than usual; and a few very wild enthusiasts
have had a good time throwing stones at persons
supposed to be disloyal, or trying with righteous
indignation to get them ousted from their billets
wherefore there will be more billets for people
who are loyal. For the sake of the empire this
country has drank, shouted, sang, kept holiday,
gone to special patriotic theatrical snows, and
written to the papers about its own virtues and
the vices of the other fellow wbo doesn’t hold the
same opinion as it does itself. It has had extra
horse-races and bike-races and picnics for the
sake, of the empire. Out of its love for the
empire it has risen up and done everything
that a pleasure-loving and adventurous people
wouldn’t have missed doing for a lot of money in
any. case, and it has taken great credit to itself for
having this good time. Yet the one thing that
was disagreeable and sordid and hard and neces-
sary it has left undone with absolute unanimity.
Its love for the empire didn’t go so far as that.
It has made no provision for paying the bill.
* • • *
The bill will be a large one, and either this
generation must pay it, or it must be openly or
surreptitiously added to the public debt and left
to posterity to pay. The Bulletin votes that
this generation pays it like a man. If it doesn’t—
well, every Jingo who won’t face the payment
of the bill is a traitor to the empire, even
though he yells anti-Boer sentiments till his
voice cracks, and breaks 48 windows in the
houses of allegedly disloyal citizens, and sings
the “Absent-minded Beggar” all night, and
assaults 144 miscellaneous and suspected foreigners
in. as many public-houses, and throws his hat two
miles, high in his exuberance. If this country isn’t
sufficiently loyal to the empire to face the only
necessary concomitant of loyalty which has no
pleasing features about it, then its alleged loyalty
is only sounding brass and a tinkling cymbal. It
is hardly even respectable enough to be called a
cymbal, and partakes rather of the nature of a
cheap mouth-organ, or a comb covered with
brown-paper. The payment of the bill is the only
thing that represents hard, dull unemotional
grind. There is no excitement or pomp about it.
It. signifies less luxury or more work—more strug-
gling up and down a plough-furrow, more urging
of the dismal poley-cow on the way it should go,
more sordid bootmaking or tree-felling or digging
out of coal. Anyway, it signifies more misery.
The bill has to be paid whether the cause for
which it was incurred is good or bad. The Jingo
crowd in general asserts that the cause
is a good one, and yet hasn’t said a
word, about any special measures to meet
the inevitable outlay, The Bulletin doesn’t
think it a good one, but it recognises that it is a
mean thing to leave future generations to pay for
this generation’s loyalty; also, that it is a degrad-
ing thing for this generation to have the kudos and
the excitement, and the holiday and the banners,
and the advertisement and the V.C.—and pass the
pain and the sordid drudgery of the business on to
the infant. So it proposes that the people of to-
day should pay up.
* * * •
So far, they have done next to nothing in that
direction. The Patriotic Fund is supposed to be
for the injured and the widows and orphans, but
it. leaves the ordinary daily expenses of the expe-
dition quite unprovided for. The Bushman’s
Fund is for a special purpose, though it won’t
cover more than a fraction of the expense even
there, and it also leaves the expenses of the regular
contingents quite unprovided for. These expenses
will be very Heavy, yet, even in the wildest hour
of popular fervor, not one G-ovemment' in the
seven dared to propose levying a special tax to
cover this heavy special outlay. N.S.W., where ex-
penditure is even now some £300,000 ahead of re-
venue; has already charged part of its war-bill to
loans in a small, unobtrusive way. What is called
the “ reserve stock of ammunition ” was bought
out of previous loans,, and it has mostly gone to be
used up in South Africa, so it is to he replaced by
buying another “ reserve stock of ammunition ” to
the extent of £lOO,OOO out of borrowed money.
By this means, a great part of the outfit of the
troops is charged to posterity. Then, to relieve
the revenue and enable it to bear the strain of pay-
ing the N.S.W. Contingent’s wages, an unusual
number of small works which would otherwise be
charged, to revenue, are charged to loans instead.
This is just the same as paying the Contingent’s
4s. 6d. a day out of loans direct—only not quit® so
honest. S.A. reckoned, before the present excite-
ment started, on having a surplus of exactly £195
for the year, and Victoria anticipated a surplus of
£33,910. Victoria now reckons on a deficit and on
stopping the payments iA reduction of the old
shortage. S.A. doesn’t say what it reckons on.
Neither province has made any arrangements
to meet the expense involved by its outbreak
of loyal enthusiasm; so apparently it will simply
be passed on to. posterity in the shape of so
much extra deficit for posterity to pay off. And
the other provinces are m the same situation, more
or less.
* * » *
. Whether the bill involved in Australia’s yeam-
mg-to help the empire is paid directly out of bor-
rowed money, or indirectly out of borrowed money
by charging a large number of other things to
loans so as to ease the revenue and enable it to
stand the strain, or by stopping the liquidation of
our own old deficits thereby leaving more of our
deficits for our children to pay, or by adding to
the deficit with the same result, it all comes to
the same thing. It all boils down to the fact that
the people who had no hand in making the war
are to face the one grievous trouble and responsi-
bility therein involved. It all amounts to the
fact that the big grown man of to-day won’t
carry his own burden and piles it on to the
narrow shoulders of the infant—which is ail
inexpressibly mean thing to do in anv case. And
it is a very dismal commentary on this country’s
alleged devotion to the empire’s interests. A
community has presumably the right to spend its
own money in aily cause, but it has no right to
spend other people’s money by way of demonstra-
ting its own patriotism. If the amount is large
the wrbng done to posterity is so much the worse.
If it is small, the meanness of the present patriot
who wouldn’t even make a small sacrifice for his
alleged principles is so much the more glaring,
Yet so far not a soul, to The Bulletin’s know-
ledge, has suggested that the people who get the
glory and the excitement should face the disagree-
able aspect of the case also. The nearest approach
thereto has been a patriotic suggestion that A
new. stamp should be issued, and the proceeds
of its sale Used towards War expenses—which
simply means, after all, that the public Would use
fewer of the existing stamps, so what was gained
in this direction would be lost to the postal
revenue in another way, and the deficit that would
ensue would be left unprovided for just as it is
now,
* * * •
Britain also charges part of its war-bill to loans;
but then Britain manfully pays off its debt at the
rate of millions a year in time of peace ; Australia
never pays off anything. What the case calls for
here is a special tax to cover the war invoice, so
that this generation can say that its hands are
clean, and that its patriotism is honest, and that
its alleged willingness to make sacrifices for the
empire isn’t all a sham. An extra 3d. in the £
income-tax would cover the expenses in five Or si*
years at most. So Would a poll-tax, or art addi-
tional land-tax, or a bicycle licence duty, Or arty
one of a dozen more or less unusual expedients.
That would be a real proof of devotion to the im-
perial ideal. All the cheering, all the wild cries
of “she bumps” and other like irrelevancies, all the
kissing of the contingenters, and the holiday-
making, and. the joy of reading the cablegrams,
and the thrill of aiming half-a-crown at the
eye of the girl who sings a Kipling ballad, and
the extra newspaper circulation that arises from
the .war, and the profitable meat contracts, and
the joy of havinga big advt. through sending a
cheque to the Patriotic Fund, and the extra
drinking of the Queen’s health, and the fun of
running after suspected Boer sympathisers with
road-metal —all of these prove nothing. They
don’t provide a copper towards the big inevitable
bill for. the actual cost of Australia’s share in the
campaign, and so long as this country remains in
that state the alleged readiness to perish for the
empire is a thing to be viewed with doubt if not
utter scorn. Even the fact that a lot of people
are willing to go, at 4s. 6d. a day, to have a thrill-
ing adventure, doesn’t necessarily prove very much.
There is doubtless a vast amount of real enthusi-
asm among them ; still Britain could have raised
10,000 very decent soldiers on the same terms
among the wild-cat republics of South and Central
America if she had wanted to try such an experi-
ment. They might not care a straw for Britain
itself, but in half the countries of the earth it is
unhappily the case that there are thousands of
brave men who would fight hard for any country
whose 4s. 6d. a day is as reliable as Britain’s is.
And the fact that the rest of the community stays
at home in a state of enthusiasm, and refuses to
burden itself with any provision for paying the
4s. 6d. doesn’t prove much either. No Ministry
wants to close its financial year with a discredit-
able shortage, and it is tolerably certain that most
of them would have proposed extra taxation to
cover the extra expense if they had believed the
popular fervor was deep enough to stand that.
Apparently none of them thought it Was deep
enough. Possibly they were wrong, but there is
up to now a grievous lack of evidence about their
wrongness. Anyhow, this anti-jingo paper is the
first to propose that the present generation should
make a genuine, all-round sacrifice for the empire.
Will some prominent jingo rise and second the
proposition ?
The S.A. Copper Boom.
Dear . Bulletin. —Not so many years ago the
Adelaide press used to refer to that portion of
S.A. north of Port Augusta as “our magnificent
Pastoral Country”—and so it was then—to some
extent at least. Subsequently Playford gave the
lands to farmers, rents were raised, runs aban-
doned one by one, and the Government now keeps
a great part of the country as a dog-reserve. Tne
farmers wouldn’t, or rather couldn’t, pay rents, and
the Government dare not turn them off the land,
because the men are so frail, weary and worn they
would fall down if hustled, and, besides, there is no
where else to put them. So what with Playford,
and take-all, smut, farmers, rabbits, locusts, mice,
2 bushel averages, red rust, drought, hot winds,
low prices, wild dogs and sundries, the press got
ashamed of mentioning the GoD-forsaken waste.
After the rabbits had eaten the scrub, and cotton,
salt, and blue-bush appeared spasmodically
where it used to grow luxuriantly, and
the country generally . was snail - bald, a
small rise in copper reminded the S.A. papers
that the north was one mass of high - grade
metalliferous ores, and that they had said so many
times before; and they boldly referred in leaded
type to “Our Immense Mineral Resources in the
North.” Their best young men were sent forth
to interview all such interested parties as pub-
licans, storekeepers, etc., and their views issued
broadcast to the. public. Some old local preacher,
who had been in Queensland for the past twenty
years, told the Register the copper was there, if
they could only find it. Some idiot wrote
that an area of 5000 square miles of mostly
horizontal slates should be prospected by diamond
drills, and that a school should be formed to train
prospectors in the use of the Rontgen Rays, etc.
The Old Northerner laughed, and recognised that it
was about time the old bald North should have its
periodical copper boom, and so he hired some
sandy-blighted fossil to peg out under a new name
the old claim he had sold in 1888, and sold before
that m a previous copper boom in 1873. Then the
typical Old Northerner sent a telegram to the
press about an important copper discovery, and
sat in his burrow and waited. And they came,
the syndicators and a fresh crop of experts that
would buy anything green, and matters began to
look up. All this time the papers were hammering
away that what was wanted was men of abilitv,
integrity and - capital to take the matter in hand,
and two or three philanthropic W.A. millionaires
tumbled—and let the public have one-tenth of a
second-hand surface show for £IO,OOO.
* * • •
Soon the boom was at its height, and telegrams
hummed like bees on the wires to tell that one
truck of ore had passed through Farina last week,
consigned to Wallaroo, and shares went up,
and all the mines had big lodes, whips of
ore, &c. ; and assays were circulated—and—
you know the old game. After a short time


inquisitive shareholders, of the stamp that
ought to be put down by some short Act,
began to ask why that truck of ore didn’t
arrive. Then the companies which had the most
ore could not get it carted—because no respectable
beast of burden would live in that blasted desert.
Some mines had lots of ore too poor to transport
and wanted water for concentrating. Others (the
most of them) had no ore, but proudly explained
that they had a well within a mile of them. The
more honest companies wound up. And so the
Boom burst, and you will hear no more about the
mineral resources until the Federal Parliament is
asked to sanction the making of a line from Her-
fott to the Queensland border. The GoD-knows-
ow-manyth S.A. copper bubble has gone to
pieces, and the truck of ore that passed Farina has
evaporated. But seriously, if all this wealth is
there —and if it ever was there it is there still, for
no one has taken it away—do the South Aus-
tralians honestly believe in their mineral resources;
and if they did would they develop them ? I say
no.
* * • *
In the desert 300 miles N.W. from Port
Augusta, is a place called Wilgena—similar to the
worst of W.A. country—sand, salt-lakes, and
granite. From this sheol a prospector sent
down a lump of silver lead to get assayed. It
turned out to be tin, good enough to eat, showing
60 per cent, of metal. Careful and separate en-
quiries left no doubt that the specimen had come
from Wilgena, and other rich samples of the same
distinctive character were received. There was
obviously an opening for a bona-fide prospecting
syndicate. Such a syndicate was formed with the
assistance of a lawyer. A good man was selected
and sent up. The man was new to the game, and
did not understand what was required from a
prospector with an £BOO S.A. syndicate at his back.
He should have slumbered in his bunk and waited
till the camels that were not sent for came in, then
shoo’d them off again. He didn’t. He made a
mistake, and is a disgrace to the genus prospector,
for he quickly got some men together and did some
good work. On receipt of his first report instruc-
tions were sent to him to sell all plant and return.
Wonderingly he came back 600 miles, and at the
winding-up meeting the lawyer explained to him
that the syndicate was sadly disappointed. It had
spent £IOO in Adelaide on stationery, and the
prospector’s travelling expenses had cost £25, and
he had wasted three weeks valuable time prospect-
ing, and wilfully squandered £25, and with that
time and capital he had not dropped on a tin
mountain valued at £20,000,000, and they were
going to wind up, and the loss of that £25 would
be a warning to them never to engage that pros-
pector again. And they wound up. S.A.’s
struggles to develope the immense mineral wealth
of the North are so violent that the province will
break asunder in the back some day.—l.H.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Melbourne Tramways ancj the Minimum
Wage.
Vio. Leg. Assembly was last week invited to give
practical and specific effect to its alleged belief in
the minimum-wage theory—and it wasn’t game.
The occasion was the taking through committee of
the amending Tramway Bill. The first clause,
enacting that the days of labor should be eight
hours, and that any time beyond that limit should
be paid for as time-and-a-quarter, met with con-
siderable opposition from the Tory irreconcilables.
Irvine, Attorney-General in the alleged-Liberal
M'Shiel’s Ministry, with that nice perception of
the interchangeability of black and white and
that due regard for dubious phraseology that
characterise the law, argued lengthily, but
weakly, that the special payment for overtime
mentioned in the original Act merely meant ordi-
nary payment for “extra overtime.” Ex-Premier
Turner, having recovered some of his old-time
backbone, took a much firmer stand than when he
aforetime rose from the Treasury bench. After
telling the House that, for over two years, he had
vainly endeavored to come to some reasonable
understanding with the company of sweaters, he
declared straight-out that—speaking for the Oppo-
sition —his view was that the original Act declared
a week’s work to be 48 hours, and that ‘‘special
payment” meant just what it said—a special pay-
ment, and something over and above the ordi-
nary rate of payment. Finally the House
decided, on the voices, that 48 hours should con-
stitue a week’s work and that overtime should be
paid for at time-and-a-quarter. Then the second
clause, providing a minimum wage of 7s. per day,
was negatived without debate, and an amendment
providing a weekly minimum wage of £2 shared a
similar fate. In the absence of any minimum
wage the proviso that overtime shall be paid for as
time-and-a-quarter is of very little use. If the
hours are 60 per week and the wage is Bd. per
hour with no allowance for overtime it is obviously
£2 a week. And if the hours are 48 per week and
the overtime is time-and-a-quarter, and the wage
is 7M. an hour for the regulation 48 hours and the
12 hours overtime are a quarter more per hour,
that is just as obviously £1 19s. per week.
Making the co. pay extra for overtime is no con-
cession unless it is also provided that the extra
money won’t be deducted from the money paid for
ordinary time. Then the rueful-looking M ‘lntyre
and the equally fearsome Gillies proposed that
the co. should be allowed to deduct £lO,OOO per
annum from its payments to the sinking-fund, to
recoup itself for this alleged loss in giving the men
the 48 hours to which they have always been
entitled. Seeing that, by paying their men at the
ordinary rate instead of the special rate provided,
the company has diddled them out of between
£15,000 and £20,000, this proposal was a choice
specimen of frozen gall, and it is pleasing to note
that the House promptly squashed it. The bill
was then passed on to the House of 48 Rich Land-
lords, and as to what may happen there no man
knows, for the 48 Immoveables are in a bad temper
just now, and have been amending the Factories
Bill to such an extent as to cause a block.
Probably the House of Levi, the Jew, and Cor-
nelius Job Ham, which isn’t a nice thing to sit
alongside Levi, will deposit the Tramway Bill in
the gutter with the remark that the time has not
yet arrived for legalising the eight-hour system,
and that the interests of the shareholders must be
respected, and that the conditions of a contract
must not be lightly interfered with ; regardless of
the fact that one of the parties has persistently
and wilfully broken most of those conditions from
the very start. One incident of the debate is so
very unusual as to almost take the breath away.
One M.L.A. (Gillott), refused to vote on the
question as he was a tramway shareholder, and
therefore an interested party. Gillott is quite a
new member, and is, politically speaking, young
and honest; he will acquire wisdom by-and-Sye.

The Moral Caterpillar.
Otm old friend, the Jingo man’s one solitary hunk
of argument, turns up again—this time in the cor-
respondence columns of the Star :— •
(To the Editor of the Australian Star.)
Sir,—l have read with pleasure in your paper the article
dealing with The Bulletin’s attitude towards England in
the present war. I think it very ridiculous that The
Bulletin should imagine that it knows more than the
Government of England about the rights of the war.
According to The Bulletin’s own arguments the majority
is always right, and I do not think it can claim a majority
against the Government and people of England, and the
thousands of people inhabiting her colonies. Even if it
could do so, I think that it is a most miserable and mean
proceeding to try and villify, and dishonor, the country
from whence it sprung, under whose laws it is enabled to
make a living, and to enjoy the privileges of citizenship.
—Yours, &c., Jackeroo.
Of course it is ridiculous for The Bulletin to
know more about the rights of the war than the
“ Government of England.” And, in addition, it
is ’wildly ridiculous for the Star to pretend to
know more about fiscal matters than the “Govern-
ment of England,” and when it professes to know
in its own small way that Protection is the best
thing when the British Government knows that
Freetrade is best it presents a regretable spectacle.
Likewise its conduct in knowing more about the
Dretfus case than the French Government,
and more about how the Transvaal should be
managed than the Transvaal Government, are
painful examples of the small boy giving good
advice to his parent. As to the conduct of the
Star and all the other Australian dailies in profes-
sing to know better than the British Government
how the war should be conducted, and giving
hints to British strategists, it is simply a disgust-
ing case of high treason, aggravated by ingrati-
tude, incivility and procrastination. For want of
space, no further allusion will be here made to the
smfulness of the Star’s many articles on the suf-
fering and poverty of England—said to arise from
Freetrade —and the ridiculousness of its profes-
sions that it knows better how the English social
problem should be tackled than the British
Government itself. The Jingo crowd in Australia
(which includes almost all this country’s prominent
politicians) tries to lay it down as an axiom that
on this one matter there must be no independent
thought or independent judgment. Yet it hasn’t
the courage of its own alleged opinions. It daren’t
set up the theory that a State-subsidised Church,
or privately-owned railways, or unpaid members
of Parliament must be the right thing because
Britain says they are. It won’t start paying off
its public debt out of revenue because Britain
holds that is the right thing to do. It daren’t
allege that the British Government was necessarily
right when it held that 30,000 men were sufficient
te extinguish the war, though that Government’s
utter ignorance of Transvaal affairs on that
point seems to imply that, it might be equally
ignorant on others. It is too late to hold
up the Government which made so many blunders
as infallible. The individual whq believes
in the absolute justice of the war from his
own intelligent study of the case is one that no
one can take objection to, and the one who be-
lieves that it is absolutely necessary now for Bri-
tain to win, be the merits of the case what they
may, is undoubtedly right. Britain can’t afford
to lose now, even if its case was a thousand times
worse than it is. But the person who alleges that
the war must be just.because the British Govern-
ment says so is not a man —he is only one more of
the Servile Things that Crawl. He is the material
that slaves are made of. And just now Australia
is unfortunately pretty well covered with Servile
Things that Crawl. It looks like a place infested
with moral caterpillars.
Victoria’s Beautiful Penal “ System.”
The state of the penal system in Victoria, at pre-
sent, as set forth m the House by M. L. A. Hume
Cook, constitutes a tremendous indictment of the
incapacity and negligence of previous chief secs,
of that province. Cook showed beyond question
that the present alleged system is not only not
reformative, but it treats old long-sentence men
more leniently than “ short-'timers ” and first
offenders, and year by year it increases the per-
centage of “old hands.” The present Inspector-
General introduced an original idea by which male
prisoners spend the last ten days prior to their
release in separate confinement, while women are
so separated for 30 days. In answer to complaints
the LG. said that this tended to prevent their
coming back after release. The lie direct to
which fanciful allegation is given by the fact that
during the past two years the local police have
been habitually requested to re-arrest discharged
prisoners at Pentridge gates on the ground of ill-
health-, and have them returned for further
separate treatment ; and also by the particularly
damning fact that while, in ’BB, the number of
prisoners with at least three convictions was 2000,
the percentage steadily increased until in ’9B, out of
a smaller total number of prisoners, the ‘ ‘ old hands ”
with three or more convictions were over 3000.
Furthermore, since ’95, though the daily average
of prisoners had not varied to any great extent,
the number of cases of sickness steadily increased
from 1044 in ’95 to 1200 in ’96, 1376 in ’97, and
1400 in ’9B. But the most convincing fact —the
fact that shows-up most distinctly the utter rotten-
ness of the whole system of treatment —is that,
during the last ten years, no less than 423 prisoners
have been transferred to lunatic asylums. Of
these, 92 have been removed during the last three
years, and the Inspector-General’s report on these
92 states that in three cases such insanity origi-
nated in gaol, in four others the originating cause
was unknown, while of the remaining 85 nothing
is said; the inference forcing itself, therefore,
that either Victoria is in the habit of gaoling its
lunatics, or else its gaols are a vast breeding-
ground for insanity germs. Another point is that,
during the last five years, while the number of
prisoners had decreased by one-sixth, the number
of punishments inflicted in gaol had increased
by one-fourth —most of these punishments being
inflicted in camera by a couple of magistrates,
whose powers, often utilised to their full extent,
far exceed those of the Supreme Court Judge who
originally sentenced the prisoner. Once again let
it be stated: No -prisoner should ever have his
sentence increased or extended except by trial
before a jury in open Court. Even the slow-going
fogies of Westralia begin to recognise that, while
England has long since granted this measure of
justice. It is interesting and characteristic of
Victoria to learn that the penal authorities have
recently abolished the evidence-book placing on
record the punishments inflicted in prison, so that,
unless information be voluntarily furnished to the
press, no man knows what goes on inside the iron
gates, or how many men have had their sentences
increased, or how long some of them have been
there. The beneficial effects of publicity have
been wholly cut off. In fact, if any man once gets
into gaol on a six months sentence, there appears
to be no certainty that he will ever get out,
especially if he should happen to incur the enmity
of some unscrupulous official. In reply to these
and other charges of negligence and conniv-
ance at cruelty and brutality, Minister M'Coll
weakly promised an investigation into the
matter by the officials of. the department—
an investigation by the persons accused,
who will sit in judgment on themselves—
which is just as much of a farce as expecting a
criminal to catch himself and bring himself to
justice. The charge is that the department is
stupid, brutal, ignorant, and antiquated, that its
methods are out of date, that. it is going back-
wards instead of forward, that it is a failure and a
disgrace, and not worth its salary and its salt.
And M‘Coui/, the feeble, clumsy M‘Coll, pro-
mises to let the department inquire into itself, and
look at its own long ears in the glass, to decide if
it is really the ass and worse that the evidence,says
it is.

Percy Wilkinson, in his valuable criticism of the
Victorian wine industry, leaves the adulterators
without a leg to stand upon. The Victorian
climate, says P.W., offers a wine-maker no excuse
for introducing salicylic acid into the business, for
he can, by taking trouble, control the temperature
of fermentation and the proportion of acidity in
the must, as is done in the hot climates of Southern
France, Algiers and California, and by the more
progressive Australian vignerons. “ The foreign
wine-maker can (and does) produce a wine and
send it across the equator, store it for years,
and sell it in perfect condition in a country
where the use of salicylic acid is not for-
bidden, without condescending to avail him-
self of the free opportunity for adulteration.”
Mr. Wilkinson’s story is the same old tale that all
seemingly competent critics have told. The
grapes are good, the climate is favorable, and only
man is vile where the wine industry is concerned.
One Government vineyard, managed by a couple
of enthusiastic experts on the most conservative
principles for ten years, would probably astonish
the natives with its matured results attheendof the
period. But no Government cares to start a work
whi§h will reflect no glory on anybody for the next
ten years. Political humanity is not built that
way.
The spy fever has fairly got hold of the Sydney
Pastoralists’ Union. Some men who have joined
or want to join as Absent-minded Bush Beggars
have been secretly denounced (“Delated” is the
old 17th century term, according to Roger North)
as individuals who don’t thoroughly approve of
the war. The charges come by post, and are
mostly anonymous. And the bosses of the move-
ment are so serious in the idea that the country is
in peril and the enemy howling at the gates, that
it sends these beery jokes on to the Brigade Office
so that vast precautions and cannon and entrench-
ments may be taken on the subject. Says the
S.M. Herald :
In all cases the military authorities are informed, and
the chief staff-officer, Colonel Mackenzie, takes the
necessary precautionary measures. The suspected men
are not at once drummed out, but unless inquiries abso-
lutely prove the suspicion arrant nonsense, the suspects
fail in their tests or are not ultimately called on to enlist.
When a letter comes saying that the unknown
writer heard Billjim speak disrespectfully of
Britain, or Europe, or the equator while under the
influence of the rum that has plug tobacco in it
the “necessary precautions” are taken—the way
Belshazzar didn’t take them in Babylon, where-
fore Cyrus burgled the city late at night
and the empire fell. It all sounds beauti-
fully large and dark and military, and sug-
gests the commander moving stealthily through
underground passages at night looking for
bones and clues with a torch. There are no real
disloyalists in Australia—only a lot of people who
don’t take the present military fervor so seriously
as others. But no great military movement is
complete without conspirators, and people who
are trying to secretly surrender Five Dock to the
enemy, or to carry incriminating documents on
foot by night from Watson’s Bay to the Boer
camp. These people are necessary “ props,” and
the authorities try to supply them by darkly in-
vestigating the truth of the report that Billjim
was once disrespectful while mentally unclothed
and possessed by seven long beers.
There is a great mountain of mystery lying atop
of the recent alleged outbreak of bubonic plague
in Adelaide. When the combative Ramsay Smith
left the Hospital for the chairmanship of the
Board of Health, the old rumpus seemed decently
buried ; but when he accepted a suspicious case as
a proven one of plague, and put the Hospital in
quarantine, and raised a scare, all that section of
Adelaide that hates the political party which
appointed him and the other section which was
affected by the disturbance of interprovincial
trade sent along to him all the dead rats and cats
and dogs they could nose out. One warehouse
cat, which had been crushed between two cases,
was a special prize exhibit. Amid it all, Opposi-
tion-leader Solomon raged up and down expressing
his conviction that Smith ought to be made to eat
all those cats with Kingston (who appointed
Smith) to assist him. If it was a case of bubonic
plague there is a pretty general belief that the
patient, who at the beginning was simply a very
bad case of dirt, contracted it at the hospital; and
people naturally want to know how on earth it got
there. There are so many people who are remark-
ing that a doctor might have been experimenting
with bacillus that sooner or later an enquiry into
that aspect of the case seems inevitable.

Melbourne is suffused with simple piety, and its
Seawtchbyterian regard for the Sawbath is over-
whelming. The other day it routed out an ancient
statute of Charles the Second — Nell Gwynne’s
friend —to deal with a barber who shaved a man
on Sunday. Said statute, dated 1676, enacts that
any person over the age of 14 years doing any
work or labor on the Lord’s Day, the same not
being a work of necessity or charity, etc., etc.,
shall pay a fine, or be. placed in the stocks, or be
whipped at the cart-tail, or have his ears abbrevi-
ated, or something of that sort. The barber was
fined ss. for shaving on the blessed day. Some
day some ill-regulated person may get annoyed
a.nd put this law in motion thoroughly, and then
Melbourne will be treated to the spectacle of ‘half-
a-dozen tramway directors in the stocks arid a
score of law-breaking shipowners and a Minister
of Railways being scruffed away to durance vile.

The little Austrian community in Maoriland has
a paper of its own—the Danica— and it is largely
devoted to complaints about the underhand dodges
and devices adopted by the Maorilarid Boers to
prevent the Austrian Outlanders getting the
franchise. One item from a recent leading article
in the Danica :
They say that every alien is entitled to become a British
subject after 12 months’ residence in the colony; but,
when the applications are made, the Government desire
that every person applying for naturalisation shall be
known to a Magistrate or a J.P. for not less than two
years. We may say here that there are thousands upon
thousands of Britishers and aliens who are not known to
a Magistrate or a J.P. at all, yet they are for years in the
colony. But who are known to a Magistrate or a J.P. ? All
those low class people and thieves, who often come
before the Court to receive their sentence and
punishment. Then these people are entitled to
become British subjects because they are so well
known to a Magistrate or a J.P., and honest persons can-
not become British subjects because they are not known
to a J.P. But this rule applies only to the Austrians and
Chinese. The New Zealand cry is—Why do the Austrians
not become permanent settlers, and then they will have
the same privileges as any other Britisher ? That is very
easy to say; but when the Austrians become settlers, then
they receive worse treatment than Chinese. Here is one
example of how the Austrians are treated. On March
last some Austrians took up Crown land at Herekino, and
about two months ago they applied for letters of
naturalisation, but received no attention up to date.
When those people spent their money by working on their
farm to improve the land, they went to the gumfield to
make a few shillings to keep themselves and their farm.
They were digging for a few days, when the ranger came
over and summoned them to the Court for trespassing on
Kauri Gum Reserves, and they were ordered to pay about
£4 costs because they were not British subjects.
We are all Boers and the Boer is much the same
everywhere. The trouble is that it is so easy to
seethe mote in the other fellow’s Boer, and t<>
overlook the beam in our own Boer.
On the Altar of Mammon.
rfalt of the Barth ! sing the song of wretchedness,
sing it strong and bitterly, with its shameful
chorus.
Drain again the hateful cup, ’tis your wine of
Italy. Drain the dregs of bitterness that the
devils pour us.
When before the Judgment Throne, whose shall
be the shame of it, for those who stagger
heavenward, seamed face and weary feet ?
On the human devils, who shall fix the blame of
it for those who were the rich man’s salt,
whose labor was his meat.
By your wretched cradles stood a devil beckoning.
Naught was there to hold to, save your parents
steeped in crime.
If Heaven’s God’s a just one, ye shall pay no
reckoning; ye shall not be called on for the
way ye spent your time.
Though our tongues are foul, ’tis the foulness of
our keeping. Comrades of mine, we reap not
what we sow.
Devils sow the crops for us, we must do the reap-
ing. Every year more bitter shall the bitter
harvest grow.
Foul is our work and foul shall our pleasure be—
scanty though our pleasure, we must catch it
where we can;
Drunkenness and thieving, lying and adultery—
these are this world’s pleasures for the honest
working-man.
“ Thou shalt not steal.” Satan in his malice never
cracked a joke like this unto a soul accurst.
Give unto a thirsty man a brimming crystal chalice,
and bid him, as he loves his soul, not to quench
his thirst.
Every year more bitter is the wretched harvest
growing. No longer can the reaper keep reap-
ing and be dumb.
Every year increases the debt of hate that’s owing,
every day brings nearer the dawn that soon
must come.
Ye who made the laws that foster wealth and nurse'
it, ye shall rue the day should the reaper’s
hand grow red;
Ye shall rue the day, and most bitterly shall curse
it when the hate'of all the ages shall fall upon
your head.
Cast your bread upon the waters. And surely have
ye done it. Is it Glory’s vain desiring—is it
brutal ignorance ?
The web of Crime is round us and ye are they who
spun it. Ye might write a name immortal,
yet ye fling away the chance.
Ye have given to the missions and churches have
ye builded with the money ye have stinted to
the reaper that ye starvest,
And ye thought the heart was golden when ’twas
only thinly gilded, and ye said ye sowed for
God, yet the devil reaps the harvest.
Oh! ye wretched hypocrites ! see ye not the faces
of our sons and daughters steeped in ignorance
and want ?
With such a field before ye, must ye seek in
foreign places to gratify your vanity and j ustif y
your cant ?
Ye have gone unto the heathen, as your brothers
ye have hailed them; are not our children
worthy of your succor and relief ?
They are hungry and ye starve them, unlettered
and ve gaol them, and we shall hold ye guilty
for the harlot and the thief.
Ye evangelized the heathen—as an offering ye have
made him, that He might bear ye witness of
your deeds upon the earth.
On the altar of your conscience ye smugly knelt
and laid him, and all the imps of Satan
laughed in shrill sarcastic mirth.
For ye made another offering on Mammon’s cursed
altar, ye knelt unto a false God, yea, humbly
knelt and prayed him,
And the weeping of our children was your false
God’s psalm and psalter, and our wretched-
ness and woe the offering that ye made him.
C.P.Q.C.
Ballad of the Cornstalk.
The Bulletin
He hung up the stock-whip and laid by the
It may be for months and it may be for years;
It may be for ever—but restless was he:
“ I’m tired of the bush and I’m off for a spree !.
“ I’m going to the war, and I don’t know what it’s
But the other chaps are going with the Bush
Contingent men,
But if I should stay behind, there’ll be trouble in
the wind,
For my girl would throw me over when they come
back agen.”
He rode to the homestead to bid her good-bye,
And pride for her boy lit the tear in her eye.
They kissed at the slip-rails again and again—
And long may she wait by the slip-rails in vam.
“For I’m going to the war,” &c.
He mounted his waler and rode to the sea
(He was sick of the bush and he longed for a
spree),
He looked’up the “member” and called for his
aid
To go to the war in the Bushman’s Brigade.
“ For I’m going to the war,” &c.
To spy out the Boer lines he rode with a force
And he in the gloaming lay shot from his horse,
A gum-tree transplanted that grew on the veldt—
And that’s where they laid him, for that’s how
they felt.
“For I’m going to the war,” &c.
She waits by the slip-rails at sunset again,
But long may she wait by the slip-rails in vam !
She’ll fret till her heart breaks, but such things
shall be
While Cornstalks are restless and go for a spree.

But the other chaps are going with the Bush
Contingent men,
And if I should stay behind, there’ll be trouble m
the wind,
For my girl would throw me over when they come
back agen.”
Sydney, Jan., 1900.

MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Theatre Royal “ The New Barmaid.”

Alexandra “ Robbery Under Arms.”
Something alleged to be “Rip Van
Winkle ” took place at Sydney Opera House last
Saturday, when that tram-haunted edifice did one
more awakening from its usual silence. It was a
very attenuated “Rip Van Winkle ” —in fact, it
gave the impression of an opera that had just
struggled through a wasting illness, or undergone
some secret sorrow that had reduced its dimensions
considerably. The fact that somebody tried to fill
up the hollows with ditties about the Brier war
made the audience feel just as the opera looked as
if it felt. The scenery was calm and unobtrusive,
and the company had the appearance occasionally
of wanting to hide behind it, especially when one
of its number raced after a particularly high note
and won by a neck—or didn’t. Howard Vernon,
who owns a big, useful kind of voice, makes up
all right as the somewhat gigantic scapegrace whom
the nice girl always prefers, and Alice Howard
Vernon is just that sort of nice girl who prefers the
scapegrace, and after he is dead tells everybody
how good he was, and that he brought
home the money regularly, and was a strict tee-
totaller. It is always a melancholy reflection that
that nice girl has to be killed off before Act 111.
in order that the leading lady may reappear as the
daughter. For the rest, R. E. Watson might be
commended as the Schoolmaster if he could
leave out that ditty about sending the Boers
to a warm, place, and the small child with
the big voice is approved of for her share
in the item, “ These little heads now
golden” (neither of the heads in question are
golden). The co. is a small one, and the half-
dozen or so Blue Dutch ghosts have a remarkable
resemblance to the similar number of variously-
colored gentlemen who compose the population of
Sleepy Hollow. Several people wno are them-
selves in the first act are other people later on,
but as their names are always printed twice over
in the programme, even when they are only them-
selves twenty years later, the list makes a very
good show indeed. On Feb. 10 the co. intends to
produce, for the first time in N.S. Wales, some-
thing in the operatic fairy line, written by Brunton
Stephens and composed by G. B. Allen.
** * *
“Tess” still holds the stage at Sydney
Royal, and the men still anathematise the conduct
of Angel Clare in throwing off his newly-wedded
bride. Every man is indignant when the other
fellow resents his married wife’s unmarried past;
meanwhile he goes on getting quite annoyed, just
as usual, when anything of that sort happens
beneath his own roof-tree. It still remains as
great a mystery as ever how Tess could prefer the
good young man who loves her in fits, and starts to
no particular purpose, to the bad young man who
loves her in bigger fits and starts to the extent of
providing for her and her family. The fact that
Tess sticks to the nominal husband rather than
the real is another proof of the conservative sex’s
inexplicable reverence for the mere marriage cere-
mony. As a French writer puts it, “ Man endures
marriage for the sake of the woman; woman
endures man for the sake of the marriage.” The
average man like Alec. D’Urberville is inclined to
think the man who pays the bill is the most
married of the two ; not the one who talks about
dreams and sunsets and then goes away. This
sordid way of looking at it shocks the pure soul
of his inamorata, and she promptly knifes him—
after letting him pay the bill.
*# # #
At Her Majesty’s (Sydney), Her Majesty’s
nice, pink-cheeked troops continue to make evolu-
tions round the Boers’ stronghold till the blast
from Mr. Wolf warns them to hurry up and come
to business. The Panto, by this time has
sunk deep into the public mind, and the jokes
about the athletic butter, and the milk that re-
quired a lifebelt to keep it from drowning, are still
older friends than they were a few weeks ago.
The school scene, like the grasshopper, has become
a burden. It is too much like the school that
Tommy and Jennie play at, wherein the fun
depends mainly on the use of the switch on little
Johnny. The joke concerning the strawberry
mark on the right shoulder falls rather flat, too, in
these days when even the slavey has discontinued
the missing-heir style of yarn for Marie Corelli
and the holy Sheldon. Carrie Moore’s “ Children
of the Empire ” effusion enjoys but a moderate
success, because Carrie’s voice is more suited to
soft, gentle things, like “Somewhere in the world
there’s a girl that is waiting for you.” This
always strikes a solemn chord—with the married
men especially, as they have the added informa-
tion that the girl in question is waiting behind the
front door, with something heavy for them, if
they stay out to see a man regarding more than
three dogs.
** & *
The play that begins with a coffee stall
and ends with Rule Britannia is drawing to a
well-deserved close at the Sydney Lyceum. Up
to date the funny parts are as sad as usual, and
the tragic items are as full of sparkling humor.
The last scene is especially refreshing because
therein nobody makes any remarks at all. It
passes in dead silence with much paint and glare
and magnificence. Seeing that in the average
melodrama the plot is always the same, and merely
the spectacular portion is different, and that
isn’t always different, and its difference isn’t
always noticeable even when it differs, it seems a
pity that the actors should trouble to say any-
thing either in the last scene or any other
scene. A time may be confidently expected
when the heroine will be nearly thrown off
the bridge or the monument, or the hero
saved at the last minute from the blown-
up ship, without any dialogue at all, be-
cause the audience has heard the whole dia-
logue in a hundred other plays before. Seeing
that the one-time silent pantomime has expanded
into a melodrama, why shouldn’t the melodrama
turn into a pantomime ? Meanwhile, “It is Never
Too Late to Mend ” follows “The Power and the
Glory,” and the convict chipping at the old stone
block will give the coster and the coffee-stall a rest.
