At Sydney Criterion the Caddam Woods
revels would be somewhat improved if Mr. Sayles
didn’t exclaim “All-ryght!” every time the stage-
manager announces a singer’s name. He has been
saying “ All-ryght! ” for several months now.
“The Absent-minded Beggar” is off, and his
place is taken by “The Cunning Sort of Bushy,”
sung by Miss Noni Rickards. “ The Cunning
Sort of Bushy ” seems to have got away with a lot
of the Absent-minded one’s fittings and furniture.
The audience has given up heaving copper
coins at the singer’s eye, and now drops silver
into the tambourines of Fanny Powers and
the Smith Sisters, who come round each
seat like church-collectors to rake it in.
Ludwig Amann, facial artist and impersonator, is
the newest attraction. On Saturday, he announced
that he was about to impersonate Napoleon,
and dived behind his cabinet, and backed out
again with a grey overcoat. Out of the depth of
his cabinet he could be seen seizing a wig, a hat,
and other things. Then he turned round, and
presented a very respectable Napoleon to the audi-
ence. He also did Wellington, Bismarck, Roberts,
Kitchener, the Prince of Wales, Beaconsfield,
Gladstone, Cecil Rhodes, Chamberlain, Kruger,
and Victoria Regina. They were all done
reasonably well, and they were all well received
except Oom Paul; when he arose, in bell topper,
chin fringe and pipe, the audience cursed him.
Mademoiselle Cel inski gives a concert at
Sydney Centenary Hall this (Thursday) evening.
** * *
“The New Barmaid,” which struck Aus
tralia for the first time at Melbourne Royal, last
Saturday, revives all that is saddest in this
country’s recollections of previous musical comedy.
The opening scene is an Anglo-American club, or
pub., where tame young men warble ballads and
talk inferior dialogue concerning the high times
in store for them, and the waiter turns out to be
own brother to a thin comedian, as in “The
French Maid.” Also, the waiter repeats the
ancient business (identified with “ Old Guard ”) of
teaching the new barmaid how to beguile any
poor Adolphus who may call for a drink, and the
sentimental lover (Louis Luscombe) brings his
historic nose of magnitude to bear upon the
heroine’s affections, as though he were a passable
substitute for the late Charles Ryley. Further-
more, Miss Gracie Whiteford invades the club
in the time - worn character of a feminine
good fellow, who is the life and soul of
everything. And the old barmaid, having been
one time bigamously enamoured of the gigantic
Nose, plots against the new barmaid, but the plot
breaks down in the second act, owing partly to
the waiter, who is now an African millionaire in a
plum-colored suit, whereas his brother, the former
capitalist, has become reduced to the necessity of
attending to gents’ horders. Finally, the new
barmaid is found to be a long-lost heiress, or some-
thing, which enables her to marry the baritone
with the colossal features on his face, and then all
the people who have either contributed songs
themselves or helped to make a wild stage uproar
at the conclusion of somebody el e’s “ turn ” are
required to join in the chorus of Gracie White-
ford’s last number on the programme.
* m * *
“The New Barmaid ” betrayed no symp-
toms of enduring success last Saturday. It will
improve, no doubt, as it gets used to itself and is
knocked into shape by the stage-manager, but it
hasn’t much material which would stand a good
hard knock. The “ book ” bristles with local
allusions of a painful nature, and if there be any
points about the leading characters, the points
were blunted on the first night. One of the very
few bright incidents is the reunion of brothers J.
F. Sheridan and Fred Sinclair, when the plum-
colored millionaire gives his thin and fallen rela-
tive a £2OOO-note to buy a shave with. For the
most part, the show seems to go slow when it is
left in the hands of two or three principals ; al o,
when all hands are gathered on tne scene, the fes-
tivity is rarely exciting. A marionette burlesque
by Sinclair and Miss Jessie Williams, especially
the lady, can be singled out for praise, as the daily
papers say. Johnny Sheridan is only remark-
able for appearing as an Irishman instead of an
Irish woman ; nice girls Miss Maud Denny and
Miss Mavis are not cast to advantage as the two
barmaids, and even if they were more effective in
themselves they would all the same fade into
insignificance whenever the nose of their baritone
lover comes to meet them. Mr. Luscombe’s
profile, although not without honor and dignity in
private life, is peculiarly out of place in stage hero
parts. Viewed in conjunction with a flaxen wig
and an amateur-deportment it amounts to an
awful joke.
** * *
The vociferous section of Melbourne pub-
lic which believes in the Alexandra when Dam-
pier is there must have taken the big house by
storm on Saturday night. The gallery was so full
that small boys appeared to be sticking to the
ceiling like flies. Every seat on the ground floor
had its clammy human freight, and the circles re-
served for the plutocrats and the eighteenpenny
middle-classes were in a state of S.R.O. All for
Dampier, who has come back to his old pitch look-
ing uncommonly like the gallant Alfred of 20
years younger. There are sundry sound reasons
why the Alexandra crowd has a warm heart for
Dampier, but probably the first reason is the
actor’s voice. It bitas the house’s hearing as no
other voice can, and keeps the yarn together
when certain other members of the co. are
almost too inaudible for words. “ Robbery Under
Arms” lias gone through half-a-dozen revivals,
more or less, at its dingy birthplace. This revival
is not the best on record, save as regards Dampier’s
rejuvenation, but the narrative contains the usual
amount of Australian horse, and coach, and local
color, and the same old lavish display of innocuous
firearms. Besides, it presents bushmen in conflict
with policemen, who are tantamount to Boers in
an Alexandra way of speaking. Therefore “ Rob-
bery Under Arms ” strikes a topical note of
attachment to the dear Muvverland, and will run
till further notice.
#* * #
The company at Rickarcls’s is the same as
last week, plus the Newsky Family of five braw-
limbed ladies, two men, and a boy. In their
strictly Russian song and dance the Newskys
are an interesting eight. They make music that
has a charm of novelty for us, and their strong,
substantial animation comes as a cool breeze from
the north. Also, their peasant costumes suggest
that the working girl leads a gaudy holiday life in
Moscow. Unfortunately the visitors from Russia
knock a large comer off their foreign interest by
warbling “ Soldiers of the Queen,” which is about
the most un-Russian hymn they could be guilty of.
** * *
“Aletes” on Maud MacCarthy and on
matters generally:—
Accepting Compton’s dictum (13/l/’00), I find Maud
MacCarthy’s playing was artistically satisfying, thus con-
firming a previous suspicion. For Sykes never material-
ised to me except when other performers were abroad.
So our old friend, the personal equation, crops up as
usual. Curious, however, how many people mistake for
coldness what is truly simplicity and refinement. I pre-
sume the mental epidermis is to blame generally, being
insensitive to the pure soft glow of incandescence, per-
meable, perhaps, by nothing colder than a log-fire.
Young as she is, Maud possesses many of the qualities of
the matured artist —dignity, self-control, reserve—chilly
things to the irresponsive. Things, too, wherein
most of our local debutantes are lacking, to
our regretful hurt. If she has a fault, it is
perhaps,j>ver-reverence for the trodden path, distrust of
her own individuality—mark the contrast with Ham-
bourg. Courage will come with years. Her comparative
failure to arouse enthusiasm is intelligible on quite other
grounds. A concert audience, in the mass, values the
merit of a performance rather by the material handled,
than by the way it is handled —which is wise if not just.
The MacCarthy gave wine to palates better accustomed
to tea. Such palates belong to the many.
Apropos of concerts, what a coarse, at times even
brutal, method we have of showing approval. If a fine
poem or picture moves us, we are not called upon to ges-
ticulate with hands and feet, like wooden marionettes,
to certify to the throb that is within. Is music
on a lower plane, or is it, simply and solely,
the sheer force of habit, centuries old, that
necessitates this muscular movement? Personally,
an artistic exposition moves me to silence, the tense
silence of imaginative thought. As often as not, how-
ever, I behave like any other unseemly idiot—which, after
all, 1 fancy is quite rational. The man who writes the
poem, or paints the picture, or weaves the melody, isn’t
there, as a rule. The other man is—actor, orator,
musician—he who interprets what the first man merely
created. He gets all the applause. Personal presence is
everything. The man who is away doesn’t count, which
proves the chain of reasoning is herewith dropped—
the motive power of applause is mostly vanity.
Poor old “ Will Honeycombe,” known all
over Australia for over a quarter of a century’s
quaint and curious moralisings in his little paper,
the Dubbo Dispatch, and out of it, lies stricken
with paralysis, in Dubbo. Real name Morgan.
The Queensland contingenters had a very
busy time in Melbourne, but the most exciting
incident of their stay was not reported. On the
night of the concert at the Exhibition a Carlton
“ lad ” missed his bit of skirt, and found her being
mashed on a seat in the grounds. The masher
was a soldier boy, but the “lad” forgot his
loyalty, and promptly battered his rival over the
eye, and a vigorous action was opened on the spot.
The civilian was a very busy man in a mill, and
was making very favorable progress when the fair-
minded crowd went to the contingenter’s assist-
ance and 14 of them knocked the civilian out. The
unfortunate sat up amongst his ruins, and com-
mented: “ Well, if this ain’t stiff, an’ here’s me
bin singin’ Gor save the bloomin’ Queen orl the
evenin’.”
“ Old Soldier ” tvrites :
Dear Bulletin.— You ask for evidence (8., Feb.
2, p. 18) re the payment by recently-departed N.S.W.
Mounted Rifles of men to shoot and ride for them. Well,
I can produce as evidence one of such substitutes, who is
willing to swear that he averaged 30s. per day in shooting
alone for said warriors during the whole time they were
encamped at Randwick. Better evidence still : Can, if
you come out, show you the same going on amongst the
bushmen. Since writing last, have seenil2o odd alleged
bushmen, who failed in their first shooting test, put
through a second one, 29 passing. One man, who has
passed all tests himself, shot for four of these, getting
them all through. Any of your readers who doubt this
can go out any day the tests are on and see for them-
selves. The press pointed out that it was going on
amongst the Mounted Foot at the time, but, of course,
the press doesn’t mention that the Bushmen are doing it
also—it wouldn’t be patriotic. Also, the riding test is a
farce. The fact of a man being able to hang on
over a 3-foot jump doesn’t prove him a horseman.
File men at a trot past a real horseman all
day, and he’ll pick out every man that can’t ride without
making a mistake. As the test stands now, if a man can
hang on over the jump without getting all over a nag
from his tail to his toe, he passes, whilst a real horseman
may strike a horse that nothing will make jump—and be
rejected. This scribe is watching the case of one volun-
teer with interest. He had shot as a cadet when a
youngster, and never ridden in his life. Still, he scraped
into the last carefully-selected Australian Horse squadron,
and was only spotted and heaved out at the last moment.
He’s now advertised as a Bushman who has passed all
tests. ,
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The cabled report that N.S.W. has got down to
the lamentable expedient of borrowing £1,000,000 in
London on Treasury bills for two years at .the very steep
rate of 4 per cent, is a ghastly shock. This brings
N.S.W.’s credit down with a great fall from its former 3
per cent, level. The fact that the Treasury bills
are being offered at £9O 10s. is. another shock.
This is one more example of the current impres-
sion among Australian Governments that the Aus-
tralian investor is an inferior beast and deserves no con-
sideration. The N.S.W. capitalist is offered 3 per cent,
stock at par, and he is buying it up very steadily. But
when the Cow-Shaking Government has 4 per cent,
securities to sell it offers them in London—3 per cent,
is good enough for the local man, but the gorgeous 4 per
cent, is reserved for the Britisher. Australia would joy-
fully have put up £1,000,009 at 4 per cent, and paid in-
come tax on the interest (the British investor pays no
N.S.W. income tax) and saved the stamp duty payable to
the British Government, and the cost of remitting the
interest to London—but the idea of offering At in the
golden opportunity never occurred to anybody. He is
only a low, common Australian.

Taken all round, this January has been, in a financial
sense, the best month Australia has had for a very long
time.
And yet, with all this glory, there are draw-
backs. The N.S.W. revenue for the seven months of the
financial year that have expired is £209,684 larger than
for the corresponding period of last year, but the expen-
diture has increased by £179,741, so things are not very
much better. And the Cow-Shaking Government has
still to face its big war-bills. At present, with more than
half the financial year gone, expenditure is already
£274,589 more than revenue, which is a very heavy deficit
to be wiped off. And the loan expenditure for January
was £374,435 (£180,462 more than for January, 1899) so
the policy of piling more and more ordinary expenditure
on to loans in order that the ordinary revenue may look
better able, on paper, to stand the rush of military
expenditure is beginning to tell. For the seven months,
loan expenditure has been £1,328,282 —or £180,468 more
than for the corresponding period of last year. At this
rate the year’s loan expenditure will be something like
£2,300,000, which will beat George Reid’s worst record
completely out of sight.
Nothing is known about the expenditure in
Victoria or Tasmania. In Queensland, revenue is now
£442,498 ahead of outgoings. Also, at latest advices, loan
expenditure was going on at the glorious rate of £1,100,000
ayear. And, in YVestralia, Bigjohn still keepsatight hand
on the outgoings so far as ordinary revenue is concerned,
and the deficit is now reduced to somewhere in the vicinity
of £55,000.
* * * •
Attention was drawn to a most amazing state of
things at the recent meeting of the Trustees, Executors
and Agency Co. (Melbourne). Trustees (including trustee
companies) are forbidden to invest trust funds in the
State-guaranteed bonds of the Victorian Credit Foncier
(State Land Bank) unless “where no other form of
investment is specified.” In other words, they are
debarred, except in a very few instances, from investing
trust funds in an absolutely safe Government security.
No wonder the bonds of the Vic. State Land Bank are
difficult of sale, when obstacles are thus deliberately put
in the way of investment.

The Commercial Bank of Australia held another
half-yearly meeting last week, and again the wonderful
board dodged giving any information of the least value
about how the wonderful Assets Trust is getting on. The
chairman said, however, that, “ with regard to the old
assets taken over by an Assets Co., the latter had, up to
the present time, met all its obligations out of its own
resources, and had a considerable credit balance in the
bank.” This looks well, but it means less than nothing.
The Assets Co. bolds more than £4,090,000 of the bank’s
assets, and is responsible for more than £3,400,000 of the
bank’s liabilities to depositors. It has paid, up to now,
about £300,000 in interest on these deposits and
has repaid about £200,000 of the principal (with
the assistance of £183,000 generously donated by
the bank). These are the obligations met up to date
so the chairman’s assurance tfet- the Trust has met all its

Mr. J. A. Wallace, the pioneer of the gold-
dredging industry in Australia, holds a long stretch of the
Sandy Creek near Yackandandah and intends installing a
very large electrical plant to operate several dredges. An
order has just been placed with the Austral Otis Engineer-
ing Co., of South Melbourne, to supply high-class com-
pound condensing engines with Corliss valve gear of 400
h.p., together with boilers and fittings>to operate the first
section of the plant. A duplicate plant to this will he
installed later and will work three electric dredges on a
Stretch of about two miles of the creek. Each dredge
will have a capacity of about 150 cubic yards per hour.*

At nearly all the half-yearly Westralian mining
meetings held in London at the end of Dec. a complaint
of the costliness of management was raised. The enor-
mous directors’ fees and the cost of palatial offices, and
the ruinous British gorgeousness, certainly call for un-
limited cursing. One instance : A small Kalgoorlie show,
'Chaffers Extended ; scrip only a nominal price of about
Is. 6d., costs shareholders £5OOO a year in office manage-
ment. A good deal of distrust of John Forrest and his
“ body servants ” was expressed at some of those meet-
ings. “ Will he further tax the goldfields, and will he
force on a conflict that may accentuate the cry for Separ-
ation in Westralia ? ” were questions asked. At one meet-
ing Forrest’s meat monopoly was compared wi th Kruger’s
■dynamite monopoly, rather to Kruger’s advantage.
A party of four working miners out-back from
Mackay have got a good thing. Whilst prospecting, they
found an old copper-working, and they fossicked round
and got ore averaging 30 per cent. Enquiries for owner
showed that he was drowned 20 years ago, and next of
kin are unknown. The curator of intestate estates won’t
move unless pushed, so the party is meanwhile getting
■out ore that realises £l4 a ton at the nearest seaport.
Average earnings, about £lO a week each, and a man who
digs out copper ore in a Queensland summer deserves it
all.

“ The chairman said it was out of order to discuss the

which this meeting had nothing before it to judge on.
He would, however, say, in answer to Mr. Rouse, that

ceived an additional amount for an asset beyond what
would have been the value three years ago. The assump-
tion was that the assets stood at a valuation, and that the
shareholders should be told how each asset went above or
under. Mr. Rouse had before now been told that never
from the first had a valuation been made of the assets.
They stood on the books at amounts which had no rela-
lation to the amount they were sold at. The directors
declined absolutely to value the assets. It was a question
for the shareholders to decide whether they trusted the
directors. (Hear, hear.) He wished at once to say this,
so as to bring these discussions to a conclusion. The
directors would not comply, and further discussion was
perfectly aimless. He would not allow Mr. Rouse to
further discuss the affairs of the Assets Company. (Hear,
hear.) He could discuss the bank’s balance-sheet if he
liked.
“ Mr. Rouse : * What opportunity have we of discussing
the Assets Company if not now ? ”
“ The Chairman : ‘ I decline to permit you to discuss
the Assets Company at all i ”
The situation can, therefore, be put thus: —
The Assets Trust is about as large as the Commercial
Bank of Australia proper. The bank’s liabilities to the
public are about £4,200,000, and its investments are about
£5,400,000. The Trust’s liabilities to the public are
£3,400,000, and its investments are supposed to be about
£4,200,000.
‘The Assets Trust is, to all intents and purposes, as much
a part of the Bank as the Bank itself. The Bank gets the
surplus, if any. The Bank shareholders are responsible
for every penny of the deficit, if there is any.
The Assets Trust is the only dangerous part of the
Bank. It contains most of the' bad old assets left over
from the boom period—the assets whose unrealisableness
caused the trouble of ’93. It is far more essential to know
that the Trust is getting on all right than that the Bank
is getting on all right.
No statement is apparently ever published to show
whether the Trust’s properties yield enough revenue to pay
the interest on its deposits, or more than enough, or half
enough, or anything else. If, by any chance, the Trust’s
assets were yielding only £20,000 a year, then it might be
fair to assume that they wouldn’t sell just now for any-
thing like a million —in which case there would be a
deficit, on present values, of over £3,000,000, and every
Eenny of the Commercial Bank’s paid-up capital would
e lost unless prices improved. On the other hand, there
is no evidence to show that the Trust isn’t earning
£lOO/100 a year, in which case it would probably be quite
solvent. Only if the Trust is doing well the flat refusal
of information is all the more remarkable.
And the directors of the Commercial Bank flatly refuse
to give any information about a matter which involves
the question whether the Bank’s paid-up capital is intact
or whether it is partly or wholly lost. When they are
asked how much income the Trust’s properties yield they
invariably reply that they refuse to put any valuation on
the principal —which is just as relevant as if they said
they declined to form an opinion as to the whereabouts of
Andrde’s balloon.
They don’t state whether the assets sold up to now
realised the value set against them, or a tenth part of it,
or anything between. Some of them, it seems, realised
much more than they would have done three years ago, but
whether they realised anything like the value set against
them in the Trust’s books isn’t stated.
They refuse to give any reason for this reticence. They
merely say that they decline to furnish the shareholders
with any information worth calling such about £4,000,000
worth of their own property.
And they indignantly inquire if the shareholders have
not confidence in them.
* * # •»
The balance-sheet of the Australian Joint Stock
Bank for the half-year to 31st December, 1899, is some-
what better than the two which immediately preceded it.
It is difficult to say much more than that. The six
months profits are £2912, which compare as follows with
some previous returns :
Profit. Dividend.
June, 1893 .. £12,221 Nil.
December, 1893 .. 51,040 5 per cent. = £22,951
June, 1894 .. 32.083 5 24,869
December, 1894 .. 21,915 4 „ 21,530
June, 1895 .. 24,932 4 22,675
December, 1895 .. 25,825 4 „ 23,354
June, 1896 .. 20,629 Nil.
Loss.
December, 1896 .. £48,200 Nil.
June, 1897 .. 53,507 Nil.
Profit.
December, 1897 .. £1,725 Nil.
June, 1898 .. 3,746 Nil.
December, 1898 .. 266 Nil.
June, 1899 .. 1,358 Nil.
December, 1899 .. 2,912 Nil.
The.profit is at the rate of about 9s. 6d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders funds (capital £1,168,042
and alleged reserve fund, £52,000). Why the capital
remains at this old nominal figure seeing that it is prac-
tically certain, on the evidence of these profits, that the
great bulk of it is lost, and why the pretence of a reserve
fund is kept up when it is practically certain that the fund
never had any real existence, being swallowed up all along
by depreciation, it is difficult to understand, but the
A.J.S. has a tendency to do things this way. On the evi-
dence of the profits, and assuming that a sound bank
should be able to earn at least 2£ per cent, on its real capi-
tal (and it should be able to do more than that) the A.J.S.
needs to write off £1,000,000 to make its bala; o .'-sheet a
true statement of its position. But it never faces the
difficulty, never admits the extent of its losses, never
attempts to vigorously clean up the old mess and start
afresh.
More than three-fourths of the A.J.S. Bank’s
assets consists of the item “Bills discounted, advances,
and all other debts due to the bank at old valuation,
£6,637,986.” And “ old valuation ”is a beautifully elastic
term. Julius Caesar’s 1.0. U. and the paper-money of .Jeff
Davis’s Government were all worth a lot of money “ at old
valuation,” and so were shares in the Federal Bank. A
balance-sheet which doesn’t even profess to give the real
value of the assets is a quaint and curious joke, but the
law and the public tolerate such things in Australia. The
balance-sheet says nothing, and has said nothing for a long
while, about the old reserve-fund, which was set aside to
meet losses. Presumably it has been all absorbed in meet-
ing losses, though there is plenty of evidence that it hasn’t
met nearly all of them. On one point, however, the re-
port is more explicit than it used to be. The institution
has made £88,908 up to date by the repurchase of “ B ”
deposits at a discount, and this is set aside towards
covering losses. During the half-year total deposits have
increased nearly £200,000, despite a considerable reduction
in reconstructed deposits ; and cash and liquid assets
(£1,556,824) have increased by more than half-a-million.
Also there is a large increase in note circulation. What
alteration there is is an alteration for the better. Still it
would be interesting to know when the directors expect
to put their very dilapidated institution straight at the
present rate of progress. In 24 years the bank has
accumulated £10,007 of earnings and made £88,908 by the
repurchase of its deposits at a discount—this towards
writing off losses which certainly appear to be not less
than a million. It will probably earn larger profits in the
future, and it will probably make less by the repurchase of
its deposits, for as its credit gets better its deposits won’t
be obtainable so cheaply. If the institution means to keep
its nominal capital intact, and to write off losses out of
earnings, the process looks like the work of many years.
The A.J.S. Bank seems to love its old inflated valuations.
It has £150,915 available without touching its capital
(alleged reserve-fund, £52,000 ; reserve from re-purchase
of deposits, £88,908 ; and profit balance, £10,007) with
which to write down some at least of the old inflated
values, but it keeps all these paper-reserves standing on
one side of the account, and the inflated assets on the
other, and leaves things as they were.
* * * •
North Coast Steam Navigation Co. (N.S.W.)
reports a profit of £5600 for the half-year ended 31st De-
cember, 1899, as compared with £5286 for previous half,
and pays its 8 per cent, per annum (part of it being called
dividend and part bonus), which absorbs £5250. The
other £350 goes to the reserve for equalisation of divi-
dends. The co. has £131,250 paid-up capital, £16,181 in-
surance reserve, and £9588 reserve for equalisation of
dividends. The one really solemn fact about the concern
is a mortgage of £40,000 on its property, which it is seem-
ingly making no great effort to remove. At least the
mortgage has been there so long that it looks like a very
old friend, and so long as the policy of paying away so
much of the profits in dividends is adhered to, the old
friend will probably remain on deck. A reduction of the
dividend and bonus to 5 per cent., and a steady effort to
remove this weary Dweller on the Wharf, would be much
better for the co. in the long run. The co.’s balance-
sheets, by the way, are a trifle incomprehensible in places.
For insta’nce, at 30th June, ’99, the equalisation of divi-
dend-reserve was £8269. Then £247 was added to it, and
in next balance-sheet it appears at £9238. In the absence
of any revenue and expenditure account, how it got there
is a matter for cheerful and interesting speculation,
though the fact that it has got there is gratifj’ing.
* • * *
The Riverina Co-operative Life Assurance Trust
of Albury (N.S.W.) is an interesting and very promising
gamble in the Tontine business, concerning which Tub
Bulcktin already stated that the only visible draw-
back was the want of a Trustee Co. to hold the money
and be responsible for its repayment to the beneficiaries.
This drawback has now been removed, it having been
arranged that the National Trustees, Executors, and
Agency Co. of Melbourne will have charge of the funds,
and give its reliable guarantee for their appearance when
the time comes to divide the profits.

The New York and Brooklyn Tobacco Co.
(Sydney) finished the second year of its existence on 31st
December, 1892. Its brief record is as follows :
Profit. Dividend.
1808 (13 months) £2538 .. 10 per cent. .. £1625
1899 (12 „ ) 1685 .. „ .. 1500
The second vear certainly doesn’t look anything like so
well as the first, still the co. has paid off all preliminary
expenses, £ll9, and has given the manager and secretary
£lOO bonus, and has a credit balance of £879. One item
among the assets, “ Goodwill and Brands, £2000,” is the
often inevitable but always objectionable paper asset
attached to so many enterprises, which requires to be
written off as speedily as possible to put the concern on a
really good basis. If next year’s profits don’t show the
same kind of slump which last year’s did, the co. may
have a good prospect before it. But the slump certainly
looks pretty awful already, for an enterprise only two
years in existence. *
Newcastle Wallsend Coal Co. (Newcastle,
N.S.W.) shows a profit of £7128 for the half-year ended
31st December, 1899, and a dividend at the rate of 15 per
cent, per annum absorbs £7500. Consequently, the
balance at credit of profit and loss is reduced from £1760
to £l3BB. The co. has £lOO,OOO paid-up capital, and
various reserves amounting to more than £50,000. It
reckons, probably with sufficient reason, that the dimi-
nished profits this half-year (though they are still over
14 per cent.) are a mere passing accident, largely attribu-
table to scarcity of tonnage. Anyhow, this co. has done
amazingly well.' It paid 42J per cent, in 1891; for a long
time prior to 1896 it paid 20 per cent, regularly, and since
then the dividend has been 15 per cent.
* * * *
Sydney Harbor Collieries. The London direc-
tors say they are experiencing no trouble in sinking this
great shaft or in dealing with the water ; but they are
having a good deal in raising money, and as the payment
of a large amount of the company’s working capital is
still the subject of litigation, they have under considera-
tion the issue of debentures. It will be a long time before
they raise their first ton of coal, and many things are
likely to happen in the meantime. This is probably the
largest coal-mining enterprise in the world, no expense
being spared in the character of the machinery, so that if
they get coal there is no doubt about their ability to
handle it in a big way.

managements. Seeing how much is said of their mines

By a fluke motion at a scanty half-yearly

Hercules, Tasmania: Been sending consign-
ments of ore to the Tasmanian Smelting Works and else-
where. Now decided to erect its own simple smelting
plants near the mine, leaving its complex ore for future
operations. * * •
Anchor tin mine, Tasmania. This is probably
the lowest grade tin mine working in Australasia. It has
100 head of stamps driven by water power. The forma-
tion lying at some hundred feet above the battery is a
decomposed granite, easily broken. The value of the ore
contents is said to be only 7s. 2d. per ton, and this is mined
and crushed at a profit of nearly one half. The co. is
erecting its own smelting works at the water’s edge at St.
Helens, and is about to spend upwards of £20,000 m
bringing a supplementary water supply so as to ensure
continuous running of the 100-head stamp mill, more than
half of which has been recently hung up for lack of the
necessary supply.
* • * •
Great Mt. Lyell: Name altogether too big for
the mine. Driving several tunnels in chloride schist, and
sinking a shaft in which are occasional signs of copper.
Last report issued in London—“ Formation shows good
improvement, but owing to the epidemic of influenza,
hands have been short, and the work much delayed.
Any mine that takes upon itself such a name deserves
such a fate.
• * * •
Mt. Lyell Blocks. Another London co. This
mine has got a large body of good grade ore in its mam
tunnel, and the reserves have been largely increased by
recent developments. Making arrangements to send ore
to the Mt. Lyell smelters. A very sensible thing to do,
considering the awful cost of doing your own smelting in
such a country.

Mt. Bischoff, Tas. : Grade of ore continues to
fall, but mine still keeping up its div. record. The
resignation of Kaiser as mining manager alarmed a
number of the sleepy island shareholders. The chairman
assures them that there are many years’ work still in
sight, and the mighty Kaiser is still going to act as
consulting engineer. It would be Of some interest to
shareholders, and more to tin-ore sellers, to know the
amount of money realised yearly by this co. in ore pur-
chases. Some say it is upwards of £40,000. Anyhow, it
is time the charges were brought down, or a reduction
enforced by competition. Bischoff has too long had a
monopoly of this well-paying business, and the victims or
its charges have been numerous and long-suffering.
Dear Bulletin.— Your hard-headed old mining’
expert who is evidently at his best when he has not seen
the property he reports on, makes some mistakes, a few
misstatemeilts, and a few plain lies in his paragraph on
Cftulagoe, and I hereby contradict the lot. The railway
will not take three years nor two years nor 18 months to-
complete. The lodes on many of the properties of the
Chillagoe Railway and Mines have been proved to be per-
manent ; notably on the Griffith and Boomerang. And
as to the value of the lodes, the Magazine face-veins, and
intervening country taken on open cut would go well over
12 per cent., nearer 15 in fact. On the P.P.A. crosscuts'
N. and S. from the bottom of No. 2 shaft, show the lode
to be over 90ft. wide, and 80ft. of that is high grade car-
bonate. A bulk sampling of the P.P.A. lode 50ft. from
the surface, and for a width of 45ft. gave 10 per cent,
copper, 12oz. silver and 2dwt. gold. And so with the
Queenslander. And so with lease 38 and the Fortunata.
Your hard-headed mining expert is soft-headed.—Main-
shaft.

“F.” : On what possible or impossible princi-
ple does the London market estimate the paper value of
Westralian mines ? For instance, on 22nd January, Asso-
ciated shares were valued on the market at £6. Even if
the very good yield of 1899 keeps up, and the dividends dm
ditto, this means that buyers may expect to get back
their money in 15 years without interest. Or, consider
Great Boulder Proprietary shares at 365. 6d. If the divi-
dends keep up to last year’s level present buyers may ex-
pect to get back their money without interest in 14 years.
Great Boulder Main Reef, at the same day’s prices,' held
out the golden prospect that, provided there is no falling-
off in dividends from the 1899 level, buyers will get back
their money in about 20 years, also without interest.
Think of the sanguine nature of the individual who pins;
his trust to any mine paying regular and handsome divi-
dends for 14 or 15 or 20 years merely to get back the price
he paid for shares—if he is to get 3 per cent, interest the:
mine needs to boom for several years longer than that !
* * * *
Hewitson’s report on L. V. Consols should be as
interesting as Roberts’ review of Buller and Methuen’s
campaign. Previous management left enough of the rich
chute to serve as an excuse for the vague and hackneyed
verdict—“ Mine as rich as ever.” They’re now whispering
that same chute is likely to be dropped on at the 400 ft.,
and lower level. Of course it may be, but only the pick:
proves possibilities. Whatever they may get at 400 ft.
what they are getting is—well, good road-metal. The-
fiasco might have been foreseen any time the last four
months. The sulphide-plant muddle is the approximate
cause, and it could no more be hidden than the 30,0000 z.
monthly return could be maintained. Kaufmann’s bear
raid had its birth in early knowledge of sulphide trouble,
and having provided his informants with good billets he
lost no time in turning his knowledge to account.
Professions of directorial ignorance of the rig are-
lauo-hed to scorn on the field. Boulder’s rich:
chute could also have turned out 3P,0000z. a month,
but didn’t—for reasons. Though not so rich as-
the Consols chute by a few ounces to the ton, it is longer.
Whispered here that Kalgoorlie mine managers this time
stole a march on manipulating directors. Latter had
ordered a boom at all costs, and insisted upon the
managers helping to make it in any possible way.
Reply, in some cases, was to oversell the mines two or three
times over. Managers knew what picking eyes out meant
and acted accordingly, but it is quite possible that one or
two mines have been oversold to such an extent that some
over-zealous bears may get a frightful squeeze shortly.
Doubtful whether the managers acted together in bear-
ing. Unquestionably some of them beared individually
and have profited largely. So look out for changes soon.
Some of the local brokers got a nasty knock, and
the whereabouts of several are unknown.

ised stone is rich ; recent concentrates averaging as high
as soz. gold per ton.*

SOCIETY
Private W. B. M‘Pherson, with the
Maoriland contingent at Naauwpoort :
The awful tales of their infernal deceit and cunning
make everyone promise them no quarter.
Then he goes on:—
A section (four men) of the 6th Inniskilling Dragoons
Were out on patrol work a few days ago, and saw a few
men on top of a kopje. They started to ride up. The
Boers had in the meantime hidden among the rocks. The
Dragoons got within 20 yards before the Boers fired on
them. One got four bullets through him—two in the
body and one in each arm. He fell, and his comrades
thought he was dead, and rode away at a gallop. The
Boers came down and bandaged his wounds, gave him
water, and carried him to the roadside, where he was
CANINE.

A good many letters from the seat of war are
built that. way. Apparently everyone has heard
from parties unknown of the terrible cruelties of
the Taal-speakers, and almost everyone finds them
in practice a kindly lot of armed Samaritans.
Private M'Pherson’s loud resolve to give no
quarter to the men who gave their own coats and
blankets to their captured enemies at Nikolson’s
Nek. and themselves froze on the open mountain

exceptional opportunities of knowing, says he does
not think there is any man now in Victoria, who
is, correctly speaking, a millionaire. The province
is to be congratulated on the fact.
* * * *
The recent Schnavel tragedy at Bumberry
(N.S.W.) was one of those purposeless horrors
with a streak of madness in it, which is so
characteristic of the silent, loony plains of inland
Australia. Nearly the whole of the families
thereabouts were related to the Schnavels and to
each other. Much inter-marriage, combined with
drought, and monotony, and the habit of leaning
against things and looking at nothing, impart a
certain brooding craziness to the character of the
out-back agriculturist in many of the older settle-
ments, and it breaks out every now and then in
grotesque ways. Schnavel was 19 and his wife 22.
It is alleged that he threatened her life even before
marriage, which was evidently the crazy instinct
breaking out. In Australia, a man kills a woman
for one of two reasons—either because she wouldn’t
marry him or because she did. The peace of the
Schnavel family is alleged to have broken down sud-
denly at last because he wanted a coat and vest
washed, and his wife said the proceeding would
spoil them, but offered to sponge and brush them
instead. After darkly brooding over this wrong,
he appears to. have gone away and borrowed an old
rifle, loaded it with nails and sundries, and re-
turned home looking for vengeance. Then, so the
story goes, he pursued the wretched woman in and
out and round about their two-roomed wooden
humpy till she was almost dead with exhaustion,
and finished up by perforating her with
a gun - load of nails and screws. It
was a purely Australian tragedy—the agri-
cultural land that is met with in long-settled
places where all the families have married and
intermarried over and over again. If a few agri-
cultural Contingenters from old farming districts
bring back with them a Boer wife apiece it will
be a blessed interruption to the dismal process
whereby Smith’s son marries Jones’s daughter and
Jones’s son marries Smith’s daughter, and their
children marry more Joneses and Smiths, and
their grandchildren do the same, till droves of
more or less loony Joneses and Smiths lean and
ponder in a silent fashion against every fence in
the district.

“Australian”: To a t’othersider Maori-
land Supreme Court has the free-and-easy style of
a club smoking-room. The Judge lolls on the
bench, an open tin of cachous by his hand, appar-
ently interested in anything but the case. Groups
°f remittance men hang about round the dock
audibly discussing their own concerns, which are
mostly in a bad way. The Judge’s Associate holds
a levee of his friends under his principal’s nose,
and the usual big cop is wholly absent. A court,
however Supreme, is unfinished and undecorated
without the presence of the broguey one who
wakes up occasionally to shout “Soilence” when
nobody is saying anything.

A Sydney daily paper reports that there
has recently been a great increase in the attend-
ance at Sydney Zoo. Doubtless a lot of the city-
bred men in the Bush Contingent have been going
out there to see what on earth a kangaroo looks

• * » *
Mrs. Fraser, the Melbourne lady who shot
her husband in the head so very long ago that
people can hardly remember now which Biblical
character she fired at, was brought up last week
at St. Kilda police-court. The delay was caused
by the slow recovery of Dr. Fraser. Considering
the ironmongery in his head the fact that he re-
covered at all is not a little remarkable. In the
interval, the pretty, excitable lady with the mass of
beautiful brown hair has been out on bail, harass-
ing the Government with offers to go and be a
spy on the Boers, telling amazing stories to the
astonished young man on the Herald, and, it
was reported, nursing her battered but reconciled
husband. It was understood on all sides that the
reconciliation was something really beautiful, that
Dr. Fraser had forgiven his wife for shooting him,
that she had forgiven him for not keeping his
head out of the way, and that everybody was in a
heavenly frame of mind with a margin of saintly
resignation round it. The police-court proceed-
ings broke up this vision. Apparently, the recon-
ciliation has gone to pieces. Dr. Fraser implored
the court not to bail his wife any more; he said
he was afraid of his life while she was loose. He
also alleged that she called at the hospital where
he was recovering, and shouted at him, or shouted
generally around the premises, till he lost the
sight of one eye through the relapse that followed.
It was also set forth that, when he refused
to live with her again, she threatened to
offer up their child as Abraham offered up Isaac
or something like it —or rather as Abraham didn’t
do it but was going to. The doctor stated in
evidence that his wife was “the most hysterical
woman he ever struck,” at which point her lawyer
chipped in sharply, “ Then you have struck her?”
The doctor said he had—in self-defence ; but the
lawyer’s way of putting it left him in the attitude
of a man who always struck a hysterical woman
when he met her, even if they hadn’t been intro-
duced. In the end a loudly-sobbing defendant
vas removed without bail.
In British communities
only does the woman-cyclist
enjoy a free hand (or, rather,
foot). In Russia she is not
allowed to wheel at all with-
out an Imperial permit.
In France hubby’s written
permission is necessary before
she can join a cycling club,
and in Germany she has to
submit to a preliminary ex-
amination before the local
policeman before she is
allowed to run over the casual
passer-by. This explains why
the female wheeler is decreas-
ing in the British Empire,
and increasing elsewhere.
The best way to encourage
Woman is to discourage her.” l

• * * *
The much-execrated Greek gypsies are
still roaming Victoria devouring the public’s poul-
try and falling back on dog on slack days. But
of late they have taken to a new industry. After
camping on the outskirts of Fitzroy for quite a
long time, the tribe was just about moving on
when a debt-collector made the chief a flattering
offer. Very stylish families, since the days of
the land bubble, have learned a trick of being
“not at home” when the collector calls, so the
gypsy chief was coached up in the rudiments of
debt-collecting, and followed by three of his male
compeers, several females, a number of multi-
tinted infants—all in tatters—and eight dogs, he
approached a large house with the little bill.
“ Not at home ” and a ’aughty stare had no effect
on the Romany, who proudly camped in the road
and waylaid everyone who passed in and out of
the house, demanding the bill promiscuously.
The visiting clergyman and a doctor were among
those to whom he and the three friends and the
women and tattered children and the eight dogs
clamored for payment. In two hours and twenty
minutes the gypsy had the money, which the col-
lector, who was on the watch, recovered from him
with some little difficulty. All hope of recovering
a bad debt is apparently not lost while the
Romany remains. Time is of no value to him,
neither is dignity—as the house-dweller under-
stands it—and he will sit like an exiled king at
the front gate for three days with his tribe around
him, trying to collect Is. 6d. with a patience com-
pared to which Job’s was a nervous wreck.
ANSWERING HIS OWN QUESTION.
INDIGNANT FATER: “ Did you ever see anything
like that hoy of mine? ”
For a fortnight or more Adelaide papers
every day printed a column or two of names and
descriptions of persons who had volunteered for
the Bushmen’s Contingent. Nearly all of them
were “good horsemen and experienced bushmen.”
bisters who never suspected their city-bred
brothers of these accomplishments woke to find
their families famous. But the thing was easily
explained. Adelaide has two distinguishing
features —its wooded park lands, quarter of a mile
wide, surrounding the city; and its awful horse-
trams. Many of the 1 ‘ good horsemen ” were
merely persons who habitually rode in the horse-
tram, and got accustomed to horses through con-
stantly seeing these animals through the front
window, and they knew the bush by reason of fre-
quent wanderings with their best girls in the
wooded park. Even the proposed leader of the S.
A. Bush Contingent has for 12 out of his 35 years
been a city clerk.
AT PARTING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The joys of town that others sing,
I know not, neither care to find.
For me, fresh air, a happy mind,
A book or two—
Tried friends and true—
A maid towards me well-inclined—
For simple joys like these I pine,
And thou for comfort, pipe of mine.
A brief farewell to work in town—
Old town, where all my years are spent,
To part awhile I’m well content:
Soon—very soon—far from thy strife
And turmoil and thy strenuous life.
Long days of deep content I’ll spend
And murmur only when they end.
Sydney.
L. R. MACLEOD.
FROM BARMAIDS’ LIPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From barmaids’ lips how swiftly flies,
Between our “nips,”
The silvery laughter and the lie 3
From barmaids’ lips !
Ah 1 when I drink to Zo’s bright eyes,
In luscious sips,
I court the laughter and the lies—
From barmaids’ lips.
For, as she makes her quaint replies
I know in Life’s dark well she dips,
Whence come the laughter and the lies
From barmaids’ lips 1

BRISBANE, QUEENSLAND.
Only once in his political life did George
Turner try to be sentimental and artistic, and he
made a bad failure of it. At Federal referen-
dum time he departed into the realms of
poetic frill, and promised that every elector
who voted on that great occasion would
receive a soul-inspiring certificate to
keep the founding of the new nation
green in his memory, so that it
would blossom gloriously like some-
thing or other, and be a what-you-
may-call-it; and every elector
was exhorted to have high and
noble thoughts when he
looked at the memento, and
not to let it fall into the
jam, and so on. When
a plain man is laboring
at dragging poetry up-
hill he is a sad spec-
tacle, but George Tur-
ner tried his best to
be sentimental about
that card. And he
was as sentimental as
a tax-collector or a
gas-meter man. Six
months have passed
since then, and the
certificates which were
to gush forth as a spon-
taneous offering aren’t
ready yet Hot, cross
officials are still arguing
about the design, and
everybody concerned is
sick of the subject. No-
body cares whether he re-
ceives the thing or not —if
it is ever delivered, the baby
will ge.t it to play with and
will promptly make him-
self sick by licking off the
coloring. The expense
is considerable. Most
of the designs are
tawdry and yellow.
It was originally
proihised that the
heads of the six Pre-
miers would be on it,
and now there is a row over
the inclusion or non-inclu-
sion of the anti-Federal For-
rest. Altogether it has be-
come a small but persistent
worry, and if Premier M'Lean
would drop it all in the ash-heap
the country would feel glad, oo
would the postman, who will be
a beast of burden for weeks
delivering the wretched thing if
it ever comes off.

It seems that the salaries
of gaol-warders in Victoria
were retrenched after the
Boom, and have not yet
been restored to their
original figures. Probably
the worst effects of that
sinful retrenchment have
fallen upon the prison-
ers. Warders should
be fat, well-paid men
of musical tendencies.
- Think of the felonious
hearts that might be
•softened by a warder
singing “My Pretty
Jane ” or “ The Old Turn-
key ” in the prison corridor—to his own ac-
companiment. Many a prisoner who hadn’t
wept for years, would probably cry through the
keyhole—“ Leave off singing, and we’ll be good.”

A George-street (Sydney) tattooer, who
displays in his shop window weird pictures of the
emblems he brands into the human skin, tattooed
the upper arms of a pair of lovers lately with a
vow of fidelity running into ten words. The
legend covered the whole of the upper arm. Then
the male subject sailed for Boerland with the
“ WHEN THE EMPIRE CALLS."
second contingent, and should he fall into the
hands of the untutored enemy he will probably be
taken for a private dispatch and put to a great
deal of trouble by their efforts to decipher him.

A man writes this par :
A remarkable femirfine peculiarity at Bronte Beach
(Sydney) where the mixed bathers gather and gambol in
the summer sunlight, is the soiled feet of the grown girls,
mature spinsters and matrons.
The smaller feminine fry reveal
white and clean extremities.
There is scarcely an exception
to this rule. A long dress
appears to be a guarantee of
dusty, dark feet, and he who
takes an interest in unstock-
inged nymphs (a brace of bare
legs have an irresistible fasci-
nation for almost every man,
bald-headed or otherwise) is
impressed thereby. Even the
regular feminine bather at
Bronte has feet no whiter than
those of the casual miss or
madam who paddles by the
sea. The explanation is pro-
bably the shoe and the skirt.
The skirt gathers up the
dust and shovels it casually
into the shoes, and the shoes
carry it along. 1 have an
impression that the sandalled
feet and bare ankles of the olden
Greek girl couldn’t have been fair
to look upon after she had been out
for a few hours wiping up Athens or Thebes with her
trailing draperies, not could the tail of her garment have
been a wholesome object.

Adelaide Health Board, in view of plague
possibilities, solemnly warned citizens that when-
ever they came across a dead rat the animal should
be brought under the notice of the nearest police-
man and the corpse conveyed to the Health
Department. A certain humor attached to the
idea of calling the attention of such a very large
object to such a very small one, and the citizens
entered into the spirit of the thing. It became
quite common in Adelaide to see a procession of
three (one policeman, one private citizen, a
one rat) lighting out for the Health office,
the policeman looking very angry an
the rat very decomposed. The board present y
had to draw attention to the fact that
only fresh rats were wanted —that there was no
need to dig up the rodent that died three months
before the plague was heard of, and deposit it in
the street, and then call on the police to have 1
removed and fumigated. Such conduct, said the
Board, was excess of zeal. Also, the calling °t the
attention of the force to stuffed, embalmed, salted
or kippered rats, also artificial rats made of wood,
leather, iron or india-rubber was uncalled-tor
levity. And now a new kind of boiled rat with
onion in it has been invented which makes the
force even madder than it was before.

One night recently a man called round at
the Sydney residence of Judge Docker and rang t e
bell man ordinary sort of way. lhe door was
opened by Miss Docker. The shadowy figure
outside spoke in a thick voice and enquired ten-
derly if the Judge was at home (the Judge has a
lot of friends who think tenderly of him, including
John Norton). The young lady replied that the
esteemed one was not at home, whereupon the
voice made remarks to the effect that the dis-
penser of justice had done him a lot of harm at one
time and another, and fired a revolver, with no
results worth mentioning. This is the first occa-
sion within the memory of the oldest inhabitant
of Sydney town when a man has fired a re ™ver
at or otherwise tried to slay a woman without
being in love with her, or suffering from blighted
affection. It is alleged that the accused had been
drinking “rum and beer mixed’ —which would
account for many things.

“ S.S.” : The absence of the usual
gambling flutter in connection with the Melb.
A.N.A. festival had a marked effect on the
attendance this year. The absence of the push
was especially noticeable. Hitherto the lad and
what he describes as his bit o’ skirt have been a
conspicuous element at the Natives shivoo, but
for want of the art union and the usual chance ot
winning a wooden cottage in the suburbs they
stayed away and the gathering lost two-thirds or
its attractiveness in their absence. Ine clannish-
ness of the push used to be an interesting study at
these fetes.. It always monopolised the most out-
of-the-way corner of the big building, and danced
dolefully throughout the day and night, refreshing
casually with weak, teetotal squashes and mild
confectionery. The “peb.” paid his shilling,
behaved decently, and catered for his own sad
amusement, but evidently the gamble was the
initial draw, and without it he would as soon trot
his accordeon and his girl “ darn the bye. Ihe
A.N.A., if it has sense, will bid for the attentions
of the push next year with a decent prize for the
best pair of dancers in a larrikin hop, judges to be
drawn from the ranks of the M.O. s at push
“assemblies.” The larrikin takes his joys miser-
ably, but still they are joys to him, and if they are
blighted he will go and be happily miserable
somewhere else.
* * * *
The agricultural shows are spreading along
the N.S.W. South-coast again, and it is very
painful to hear about the same old bull, the same
big water-melon, the same lady-rider winning
every time. Why, oh why ! doesn’t some other
champion melon, or bull, or female-rider come
along, and give us a rest! Show-goers admit it
would be far better to have one big display, when
the suffering public would be called upon to view
that same bottle of home-made jam and the same
bull once a year. All the same, it will not be, for
every little tin-pot place wants its show—such as
it is—chiefly in order that business people, pubs.,
and the like may participate in the annual grab.
Writer has seen ’em all so far; and, with the ex-
ception of ah occasional extra bull or showman
with a monkey, there is not a bit of difference
same old thing a whole lot of times. ;

In certain of "the American States any
lawless persons who have a few private scores
to pay off, and are afraid to do it on their
individual own, form a vigilance committee.
Vigilance committees have become notorious
by reason of certain atrocities such as burning
negroes alive without trial, etc. lh ie y have
a vigilance committee in Moonta, S.A;, and
the other day it started out from the local post
office with an effigy in a trap, a model band, and
THE WRONG ORDER.
MRS. HARDFACE: “ Bad luck to yer, this isn’t My
leg — it’s Mrs. Murphy’s, next door.”
some mounted caricatures of British soldiers, and
paraded through the town. Five or six thousand
people (according to the local paper) joined the
procession, and the whole hideous display marched
to the residence of a man who, it is said, isn t
quite sure about the justice of the present war.
There a gallows was erected and patriotic speeches
given by a member of Parliament and the president
of the Miners’ Association. Other temporary
lunatics sang songs and gave recitations, including
the inevitable “A. M. Beggar,” and then the man
who didn’t quite believe in the war was con-
demned to death and hung. After the effigy had
hung sufficiently, a local parson (so the newspaper
alleges) disgraced his cloth by reading the burial
service over it before it was burnt. If the state-
ment is true it would seem that the said reverend
is a person with a taste for coarse blasphemy
which would do credit to a larrikin push ; and if
it isn’t true the preacher should have a good libel
action against the paper which thus represented
him as playing the cheerful ox in public along
with the local rowdies.

“Banjo” Paterson, writing from the
British camp at Arundel, mentions casually that
one of the regulars found “ asleep on watch ”
side the camp was sentenced to, two years hard
labor, but “ was very nearly being shot.” War. is
a wakeful business. The men go to divine service
on Sundays with a loaded rifle m one hand and a
Bible in the other, and the parson likens them to
the wise virgins who kept their lamps, trimmed
in readiness for the bridegroom’s coming. The
Boer is understood to be the person they are
waiting to receive.

They say that man is made by God.
No doubt the tale is true enough ;
But I’ve been told—the thought is old—
Old Nick supplied Him with the stuff !

He marched ten thousand men,
To the stony top
Of a Boer-lined kop,
But they soon came down again.
And when they were up they were shof,
And when they were down they were not ;
And whether they knew what they meant to do
Is. a question asked in vain.

There’s an Anglican Church at Fern Tree
(Hobart) called St. Raphael’s, but it celebrates
only one “ Feast ”in the year. This is a “Straw-
berry Feast,” which is celebrated with great pomp
and no devotion every January. St. Raphael's is
easily identified. It has a “ strawberry-mark
all over it. It was built from the sale of straw-
berries, and is painted strawberry-red from the
rooster on the tower to the threshold at the door.
Man’s happiness depends largely upon the
attractiveness and amiability of Woman. A
woman to be very amiable must be well dressed
and know it. To achieve this noble purpose it is
essential that she wear P.D. corsets. It can thus
be logically deduced that the happiness of Man,
or a good deal of it, radiates from the graceful
outlines of the P.D. aforesaid.*
All the principal ironmongers, or, at least, those
that are “ up-to-date,” are now carrying stock of the
Albatross Lamp Wick.” If anyone tells you they are no
better than ordinary wicks, you may be sure he does not
know what he is talking about or is greedy and does not
want to lose the immense profit on the old'wick.*
