A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 6, 1900.
My Deak Myee,—
Sir Francis and Lady Hope—May Yohe, you
know, the music-hall divinity who married Sir
Francis and lost him his dollars in conse-
quence—have been staying in Sydney the last
fortnight without anyone knowing. They
kept their visit extremely dark, going to the
Australia for half-an-hour, and then retiring to
the quieter (and cheaper) Metropole. Every night
they have been seen at the tneatres, but few
recognised them. On Saturday they were out at
the cricket match and again escaped notice,
though many wondered who the smart woman
was in navy voile with an audacious scarlet bow
on the left shoulder.
I saw May Yohe in the street to-day. Not very
tall, but very graceful and snappy-looking, with
an air about her that could not be overlooked. She
has coal-black hair, round, black Japanese-looking
eyes, round cheeks, and a very lively manner.
She was trailing a lovely string-colored lace frock
along, with masses of frills about the feet, and
wore a little turned-up cream hat with a touch of
violet. Sir Francis was with her —a big, fair,
green-looking young Englishman. They report
themselves travelling for their health, and are
going into Queensland next week, but really they
both look as smart, jolly, and blooming as possible.
I wonder Sydney didn’t find them out.
For Sydney doesn’t often miss sensations. It
made one out of the sort of triumphal tour of a
beautiful widow down Pitt - street last week.
People gathered in groups as she came along. As
far as I could gather, the vital point of all the
curiosity lay in finally settling the question—
would she wear “weeds.” A big black hat
dressed with black feathers, a black silk blouse
and black voile skirt lifted the load off Sydney’s
shoulders.
Speaking of sensations. A painful shock to
many peoole last week was the strange suicide of
“Tony West,” a boy of 22, studying law. He
was well known in society; despite his youth he
had for years been everywhere, accompanying his
mother, Mrs. Cartwright-Ashe, to everything,
from an afternoon-call to a race-meeting. In
fact, his devotion to his mother was a byeword in
Sydney. He was in the best of spirits, health,
and finances. His suicide is inexplicable to every-
one. He gave a dinner to some friends on Mon-
day and several times during the evening made
the remark that this night would be the last time
they would see him for a long while. Somehow
nobody took any notice at the time. Next day
his death was announced.
Edith Crane is playing nightly now in “ Tess,”
and the house is larger accordingly. Certainly,
she barks her way with much difficulty at times,
a tearing cough following on almost every laugh,
and I am wondering how she will manage
“ Trilby,” where there is so much laughing to be
done. By the way, the accents in “Tess” —
where do they all come from? Julia Merton’s
brogue is brogue; the milkmaids washed-out milk-
tin voices make poor attempts at “baint ’e and-
some.” Hawtrey’s “Sapt” tones are always un-
accommodating. It is a blessed relief to find
Tess, Angel, and Alec leaving dialect alone, even
though they air two American voices and a
London one.
By the way, Tyrone Power, an extremely clever
actor, and stage-manager at the Royal, lately
expressed himself flabbergasted at the quick way in
which an Australian girl gets hold of a part. A
young actress was given the leading lady’s heavy
lines m the morning, and went on without a slip
at night, and Mr. Power remarked that very few
American women could have done that. Are Aus-
tralian girls going to get a reputation for fastness
at last ?
Such discussions over the morals of Tess go on
sometimes in the d.c. that it seems there really are
people who go to a play for a lesson. What sort
of people are they ! In a word, people who want
a thing “ put before them.” Real life is too spread
and vasty. The play is the thing ; the play
gathers up a little bunch of moral lessons, and puts
it straight in the hand so that anyone can hold it;
and the artificial bunoh has been brought to much
perfection lately.
And so, nightly, you may glean inestimable bits
from suburban rate-payers. The black-satin lady
thinks the play is very rough on man and rightly.
The gentleman in pumps has his answer : “ But
Tess gets hanged.” The black-satin lady is
silenced. The gentleman in pumps scores until
the black-satin lady hisses an inspiration at him—
“ But Alec is killed, and Angel loses his wife,”
and the gentleman in pumps is knocked over for
the second. Then he jumps up triumphantly.
“Don’t that show the woman doesn’t pay the
piper any more than the man.”
I am beginning to look forward to the time
when nobody will look for anything in a play but
the legitimate qualities of a good play. When you
come to think of it, a play is a very risky thing to
take a moral lesson from. So much depends on so
many things. On the hero’s liver, and the heroine’s
feet, and the villain’s laugh, and the property man’s
comprehension of how much cold-tea will make
the villain drunk (it upsets you when the villain
reels after half-a-glass), aud the window that is
open below your right ear in the d.c., and the
woman in front of you with her hair-net pinned
over her back-hair crooked —all these have so
much to do with the way a play hits you that
there seems too much lottery about the drama as a
thing to catch ethics in.
The Rev. George Walters had a few words to
say about Tess last Sunday, and he said them with
vigor, flinging a clerical curse or two at that
“loathsome, one-quarter man, three-quarter cad,
Angel Clare.” The reverend one finished up by
requesting all good women to shun the matrimonial
advances of men whose characters are not up to
the standard set for themselves. He gave as his
reason that “the bad man had lost his delicacy of
thought, and could not respect a good woman.”
Wherein he erred. It takes a bad man to appre-
ciate a good woman. The good man sees nothing
in his feminine counterpart. Having never
known darkness, he cannot realise light.
New music continues to be loudly patriotic.
This is a great time for the man with a tune in his
head. He has only to set it to some words about
us being children of the empire and he is quite
sure of being able to give it away at any rate,
which isn’t always the case with one’s own com-
positions, mind you.
I have before me a new “Absent-Minded
Beggar ” song, the music by Mr. Albert Kaye,
who enjoys the reputation of being the youngest
organist in- Brisbane. His music is catchy when
it gets hold of you, and the words are the same
old words, although I don’t know why
that second verse is printed ; nobody begs by it.
Then the “Loyal Hearts Waltz,” by M. g!
Crichton (Paling and Co.), a tuneful valse of the
soft-pedal order; and “The Australian Contin-
gent Waltz,” by Gertrude O’Connor Fitz-Patrick,
only a little less deficient in originality than
its title. This is dedicated to John See, whose
portrait with a button-hole adorns a comer;
but not even the brightest button-hole would’
induce me to dedicate a waltz to a member
of Parliament. One might as well dedicate
an anthem to a butterfly, or a polka to a church-
warden. The last of this bunch is “The Heroes
of the Empire,” by Alfred Somers and Ludwig
Schliebner (Paling and Co.) The words include
everything incidental to heroes, and the music is
several pegs above some of the music that calls
itself patriotic.
By the way, some small children got taken very
bad lately with the desire to be patriots. In fact,
they went round from house to house about Bondi,
and begged for the Cause with little silvery voices.
It was quite idyllic until someone saw the babes
going into lollie-shops, indulging in riotous
pennorths of stickjaw at the expense of the
nation. Evidently begging is catching, some-
times.
Even the good old “ Rip Van Winkle,” which
rose at the Opera House on Saturday, was not
sacred from the common onslaught. Two patriotic
songs crept in, one very Tivolian in manner, the
other distributed free to the house with an advt.
about somebody’s boots looming large on the cover.
Good old Howard Vernon played Rip in fair
curls and a miraculously happy expression, con-
sidering how cruelly Miss Alice Howard Vernon
was casting flat notes under his nose.
Miss Maud Hewson’s fair pleasant face and
sweet singing voice were Vernon’s chief support.
But the trail of amateur theatricals was strong in
most parts of the production. Particularly on the
girls in trousers, who struggled with a desire to run
away and the even more fatal failing of stooping
over to hide their legs from a callous house. A
Miss Lilian de Vere, in officer’s regimentals and
long boots, made a nice-looking youth. But the
Rhine fay, in white skirts and large-size white
woollen bootees, didn’t account for Howard
Vernon’s awestruck admiration, and the Catskill
fome was too much of a small boy hopping. But
expect the performance improved mightily
afterwards, as it is still running.
News of Florence Schmidt at last. I hear on
good authority that she has got an engagement at
La Scala. The Steffa-nis are coming back to Sydney
soon. Up till quite lately Florence Continued
to be Steffani’s pupil, but 1 heard whispers some
time ago that she was breaking or had broken from
her old master, who wanted to bring her out as a
pure Australian product. So lam afraid that the
time hasn’t quite come vet when the Australian
hall-mark is good for anything but sheep.
A rumor of great grumbling comes from the
Bushmen’s Contingent It seems that the major-
ity of the Bushies arrived in Sydney with only a
few shillings in their pockets, expecting to be put
on pay as soon as they had passed their tests.
Some hitch somewhere. So far they have been
unable to draw any money, and many of them
haven’t the price of a tram ticket about them to-
day. The Bushmen’s Fund, in the hands of Mr.
Randal Carey, has not been administered ; the
military pay-master is unable to shell out. The
only thing left to some of the Bushies to draw on
is language, which, unfortunately, doesn’t often fail
a man in an emergency.
There is much indignation down at Dawes Point
just now, and much disgust is felt that such a nice
good man as So-and-So should actually be haled
off to the Quarantine. “Him have the plague.
Why, he’s a gentleman, Mr. So-and-So is, a
thorough gentleman,” as if the plague were a
species of ill-breeding of which no nice man would
ever be guilty. Still all people that intend to
take the plague, or to be suspected of taking it,
had better hurry up, as now are the good days for
the suspected ones. Heard that a lady-help who
inadvertently put her nose into an infected house
and was thereupon dragged off to durance vile,
claimed, besides board and lodging, £1 a week as
compensation for loss of wages. The authority
evidently did not know much about the position
of the average substitute for Mary Jane, for he
paid the amount without a qualm, by which the
L.H. makes 13s. Gd. profit weekly. A little while
and the State may have to close its purse-strings
somewhat tighter.
“ The stage will be arranged as an Australian
bush camp, m which will appear Australia’s star,
Celinski!” Bashful Celinski, advertising her
concert for the Bushman’s Fund that comes off at
Centenary Hall, this Thursday night.
A tragedy! The Evening Snooze lately
drowsed out a full account of a wedding ceremony
which afterwards turned out to be the base inven-
tion of an enemy. The Snooze proffered polite
apologies when the mistake was pointed out. But
a week later the same paper had another apology to
make. The base informer had described howcharm-
ingly Mr. Blank had sung “Remember Me No
More,” and Mr. Blank was unaccountably angry
over the nice things that were said about him.
So the Snooze made public apologies to Mr.
Blank, and announced to an expectant world that
he had never sung “ Remember Me No More ” at
a wedding that never existed. “Remember Me
No More” as the wedding song, points to the hand
of a disappointed rival in that dark, uncomely
jest. Ask Sherlock Holmes.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
, “ Lady Brassey spent two days at Frankston
with the Maddens the week before departing
hence. The ladylike Hindlip had his last junket
at Mr. and Mrs. Tom Fairbairn’s Western home-
stead, where they said only “ Au-revoir,” and not
“Good-baye,” having followed the great Dropper-
off and his party to England last week.
“ That Bulletin story of Lady Brassey and the
Jack Tars on horseback is perfectly true, except
that the heroine was the tint Lady Brassey, and
the sailors, members of the Sunbeam crew. First
time Lord Brassey visited Rockhampton in his
yacht, he convoyed his then liege lady and a
detachment of blue jackets by train to Springsure,
whence they took horse to an opal mine on Rain-
worth station. On the road, her ladyship first
laughed immoderately, then lost her temper
at the sight of her winded escort, its baggy pants
hitching higher and higher, and ever so much
brawny leg bared to the breeze. She called a
halt, and ordered Jack to pull up his socks and
Mat to haul down his pants; but two minutes
after she had passed the word ‘ Trot! ’ the spec-
tacle was just as absurd as ever. In the end, she
made every man Jack of them tear his big,
starched white handkerchief in two, and with
each half bind a floppy ‘ pant ’ to his ankle.
“Thanks to Brassey thrift and Beauchamp
piety, smart Southerners of to-day break the
monotony of otherwise undiluted worldliness, by
splashes of devout dissipation. Owners of nice
grounds gather their friends together for open-air
Sabbath gallivantings, and after dinner or
substantial high-tea lias been disposed of, the
assembly turns its eyes up and its mouth-corners
down and its thoughts inward, and sings hymns
till supper-time. Uncle Tom, a past-master at
psalms and spiritual songs, got much glee out of
this phase of half-and-half recreation, and a while
ago, when Beauchamp the Beautiful was here-
abouts, he, too, took kindly to the blend. ‘ Your
Sundays are delightful ! ’ said he to a local belle,
famous for her snowy throat, sanctified on that
particular occasion by a small black cross, sus-
pended from a narrow necklet.
“A municipal record of the Brassey munifi-
cence. When Smaleome M'Eacharn was sworn-
in as Mayor, the councillors, according to custom,
took the magnate in tow to Government House.
After the ceremony. His Ex. hesitated, glanced in-
quiringly at the City Fathers, then ponderously
drawled, ‘ Ah, gentlemen ; perhaps—aw—you
would—a w—like—to —come —and—see—aw—my
pictures ? ’ And that’s all they did see. The hot
and thirsty council feasted its eyes on Uncle’s in-
ferior art-gallery.
“The story goes that a brush occurred at a
recent military dinner given in Srnellbourne’s most
swaggah boarding-house. After the wine and
chestnuts had passed round, somebody swung up
to a popular officer (against whom ho bore a
grudge) and insulted him. The professional fire-
eater told him summarily and simply to ‘shut up.’
Then the civilian pelted more verbal mud at the
warrior off duty, until his face became the color of
the flushed summer tomato. ‘The uniform ties
my hands, and you know it,’ burst forth
his Nibs, ‘ but, by Heaven ! if you say another
word I’ll smash your skull in.’ The other
word icas said, and the next minute the fury with
the north-wind head was wiping the floor with the
bloated aristocrat. Then Authority stepped in
and hostilities were suspended. Next day the
officer stood to arms before Authority. After
wading through a heap of evidence, the Depart-
ment ruled that the civilian got his desserts, and
that the floor probably needed wiping in any case.
“ The resourcefulness of the Melbourne feminine
mind was severely tested in its efforts to evade the
rules and regulations which forbade it clinging to
the departing troops. Still, the troops wanted to
be clung to and gave all the assistance in their
power. Two pretty society women got on the
pier by working a luncheon on the adjacent mail-
boat. Then they turned up at the gangway of the
Euryalus just as the contingent was going aboard.
‘Good-bye,’ said the prettier of the two—and she
is very pretty—lifting dew-laden brown eyes to
one tall warrior. ‘Good-bye. and—’ her mouth was
suddenly stopped. One of the rank and file, a man
of prompt action, stepped briskly forward, kissed
her, and as briskly stepped on. ‘The impudent
wretch ! ’ exclaimed her friend—who hadn’t been
kissed—indignantly. ‘ If he had offered me such
an insult I’d—’ ‘No, you wouldn’t,’ sobbed
Brown Eyes, her sweet little nose buried in her
handkerchief. ‘ And he’s not an impudent wretch
either! I’d let them all kiss me, every one of
them, if they wanted- to, the dear, fat Colonel
included.’
“In Adelaide, the other week, there were 60
nurses volunteering for six opportunities to get
sent out to South Africa. And the task of choos-
ing one nurse from every ten was left to two doc-
tore. What joy for that little selection com-
mittee !
, night last was a damp one for the
M.C.O. concert-couples who went forth into
the outer darkness to nestle on the grass. Love
laughs at chills, but there were a few impression-
able darlings who cried over the damp effects the
next morning. The M.C.C. moonlight concert is
the favorite rendezvous of a smart set who start
off with the ostensible purpose of listening to a
fiery programme of martial airs and war-songs,
and rush ‘to arms’ in the darkness as if to
Britain’s mighty call at the very first blast of the
trombone. And it only costs sixpence for ad-
mission.
“ Mr. Frank Lyons, who lately married a
Sydney belle, is a great favorite in Bananaland.
Nowadays he practises as a lawyer in Burketown,
away up North, on the rim of Nebuchadnezzar’s
furnace, but in Brisbane and Central Queensland
Ins popularity dates back to the days when he was
a big blonde schoolboy, bubbling over with in-
herited Irish fun. His uncle, ‘ old Bob Lyons,’
of Rockhampton, also a solicitor, is remembered
as a genial paterfamilias, whose one-horse buggy
in the olden times never looked big enough forTts
load of large, laughing, blue-eyed boys and girls.
Frank Lyons, the bridegroom of to-day, lost his
father early in life, and his only sister went into
a convent.
Talking of convents, ’tis said that even in this
democratic country impecunious Catholic young
ladies have been known to take the veil with the
approval of their kinsfolk, chiefly to avoid com-
promising their clan and lowering themselves by
infra di/j. scrambles for the necessary crust. One
hears that the crust-struggle is especially severe
for daughters of the ‘ True Faith,’ because in Aus-
tralia comparatively few country families of their
own creed are in a position to employ a governess,
which narrows one important avenue of employ-
ment right away. Then, to make matters worse,
educated Irish parents, as a rule, bring up a
squeamish, shielded type of girl not built for busi-
ness racket. So if a refined R.C. spinster is not
rich sho can t do better, even from a mercenary
point of view, than ‘ die to the world.’
Springsure Toby,’ happening by, put his own
construction on the anklets, and, to the day of his
death, gave his version to every new-comer.
‘ My word ; snake bad longa Sunbeam,’ he would
say. ‘ You know brother b'longa Lady Brassey ?
One, two, tix million snake been run up that
pfella leg! ’
“Poor Victor Jones, of Rockhampton, shot
through the heart in South Africa, was the first
Australian contingenter to fall in the prasent
war. The lamented Bananalander in question,
whether bom in Rockhampton or not, has lived
there, I’m told, nearly all his life. His Rock-
hampton kinsfolk have always been enthusiastic
Wesleyans, capable choir-singers and untiring
Sunday-school teachers. Victor’s eldest sister
married a sea-captain and left the sultry, inland
calm of Rockhampton ‘ to sail the seas for many a
day.’
“Melbourne had a painful experience with a
poisoned picnic on A.Is.A. day. A Burnley Sun-
day-school went up the river for its annual bun
outbreak, and having consumed many stale sand-
wiches the whole picnic suddenly felt a strange in-
ward premonition, and then fell unanimously and
fiendishly ill. There was no doctor adjacent, and
the distracted few who retained control over them-
selves were invested with responsibility that turned
the superintendent’s whiskers grey in a single hour.
At length the distressed army was drafted into a
special, and then a whole train-load of invalids
was carried home. Ptomaines bred in sandwiches
a day old were the cause of all this woe. Nobody
died, but fully half a Sunday-school came very
near to it-
\ours affectionately,

your stomach. Not sick enough to take to bed, or
to call a doctor, but sick enough to not know what to do.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Queensland ex-Premier Dawson —it is
al m°st a pity to tie a tag on a man for so small
and brief an offence—is seriously unwell, and
thinks of entering a Sydney hospital.
The Rev. F. W. Wray (the Wray of light),
special rain-maker with the second Vic. con-
tingent, gets £ll a week for his services. How
often the men of peace are men of gold pieces !
<4 S.”: Have frequently remarked David
Age Syme’s strong resemblance to Carlyle. In a
late Idler is a caricature of the Sage of Chelsea,
landing upon which nine out of every ten Melb.
readers exclaim : “By Jove! David Syme.”
Trooper Cox, who died in Adelaide hos-
pital on his way to the Boer war, was a son of
jr‘ wo ’ M.L.C. Cox senior held a seat in the
first N.S.W. Assembly under responsible Govern-
ment, and has been a member of the Leg. Council
for nearly forty years.
Bailiff Withers, of Sydney District Court,
now starting on a holiday, was one of the 100
soldiers drafted from Melbourne to the Maori war
in 1860. He took part in the storming of Pake-
takmno. Of the 100 men only five escaped being
killed or wounded during their five years’ service.
Tasmania's Governor, Gormanston, has
been attacked by partial paralysis, stated to be
caused by over exertion in climbing a steamer’s
side during a recent fishing excursion. Gor-
manston gets into trouble going up, and Brassey
gets into trouble coming down, and the world is
full of trouble.
General Clery, now in South Africa, seems
a remarkable type of soldier, judging by the
character sketches in an Australian daily. It is
alleged he goes into action with his moustache
waxed, has a crease ironed down the front of his
pants every morning, and is accompanied on active
service by his French cook.
Henniker Heaton (Australia’s own Hen-
niker) has, it is said, purchased a grave space
adjoining the cathedral, at Canterbury (England),
and devises that he shall be buried there. The
dust of bygone N.S.W. Governor Gipps, and of
Dr. W. G. Broughton, first C.E. Bishop of
Sydney, also reposes thereabouts.
CORPORAL MORANT (“The Breaker”).
Second S.A. Contingent.
For sheer cussedness the fate of William
Bird, of Auckland (M.L.), is bad to beat. Bird
was a cripple of 75. His house was burned on
Sunday, Jan. 21st, and though carried out three
times by his daughter, the obstinate old cripple
went back again each time, trying to rescue a deal
table, until he was burnt to a cinder. Yet the
furniture was insured for £lOO, and if the old man
had rescued the table it could only have benefited
the insurance co. at best.
Carington was the last N.S.W. Governor
who stuck to his post for the full term of his
appointment. Duff died; Jersey and Hampden
fave it up when about halfway through ; and now
leauchamp is said to be tiring visibly in the first
lap. Loftus, Hercules Robinson, and, with the
exception of Belmore, their predecessors back to
early times took the full five years, and some of
them one or two over. Fitzroy played vice-
regality for nine years, and the useful Macquarie
for eleven.
Jimmy Edwards, the veteran Yic. sculler
and leading boat-builder of the province, now
wears a moustache, and is a “dead-ring” for
Cecil Rhodes. Felix Levien, M.L.A. for Barwon,
was previously the Melb. man most like Rhodes,
but Edwards is photographic in his resemblance.
Levien comes of a family of legislators, and
at one time three Leviens were simultaneously
members in Yic., N.S.W., and S.A. Parliaments.
In private life, he is the Onion King of Australia,
and boasts that he has brought tears to more homes
than Cecil Rhodes ever did.
This war in South Africa is beginning to
sift out the bull-pups from the terriers among the
big dogs of the sub-continent. When it is all
over people will know who really are the ,
strong men. So far there appear to be
about three only Kruger, Reitz, and
Rhodes possibly Cronje makes, four.
Schreiner’s reputation was just beginning
to look imposing, but has punctured
badly ; and Hofmeyr of the Bond, locally
known among anti-Dutchers as the
“Mole,” has practically disappeared.
Steyn seems to be a mere tool in the
hands of the astute Reitz —the thin bitter
man, who finds it hard work to live on
account of incipient paralysis. Milner is
not South African, and it is hard to say if his
strength is that of his own backbone or Cham-
berlain’s. According to one set of South Afri-
can papers, Milner said he was going to smash

N.S.W. ex-Judge Forbes, who now,
when past 80, is roused to struggle publicly
in martial verse, was more or less of a bard
during many years of his early life. His
family always esteemed his poetic gems.
Marquis of Queensberry —the man where
the boxing rules came from—threw up his earthly
sponge last week. Australia dimly remembers the
noble Marquis as a devoted attendant on a certain
lady member of its first Gaiety co. and as the
referee in a Melb. glove-fight between Slavin and
Burke. One way and another Queensberry con-
trived to be a highly disrespectable person, but his
somewhat “outcast” condition served a useful
social purpose by making it easy for him to bring
the Oscar Wilde scandal to a head. Queensberry’s
open blackguarding of the fat sensualist, resulting
in an action for libel, followed by Wilde’s down-
fall, was a’fully bad form, doncherknow, neverthe-
less humanity'felt a little better for it. Most of
the acts that do good in this world are a’fully bad
form.
The Saturday Review, in 1898, wrote
this of Redvers Buller :
Sir Redvers Buller has fought in most of our wars from
China to'Canada, and from the Cape to the Soudan, and
has proved himself a good administrator as well as a good
fighter, not a mere martinet, who regards men only as
fighting machines, invented for the purpose of obeying
orders. During the worst of the Land League troubles in
Ireland, Buller was sent over to impart the necessary
amount of military rigour into the civil administration,
and to see that no nonsense about famine and distress was
allowed to interfere with the supreme necessity of collect-
ing rents, but he soon showed that he possessed a heart as
well as a head, and he gravely offended the Irish officers
by telling them that Law and Order would not be built
up in Erin any more than in the land of the Pharaohs, by
injustice and extortion. They soon recalled him, but his
name is remembered in wild Kerry as that of a just and
strong man.
The Duke of Teck was about the most
popular of the petty German princes who have
ever lived on the British taxpayer and made peri-
odical compositions with their creditors. He got
a genial reception when he came to England
because he was a very good-looking husband for
Princess Mary, of Cambridge, and inasmuch as
cousin Mary was understood to have been doomed
to a long spinsterhood by her (alleged) jealous
Gracious Queen, the crowd thought that a hand-
some fellow a few years younger than herself
would be in the nature of reasonable compensation.
Subsequently Teck struck another chord of public
sympathy by running away with a newer girl for
a while, and then, of late years, he had the brokers
in and was compelled to sell the furniture. No
other German princelet in England has been so
practically human as the Duke of York’s late
father-in-law.
Drake’s pitiable decline and fall from being
the leader of some kind of a democratic Opposi-
tion to the high degradation of being a Minister in
the Queensland Boodle Cabinet has made things
awkward for Drake’s weekly paper, Progress.
It now pats the boodle Cabinet on the back ; only
two months ago it was patting it on the head with
a large club. Two months ago Progress said that
the Boer war was wholly unjust, and Queensland’s
share in it could only be excused through the
necessity for the Empire hanging together. That
view had also to be abandoned as part of the price
of Drake’s portfolio, and now Progress talks the
usual dreary slush about the sinfulness of the
Kruger crowd in not granting the franchise to
aliens (a thing which neither Britain nor any other
country dreams of doing) and moans about Boer
“corruption.” Think of a paper whose boss has
just swallowed all his principles and joined the
most corrupt political crowd in Australia, being
shocked at anybody’s corruption.

G. H. Kingswell, lately of Coolgardie,
where his journalistic troubles kept the court busy
for some time, has spent nine months in China,
mainly for the Yangtse Mining Corporation ; and,
like most other representatives of .T. Bull conces-
sionaries, he is not over-well satisfied. They find
the heathen Chinee’s mining regulations peculiar ;
among them is one providing for Chinese mine-
management, and another runs that one-half at
least of the shares in a Chinese mine must be con-
trolled by Chinese. Of course quite disinterested
persons are busy explaining to the Chow that
these “absurd regulations” prevent “the devel-
opment of the country ” —which is just what a
Chinese explained to a Queensland warden once
when the latter ordered him off a new mining field.
A world-weary miner on the West Coast
of Maoriland, having determined to end the night-
mare of life, made a few final arrangements in
order that the parties who discovered his body
should not get a sudden shook and be put to too
much inconvenience- He hung a nicely-printed
placard on the door with the information that he
was dead, and that the readers would discover a
stretcher in the shed, which _ implement he had
made for the purpose of removing nis body to the
nearest place where an inquest could be held.
Then he went in and solved the great problem.
Considerate to others even in death, and such con-
sideration was ever the true hall-mark of a gentle-
man.
The late Parkes was the most vigorous and
determined political opponent of Dailey’s Soudan
Contingent, and he predicted at the time a rapid
revulsion of feeling. Parkes proved a, true
prophet. There is not, however, a, politician
of his courage now in the political life of
Australia. Parkes, too, knew how to talk
Imperialism when the humor suited
the opportunity. But there was about
him a tremendous pugnacity. He
always liked to be a leader, never a
follower. He wanted to deny freedom
of speech to others. For himself he
insisted on a British subject’s right to be
heard in a loud tone of voice, and he didn’t
care whether he trod on the prejudices of an
ant or an empire.
“Melbournian”: Who now reinembers
John Curtain ? Before the Victorian boom
Curtain must have been amid the richest men in
Melb. city, apart from the pastoralists. He spent
about £150,000 (mostly his own money) at Dookie,
in what he calls a chateau, an immense vineyard,
and a distillery, besides the large Gladstone Hotel,
and other town improvements. He came to grief
over a miserable little advance of £20,000 from the
Bank of Victoria, which at a critical moment
refused to finance him further, and all his sails
came down. Curtain was an M.L.A. and
• Melbourne City Councillor. He made his
money first in a little distillery on Yarra
bank, opposite the wharf, and used to have a
share in the Herald, with Winter, Halfey,
and P. J. Martin.

William McGregor Clarke, who made a
voluntary departure from this world by shooting
himself at Footscray, was a man of many
experiences and idiosyncracies. When editing the
Williamstown paper and sitting as member for
Footscray, he wrote a not too complimentary
article about Her Gracious the Queen. This met
the eye of an eccentric but poverty-stricken
ex-military officer in Adelaide. He instantly
started with a Zulu servant, and walked _ from
Adelaide to Melbourne. Unceremoniously
entering W. McG. Clarke’s office, he asked
for the editor, and finding him promptly
belayed him with a green hide. After
Clarke’s defeat for Footscray by big Hancock,
also deceased, he went to Westralia. He was
appointed Registrar at Mount Malcolm, and sub-
sequently, with Dr. Laver, started a newspaper
there. It was not a success. Blindness followed

“ Wall an «oley” :
Re crane-building next to
hawks—hawk probably
waits until the crane
has “settled him down to rest,” and then
builds close by—with the hope of getting eggs or
chicks, during the temporary absence of Mr. and
Mrs. Crane. The crane of this locality, not to be
caught napping, frequently puts off laying opera-
tions until the hawks build —and they then find
rest for their souls in some remote spot.

A “ round-the-continent ” cyclist, writing
from Northern Territory :
Northern Territory J.P. law. A Ohinaman was brought
up before a local J.P. for obscene language (the Ohinaman
at the time owed J.P. 13s. for meat purchases, the great
epitome of the law being also the local butcher). After
hearing the case, the Ohinaman was fined £2. Just then
the Court suddenly remembered his meat-bill, and he rose
and remarked, “ What about that 13 bob you owe me ?
I make the fine £2 13s. and costs, the fine to include my
account for mutton supplied to the prisoner.” The same
J.P. tried a man for horse-stealing. The witnesses re-
fused to give evidence, or said they had none to give.
Whereupon the Court wound-up a long address somewhat
as follows: “There is no evidence at all against the
accused, but the horses have been stolen by someone, so I
will have to fine the accused £5 and £lO costs.” And he
did. 10 p.m. : The curses of the drunks are varied to-night
by the ungodly howls of the blacks, one of whom died
near the pub. to-day. Most of the camp is down with
some epidemic, and the Ohow cook is playing some
fiendish instrument next door to my room. I’ll go and
kill him when I finish this letter. Musical evening last
night, all drunk, interruptions every half-hour or so to
permit of a fight'being finished.
* • * •
Dear Bulletin. —The ’roo’s competency
in looking after himself during the dry season
calls for more praise than the despised animal
mostly receives. Have often seen the ’roo in the
sandy bed of a dry creek digging for water, and he
knows enough to invariably start rooting in the
deepest hollows. He is a great surveyor, is the
’roo, and his instinct generally finds water for him
inside 48 hours. Never knew kangaroo to go
without water longer than this. — Spoof.
“ Snell ” : This is a snake item intended
rather to instruct than amuse. Joe (who tells the
“ true fact ”) and Jack, his mate, occupied narrow
beds on either side of a bark hut on the Piper
(Tasmania). Joe was awakened one night by
what he took to be the voice of some strong man
in his agony. He sat up in his bunk, and listened
with bristling hair and a creeping skin. He
heard it again, a voice that seemed near, but tiny
and strangely muffled, and “You miserable
swine ! ” were the words it said. Joe quaked and
hastily struck a match. Jack was sitting up in
his bunk, too, and glaring like one in a pale-blue
funk. ‘‘Did you hear it, Jack?” stammered Joe.
J ack moistened his lips and nodded several times,
and his face was that of a haunted man. “ There
he goes again!” “Oh, you miserable swine!”
The voice was only a stifled whisper, but it was
distinct enough, and breathed a nameless woe j
moreover, it certainly came from somebody in the
hut. Slowly, fearfully each man lowered his
candle and his head to peer under the bunks.
Jack started back with a yell, seized his gun, and
with the barrel yanked a sluggish snake into the
middle of the floor, and as it landed there came
from it in a feeble and despairing gasp: “ Oh,
you miserable swine ! ” For a minute two fairly-
paralysed men glared in dumb terror at the ser-
Eent. Joe was the first to recover. “ I know ! ”
e yelled, pointing at a great knot in the fore part
of the snake, “he’s swallowed Pete.” Pete was
their talented parrot. For further particulars see
H. J. M‘Cooey, Esq.

“ Licensed Surveyor ” : H. J. M‘Cooey
(8., 20/l/’99) says, “A bright little girl had a bottle-
tick fastened on to her for only about three hours,
and although it was removed, and turpentine and
kerosene applied to the injured part, she died in
great agony the same night, 14 hours after being
bitten.” What on earth is H.J.M.’s “bottle-
tick ” ? The ordinary bush-tick here, as also the
dreaded “ Queensland tick,” which is not yet quite
here (Tweed River), after fastening to an animal,
human or other, developes to what we call a bottle-
tick (at any rate the female does), but not in three
hours, nor yet three days. It then falls off
mature, and lays its eggs on the grass-blades, etc.
Personally have often watched a common tick,
say on the jaw of one of the milkers, after trying
in a tired way once or twice to remove it in oppo-
sition to the cow’s evident desire to “ let it rip,”
and came to conclusion size of tick gave fair indi-
cation of
length of time it
had been adhering. Little
pigs are peculiarly liable to fatal
results from tick-bite. Believe I cured
one by administering bi-carbonate of
soda—reckoned “ the tiling ” here. Also,
as to dying in great agony. Tick-bite
appears to act by general dulling of the
perceptions, paralysis of hind-quarters— not agony
or violent spasms. May be wrong. My own
child, two years old, was very dull and sick twice
from tick-bite in the head. Have had them on
myself, the large ones in September and October,
in scores; the smallest species, almost invisible,
what the blacks call “cudgera,” in hundreds.
Would like to hear further about ticks.

“ C.C.” sends some slanders on the bush-
teacher-off-the-chain:—
Dear Bulletin. —Funnier even than those letters re-
ceived by bush teachers which you print occasionally is it
to listen to the bush pedagogue off duty. During recent
holiday coach-trip we picked up in succession several
teachers going for the holidays, and the last we took on
board was a leary youth who looked like a “flash shearer”
in his best black suit, with a scarlet-and-green silk hand-
kerchief in lieu of a collar. Ho carried a spieler’s port-
manteau, about a -foot square, and hailed his fellow
instructors with : “ Hullo i is yous coves goin’ deown
below?” “Tm only goin’ as fur as Newcastle,” replied
one, and “I’m goin’ the ’ole blinded way,” said
another. “ Where’d you shake that bloomin’ port-
man ty?” asked a third. “I brung it up wi’ me
the last time I was deown teown. It’s blanky ’andy
to carry a shirt and a couple o’ sox in.” These
accomplished young men, the driver told me, were
all “ local coves,” sons of small farmers and laborers who
wanted their smartest boys to “ keep their coats on like
gentlemen” in the “ Eddication Department.” Out of
school such teachers drop back into the vernacular, and
their pupils do likewise. In the class-room the boy may
be taught correctly to parse—" I saw you throwing stones
at the dog,” but since, out of school, the same teacher
will remark to the boy—“ I seen you shottin’ stones at the
darg,” that boy will always “shot” stones. A lad may
pass the highest standard of a public school, but if he
does not learn to speak correctly as a child, no amount of
after care will repair the error. I would suggest that
batches of applicants for bush teacherships be placed in a
room where the Minister for Education can overhear
them ; then, if he should happen to know good English
from bad, the next generation of bush pedagogues won’t
get any of those funny letters from parents to send to
The Bulletin.

“ D.S. W.” : We had been out lamb-tailing
all the morning, our dogs, as usual, being with us.
One of them was the mother of four pups, then
something under a month old. Going home at
dinner-time, she ran on ahead to attend to her
family. Family started to howl as soon as
attended to, and we went to find out the cause.
The mother had evidently been impressed by the
lamb-tailing and had taken to dog-tailing on her
own account, having bitten off three pups’ tails in
the neatest manner possible.

“Camiro”: “ Werrimee’s ” par. (B.
13/1/1900) re sucking-pig, reminds me of an inci-
dent of the early days. Some 50 years ago,
among a mob of bullocks brought from the
Upper Murray to Melbourne, was one highly
objectionable and cantankerous beast nick-
named the “ Rip.” Fences in those days
were almost unknown. Near Flemington
the mob gave some trouble, the “Rip”
breaking away over the Flats. They let
him go. That cheerful animal’s time on
the way back to his old haunts, some 200
miles away, is said to have been about
seven days. Anyhow, he was there when
the drovers got back, and in an even
worse temper than usual. Apparently the
scenery on the return journey had aggravated
him.
“ Flabby Paul ” : Be “ Kangaroo Dick’s ’’
query (8., 30/12/’99) “How should scrub tick
poisoning be treated ? ” Never detach tick by
pulling it out. Either cut it in two with a pair of
scissors or drop kerosene on it. Directly you
catch hold of a tick he starts to inject poison.
(Have had them on myself and on dogs). After
cutting it in half with scissors or killing it with
kerosene, then detach it, and put kerosene on the
spot. Dog is not necessarily done for if paralysed
in hindquarters. Feed him on soft food till he is
well. For the first day or two he will most likely
vomit all he eats, but after that he will steadily
improve, unless another tick fastens on him. If
the dog lives two days he will recover.

“ Gobung ” : Rockhampton is a town in
Mongrelia, bounded on the north by the Fitzroy
River, and on the S.E. and W. by Sneol and large
fohanners. It has a mixed population of whites,
lacks, yellows, and blaek-and-tans; also goats
and dogs, which depasture in the main street and
eke out a precarious existence on door-handles.
Insects, centipedes, cockroaches, and other
stingy creatures congregate in myriads round
the midnight kerosene, and are shovelled up in
cart-loads next morning. It is a lovely place for
rain, when there is any. The staple fluid of com-
merce is rum. Rockhampton has an open railway
station in one of its main streets. This shows the
trustful character of the authorities; they are not
afraid of thieves decamping with . the waiting-
room, or feloniously misappropriating the signal-
boxes. Also, the ticket-office is, or was lately,
the body of an old carriage that had seen better
days. Not long ago they opened a new railway
bridge. It was duly tested, and a learned bridge-
examiner announced authoritatively that the
structure had an oscillatory vibration of 14 yards
to the square pound, or words to that effect, and
that if it didn’t fall it would probably stand.
This is about all there is to say about
Rockhampton. North Rockhampton is
the same, only worse and not so much of it.
“ Asmodeus ” : Talking of black-trackers
reminds me that, years ago, a mailman out
Warialda way reported that he had been
“held up” and the mailbags looted. No tidings
could be gleaned of the marauders for a consider-
able time, when a clue was obtained in a strange
way. An orgie having, taken place at Warialda
the mailman was ar-
rested as a casual
drunk. A watch stolen
from one of the bags
was found upon him,
and he was charged
with the more serious
offence of robbing the
mail, being placed in
irons for safety’s sake.
During the night, how-
ever, by some means or
other, he shed his irons
and effected his escape.
The services of the
local tracker (Tommy
Bums) were called
into requisition.
Tommy tracked the
escapee some miles in
the direction of Triga-
mon station, then up
to the second rail of
a fence, which he had
evidently ascended to
cover his tracks, two
miles along the fence-
rail ; then on to Triga-
mon, about five miles
away. There he had
carefully mixed himself up with a camp of
about 20 other men, but Tommy picked
out his tracks among the crowd, thence
across the river, and on for another five or six
miles over rocky and mountainous country, till
the footprints gave out at the foot of a large tree.
Tommy was nonplussed awhile, and walked round
the tree several times. Then he suddenly climbed
up, and found his prisoner secreted among the top
branches. The fugitive was secured, but the
subsequent prosecution broke down, the owner of
the watch having died since the robbery.

ON A BUSH RACECOURSE-TREE’D!
A case of mistaken identity. Last year
the enterprising municipal council of Blayney
(N.S.W.) speculated in an up-to-date cart for
scavenging services. To all appearances, the vehicle
was first cousin to a country hearse. A farmer
driving into Blayney, a'ong the Norton road,
noticed the sombre vehicle approaching. He
reverently slackened his pace, then pulled up off
the track, and lifted his hat and put down his
pipe. There were no mourners, but he did not
marvel, as he had to hold his nose. He concluded
the deceased had died of something very serious.

A GERMAN LAMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I vas doin’ tam fine shplendid
Buyin’ sheep-skins, wool, and drippin’,
Und my cart-shpring mid ole Roany—
Veil, I lofe dem like mein vrouw;
Now dot lofe affair ish ended,
Und mein face won’t shtop from sklippin’
Ven I dinks apout dot pony
And dot cart-shpring and mein cow.
Duyfel turn dot tam cow over
Mit her tail-board on he’s fender—
If I tole her fifty-tousand
. Times I tole her once, to shdop
Feedin’ always on dot clover,
But, like all dot female gender,
She must want to know the “hows ” and
“ Whys; ” and so she bust up—pop 1
Veil, I harness up mein waggon— ~
For dot cow require some shiftin’,
Und she die close up mein kitchen—
Den I makes mein whip-stock crack,
Und dot cow pehind comes draggin’;
Roany up hill do some liftin’,
Und he sit back in the pritchin
Cornin' down de saddle track.
Soon dot funeral brocession
We comes windin’ down de diggin’s
Till I find a hole dot do me
For a grave-yart for mein cow
Pretty kviek I take bossession
Of dot mine-shaft sunked by Higgins,
Und I gee mein horse-cart to me—
Veil, I haf no horse-cart now.
“ Good-bye Taisy ! ” und she’s splashin*
Forty feet down mit de water ;
But dot rope I not untie it,
Und she drag mein horse-cart in—
Cart-shpring, Roany—all goes crashin’
Down mid tamful noise and schlaughter,
Und den ev’ryding’s so quiet
Dot you could pick up a pin.
BENDEE.
DRY.
The dust-clouds rolled o’er the roads of sand as the weary
teams went through.
The drovers coughed and the drivers swore—they swore
all the drivers knew.
It was in the days of the old man drought, and the rain-
less months went by,
And the bushmen’s greeting when bushmen met was
just, “ My oath, it’s dry 1”
A jackass perched on a gum-tree white, with twisted
limbs and bare,
With a saddened eye on the water-hole and the bony
cattle there ;
And he opened his beak with a grating laugh and a kind
of a lost-soul cry,
And he spoke to the moon, and. the words he said were—
“ It’s dry; it’s dry ; it’s dry 1 ”
The water-hole just off the track was but a patch of slime >
And the frogs moaned there at midnight in a runic froggy
rhyme,
And the crows joined in the chorus, lifting mournful
voices high,
To tell the silent stars—" It’s dry ; God only knows how
dry.”
For through the days of the old man drought all songs
bear one refrain ;
You hear it by the dried-up creek, and o’er the dried-up
plain,
And where the trees beside the road shake bare arms
at the sky,
By day and night the winds lament—“ It’s dry ; it’s dry ;
it’s dry.”
L.J.C.

“Kiltrumpie”: Re
slow trains. In Cen-
tral Q., have more
than once seen engine-
driver, gun in hand,
leave nis train and go
in quest of ducks. A
good stream for duck
runs nearly parallel
with one part of a
certain line, thus
affording sport, walk-
ing exercise, and re-
lief from monotonous
engine-driving on a
slow-timed road. The
train (mixed —timber-
waggons, goods-trucks,
&c., and a passenger-
carriage nearly always
empty) is brought along
by the stoker, about
lialf-a-mile behind the
sporting driver, at a
speed approaching
that of a bullock-dray.
The passenger—there
is sometimes one—
dangerously bad with
that tired feeling,
usually sits on verandah at end of carriage, with
feet dangling over the footboard; or an “ occa-
sional,” not too bad with the “feeling,” will be-
take himself to the van and have a chat with the
guard, who is keeping an anxious look-out at
every creek-bend to see if driver-sport has bagged
anything. You see he gets a third, provided the
game-bag contains more than two, or can other-
wise be divided by three.

ill. J. Brady “ for the defence ” :
Dear Bulletin. —I appear on behalf of the crow—
hitherto abused by almost every Australian scribbler.
To begin with, the crow is not uglynot as ugly as the
jackass anyhow, and he keeps better company. No
respectable bush crow ever forgot himself so far as to
associate with reptiles, and he will draw the line at
uncooked snake —if there’s any cold mutton handy. The
crow in his shining broadcloth goes to the banquet with
Missus Crow in black satin on his arm. The crow has a
dignified carriage, much more dignified than the jackass,
wno wobbles afoot like a Chinese mandarin’s wife of high
degree.
The jackass is popular with the vulgar, simply because
he can extract a coarse joke from the merest trifle. We
have seen Australian Premiers holding office for years on
no better qualification. As a domestic pet the crow beats
the jackass hollow. He learns to talk and even to sing,
makes himself generally amusing and agreeable, and is
fond of children —of the calves of their legs at all events !
The crow, civilised, displays the quality of thrift in a high
degree. He has the bump of acquisitiveness strong, and
has a fine taste in spoons, thimbles, and things.
If he can find a knot-hole in the floor he opens a bank,
and he will spend most of his spare time adding to his
credit balance, though as a precaution against burglars he
never allows anybody to see him making his lodgments.
If he thinks anybody’s looking he immediately displays a
strong interest in the scenery.
In his natural state the crow is unaggressive and of a
most retiring disposition. If anybody invades his territory
he will not stay to argue the point. He shifts, especially
if there ! s anything like a gun in the case. The crow knows
exactly what a gun is for and how far it will carry.
I was once doing a little experimental chicken-farming
in which a patriarchal crow took a keen interest. He
lived a good bit back in the bush, but he would make it
his business to get up very early in the morning to see
how the chickens were coming out of the shell. He would
always alight on a certain fence-post when he was selecting
his poultry, so I put a gin-trap on the post for his con-
venience, but he never made any use of it. Then I added
some strychnine to an egg and partly hid the egg under a
few leaves o give things a casual look, and when the crow
discovered the egg and breakfasted on it I cheerfully
awaited results. After a time he began to look serious,
and apparently concluding that eggs were not good for
him, promptly reproduced his breakfast and was well
enough by afternoon for a full meal of poultry. Then I
loaded another egg. The crow dropped it inside the fence,
and the chickens referred to it and there were several
funerals. Next I “laid-for” that crow with a wire
cartridge* behind a fowl-house, and unwittingly he came
within extreme range and was perforated.
Taken right through, the Australian crow has a hard
life. The law of libel affords him no protection from
Australian newspapir people. Up country they put a
price of fourpence, and even sixpence, on his head. But
it does not pay to go gunning for the crow, unless with
artillery.
The hand and beak of the entire bush are against the
crow. In spring-time the soldier-bird makes his existence
a misery. He cannot sit down to enjoy a little meta-
physical abstraction without being assailed in force. His
hoarse protests avail him nothing. He is hunted like
a very pariah from tree to tree.
No wonder then that he makes reprisals when oppor-
tunity offers. But his reprisals are conducted on strict
economic principles every time. In places the plains
would be absolutely monotonous if the crow did not en-
liven them with his presence.
And yet I met an unappreciative old sundowner re-
cently who spent a lot of his spare time fishing for crows
with a hook and line.
He justified himself on the ground that crows had once
sampled an ex-mate of his somewhere out on the Barcoo.
I tried to explain to this old man that those crows were
simply performing a natural and necessary duty for his
friend, and, by way of analogy, I asked him if he ever
heard of anybody going fishing for an undertaker.
But he simply borrowed tobacco from me and went on
with his sport.
“ Roughrider ” : It is to be hoped our
Patriotic Fund will be better administered than
the British Crimean one was. The committee
which had charge of this affair, at a time when it
had £900,000 in hand, flatly refused any aid what-
ever to the practically : starving widow of a
sergeant who Had fought in almost every Crimean
battle, on the ground that it wasn’t dead certain
that his death arose from disease acquired in the
Crimea. They were very particular about the
letter of the law, were these same committee-men.
Yet they were so joyous and lavish when it was a
case of misappropriating the money for the benefit
of one of their own order that they paid their
alleged honorary secretary, a certain wealthy
Admiral Fishboume, £6OO a year. Also Colonel
Young, who was honorary secretary after Fish-
bourne, not only got a handsome salary,_ but
when he abandoned the billet was pensioned
for life. The Crimean Patriotic Fund was a
fearful and wonderful affair. Who is going to
permanently manage the N.S.W. Patriotic Fund,
and at what salary ?
(By a Victorian Amateur Artist.)
It lias been truly said that practically all
the good verse-makers of Australia are on the
anti-Jingo side. Also, pretty nearly all the bad
ones are loyal and crying out for “blood” and
trying to make it rhyme with “ entrenchment.
The worst specimen up to date is John Brooks
Whitehead, of Melbourne (where Purtell and
Zachariah Sutcliffe came from). John B.W. breaks
out thus on a small sheet of paper :
A PATRIOTIC (?) ITEM BY JEW HYAMS.
At Melb. Exhibition Concert, Jan. 20, 1900.
When a man dies of thirst in a book he
must first of all become a raving maniac, and tear
every stitch of clothing from his body. In the
real thing they die all ways—and the book-way is
about the rarest. The best known of Centralian
victims this year crawled to a bush, sat down on
the right heel (most bushmen’s favorite attitude),
apparently went to sleep like that, and never
woke.
The Australians have rallied around our flag,
The good old flag our fathers bore
Over land and over sea for a thousand years or more.
The Australians have left thc'ir native shores,
Our boys have gone to fight the Boers,
They have volunteered to go in front,
They are standing now the battles’ brunt
Shoulder to shoulder with the British soldier,
Hand in hand with the sons of the sea.
Each heart beats true ’neath the red, white, and blue,
Blood is thicker than water, I know, so do you ;
The Americans proved it at the Forts of Taku.
Our boys are ready for hopes forlorn,
They want to prove they are British born,
And worthy of their sires ;
All Britons do with martial flame,
And so do these sons of ours.
Our boys of our Empire from-every clime
Where the sun never sets, where the sun always shines,
They all speak our dear mother tongue,
They are coming, they are coming, yes coming along
To meet, to meet on dark Africa’s shore
To fight and chastise the insolent Boer.
The ungrateful Boer, now and evermore,
Are down in the world’s estimation ;
They turned on us, ungrateful that they are ;
We saved them from annihilation
By the wild tribes that around them do dwell;
Who would have sent them all pell mell
To where they would never have troubled us more,
The artful, intriguing, treacherous Boer.
John Brooks Whitehead.
154 Commercial-road, Prahran, January sth, 1900.
Mr. Rudyard Kipling is only a strippling,
That is in age to me ;
He was born in 65, and I in 33.
I was dancing upon this earth
When Rudyard Kipling could not see.

FREE SPEECH.
The Boers ain’t goin’ to stop us from a-talkin’ on the
Band,
For we ain’t blanky foreigners, so let ’em understand
That every man is free to speak beneath the British flag ;
As long as he don’t say we’re wrong his tongue can freely
wag.
But when we find him on a job a-talkin’ as he likes,
Well, he must be very careful not to say what we dislikes,
For to his own opinion every foreigner is free—
But we’ll bash him on the boko if with us he don’t agree.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC) CONTRIBUTORS.
H.W.O. : Joke too weak to carry sketch U.B.A.
(Brisbane): Unsuitable... .C.P. : Absent-minded Beggar
been illustrated to death.
Apropos what is known as “ Goyder’s line
of rainfall,” now almost universally accepted as
the late Surveyor-General’s division of S. Aus-
tralia into a southern area suitable for agriculture,
and a northern area too droughty for anything but
wool-raising, the present Surveyor-General in a
recent report shows that the late Goyder did not
draw the line for that purpose at all. A quarter
of a century ago, special rent concessions having
been asked for, he had to define the territory
which had suffered from a drought then in blast;
and this line, marked for that special purpose,
was the result.

nified attitude for a parson,

“ O ! peaceful is this village dear,
And oh ! how how very pretty ;
To think that all will disappear
And one day be a city.”
’Twere easy thus to jingle for a month. Thus :
Though good the metre and the rhyme,
Damfoolish is the ditty 1
That people thus will waste our time —
It is a blooming pity.
Hash-foundry : A great truth Sympathy : Many
thanks Amb. G. : One verse printed with some mis-
givings :
Wend me by the pool
Fern-girt, and reflects the sky
She bathed her brow there.
A mortgage on his possession
The Kangaroo did give
To pay the cost of chasing
The Bullock, who does live
In “ fontains ” and in “ kopjes,”
The Kangaroo pawned his land
For Whale, who cannot stand
On shore —we must confess.
From his Brethren, the Kangaroo
Gives blood for Titles, straight,
Without a refferando.
Such, is his own ones fate,
And if they disapprove.
He puts them off their Billet
Who “ sympathizes ” fill it.
He has no country love.

PRESENT ASPECT OF THE N.S.W POLITICAL HEAVENS.
“ When the Lyne Ministry was sworn in I was told that Mr. Barton would probably go to
England in connection with the Federal Bill. Mr. Lyne pledged his word that if ever he contem-
plated sending anyone to England in that connection, nothing should be done without the
approval of Parliament. Mr. Lyne abused me greatly for paying Mr. Neild a sum of money to
cover the actual expenses of work performed two or three years before, without first having
obtained the approval of Parliament, and I was thrown out of office upon that point."

the “ Absent-.
minded Beggar ” is
becoming a somewhat risky \
business, owing to the carelessness
of the coin-throwers. Both Mr.
Harry Rickards and Mr. Tom Rain-
ford have suffered severely through
being struck by pennies.
Let the “ star comique ” adopt' this cos-
tume and he can sing “ Pass the hat for
your Country’s sake, and pay-ah, pay-ah,
pay-ah-pay-ah ” in perfect safety.

deal has been
■written of late of
the advantages derived
ffom the free supply of jam
to troops on active service. A suggestion
has been made that if one of the virtues be due to the presence of

might be attained by a supply of
honey, and several bee farmers
in Central Cumberland are
considering the advisa-
bility of offering a
large quantity to
the authorities at
a low rate, by way
of experiment.

Sleep When Work is Done.
During’ the late summer (1896) I passed several
weeks in a foreign city nearly 700 miles further
south than London. We had about ten days
intense heat, and for six nights when it was worst,
I do not think I slept as many hours altogether!
Tiie resulting nervous collapse was simply awful.
My brain reeled and I could neither understand nor
do anything rightly. I walked, as the good Book
says, “in a vain show. ” What a blessed thing is
sleep ; how destructive, how killing to lose it.
In healthy sleep the nervous system is inactive
—more especially that of the brain and spinal cord.
The body lies quiet, the muscles relaxed, the pulse
slower than when we are awake, and the breathing
less frequent but deeper. Then nature—wise
keeper of the House of Life—proceeds to renew
the energies expended through the day. To miss
this renewal, even in part, is to tread the edge of
insanity and look into the mouth of death.
“Methought I heard a voice cry, ‘Sleep no
more; Macbeth doth murder sleep!”’ So said
Macbeth himself; but there is a power which
murders more sleep than ever did Duncan’s
assassin; and to that Mrs. Beattie alludes when
she says of her daughter, “ She got no sleep.”
“In May, 1891,” writes the lady, “ m y daughter
Letitia fell into a low, weak condition, and could
not get up her strength. She had no appetite,
and what little food she took gave her great pain
at the chest and side. She got no sleep at night ,
and became very nervous and low spirited.
“ As time passed by she grew weaker aud more
'feeble, and we thought she would never get better.
A doctor attended her but none of his medicines
seemed to suit her case, and for two years she
continued to suffer.
“One day she read in a book about Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup and what it had done for
others. She got a bottle from Mr. Nieholls,
chemist, Donegal Place, and after taking it began
to improve, being able to eat, and her food
agreeing with her. After having used this medi-
cine a short time she was strong and well as ever,
and has since been in good health. For a long
time I myself suffered from weakness and indiges-
tion, and seeing what good Mother Seigel’s Syrup
had done I took it and was' completely restored to
sound health, You may use this statement as
you like. (Signed) (Mrs.) Mary Beattie, 23,
Trinity Square, Belfast, August 14th, 1896.”
“In the early part of 1894, ” writes another, “ I
began to suffer from indigestion. I had a poor
appetite, and all food gave me pain at my chest
and a gnawing feeling at my stomach. No
matter how light the food T. took I had pain and
sick headache,
“For over a year I was tormented like this,
when Mr. John Weir, living in this place, told me
how he had been cured by Mother Seigel’s Syrup,
and recommended me to try it. I did so, and
soon all pain left me, and I have since, enjoyed
good health. I know many persons residing in
this district who. have often been benefited by
taking the same medicine. Often in my shop I
hear persons say what it has done for them. You
are at liberty to publish this letter. (Signed) Alex
Wilson, General Merchant, Castlewellan, New
castle, Co. Down, Ireland, Aug. 15th, 1896.”
Indigestion or dyspepsia destroys the power to
sleep by starving, and thus weakening the nervous
system. Indeed the whole body starves, and is
thrown into profound disorder. Food may be
taken into the stomach, but, as people constantly
say, “It does no good. ” No ; but in a sense it
does harm. Fermented, soured, undigested it
develops poisons which act upon the system some-
what as a pestilence does upon a community,
flesh is lost, strength gives away to trembling
weakness, courage is supplanted by fear, and life
is dreary and desolate. That Mother Seigel’s
oyrup should be able to cure a disease so common
and so terrible is reason for gratitude to us all.
£ or the healthy work and sleep.
The Launceston (Tas.) Daily Telegraph
has discovered a new kind of white Australian
whom it proposes to train for the war. And this
is what it says of its recent find in issue of Jan. 8 :
Their height ranges from sft. Bin. to 9in. to almost any-
thing up to 7ft. They have been in the saddle ever since
they could “ toddle ” and in some cases even before that.
■ . . They ride like no other man in the world can ; they
have a pretty and workmanlike seat from which nothing
short of a “regular crumpler” will shift them. ... It is
a common thing ... to see an absolutely unhandled colt
' 1 in , hand > and within two or three days this
colt will be taken out “ kangarooing ” ridden by a 6ft.,
long-legged, keen-eyed man, armed with a Winchester
rifle. . . . They can sight a sheep or “ beast ” on a hill or
in a valley at a distance which the ordinary individual
could hardly make out with the aid of a telescope, and
tell you whose it is and wi*re it comes from. . . . They
swim rivers, cross deser.s, nle steep ichases, bowl over
running rabbits with bah cartridges. Wnat they cannot
jump the pair will climb over. . . . These men will . . .
fight any man ; they can drink anything, from “ post and
rails ” tea to bush rum, manufactured from sheep tobacco,
turpentine, painkiller, and boiling water ; and subsist on
cold mutton and damper all their lives.
“ Old Dad,” toiling and moiling on his selection,
wouldn’t know himself in this gorgeous, wild-
Indian description.
“ Y.A.” on “Thrift”
“ Thrift is a sign of inferior race ” (8.-, 25/2/’92). Wrong.
The very inferior races, such as Australian aborigines,
Oape bushmen, etc., are notoriously thriftless. Extreme
thriftiness is an attribute of nations that have passed the
zenith of their power, and whose people, accustomed to
having all they wanted, must now by means of thrift put
by enough to secure a part at least of what they have
been accustomed to. Take France and China, typical
examples of thrifty countries in Europe and Asia. Each
has been a greater power, the one but recently, the other
when Britons painted their bodies blue. The Syrians,
nearly all Indian races (Tamils, Sikhs, Parsees, Bengalees,
Afghans, etc.) exhibit this trait in a marked form : they,
one aftd all, are thrifty. Not so the Malays, who have never
known a higher state of civilisation, whereas the Javanese,
a nearly allied race, who have retrogressed from a higher
civilisation in the 16th century, are thrifty to penuriousness.
Thriftiness is quite compatible with laziness. The Portu-
guese are essentially thrifty and no less lazy. So,
too, are their colonial descendants, the Brazilians,
&c. One might consider the Germans and Japan-
ese as exceptions. Perhaps thereby they prove my
. theory, but neither race is essentially thrifty, the thrift
of each is merely a corollary to the known fact of their
industry and sound common sense. In a mixed police
force I know of the Malay and Dyak contingent were ever
in debt, the Europeans were uniformly penniless, while the
Sikh and Pathan brigade saved 50 per cent, of its salary,
of which the greater part went to India, the balance being
loaned to needy comrades at more than cent, per cent.
The Indian is the worst of aliens in this respect. He
spends nothing in the foreign country where he resides.
By usury, trade, and thrift he thrives. The China-
man, while economical, is.not penurious, is markedly
charitable, and stands head and shoulders above
the cringing “ Tamil ” and foxy Bengalee.' In the native
Malay states in Borneo, and even in the most populous
Straits Settlements, in spite of the übiquitous “ Chetty ”
(Indian money-lender class from S.W. coast), the Sikh
policeman is “ uncle ” to a host of impecunious nephews
whose security is too thin for the “chetty.” For £lOO
the charge is £l2O, repayable at £lO a month. The
first instalment 'is deducted from loan “to save
trouble.” European police-officers are their best cus-
tomers, for fear of exposure is a great security

“ 0.” : Heard a German sailor deliver this
lecture on temperance over a Hobart bar-counter:
“Und my mate he vas a-speening it oop in the
pooblic-howse; und I say, ‘Vy you go fooling your
monies liker that? Vy you no puy a moonkey
yacket? Ven you geds ouderside, und de nor’-
vester he blow, you vill den cry oudt, “Vydid
not I puy a moonkey yacket ?” ’ Und he say,
‘To ’ell vith your moonkey yacket! ’ und goes on
a-speening it oop. All a-righdt. We geds ouder-
side, und oop comes a nor’-vester, cold as ize. I
sees my mate a-carouching oonder de galley. Und
he say softly to himselluf, ‘Blow yently, nor’-
vester, blow yently, blow yently.’ Und I smiles
to mineselluff und I say, ‘Ha ! mine friendt. You
vill not go a-speening it oop in de pooblic-howse
nexer dime. You vill puy a moonkey yacket.’
‘Yes,’ he say, ‘blow yently, nor’-vester, blow
yently.’ Vat you haf, Meester Pooblie-hows-s-se ? ”
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. , Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
A prominent American newspaper-woman
writes, in the Arena, on the female gutter
journalist. Her professional life is about four
years, after which she is written out. She usually
comes up as a nice fresh girl from the country,
with a local newspaper reput xtion. The editor
sends her out masquerading in all sorts of forms,
involving much lying and deception, for instance,
as Salvation lass, masseuse, nurse to a gentleman,
and other ingenuities. She even invites insult by
passing as a woman of the pavement, all for the pro-
duction of columns of copy, with her own name,
and the names of such victims as she can impale.
Weather Forecasts.
When a picnic or a cricket-match or any other
outdoor jaunt is in prospect, how eagerly we look
for Mr. Barrachi’s or Mr. Wragge’s prophetic
utterances on the subject. In these colonies where
climatic changes and disturbances are so rapid
that, though the morning may be warm and sunny,
the afternoon rain may drench you to the skin,
and the evening air chill you to the marrow, it
must be very difficult for even the most expert
weather scientist to prophesy with anything like
accuracy. We frequently have the greatest diffi-
culty in judging what clothing to wear, and es-
pecially will this be the case with those suffering
from liver and kidney complaints. The only safe-
guard for such is a prompt recourse to Warner’s
Safe Cure. It quickly regulates the liver, and
has a soothing tonic effect upon the kidneys, re-
moving the pains in the small of the back, and is
a positive cure for all forms and stages of kidney
disease. At least Mr. Mackinnon of 203 Liver-
pool Street, Paddington, Sydney, N.S.W., found
it so, for he says : “I suffered terrible pains in my
back, and found no relief until I used Warner’s
Safe Cure and pills. I would very strongly advise
all my friends and others to use same, and I can
recommend it to be a certain cure. I always re-
commend Warner’s Safe Cure to my friends, who
also have found it very beneficial.”
Much bunkum has been poured forth by
jingoes in the Yic. Civil Service. They kept up
the rapid splutter till a Minister gave the vague
understanding that the Cabinet would “deal with ”
any civil servant who wasn’t fully convinced of
the justice of the war. The Victorian Government
has no power to “deal with” any civil servant
for holding any opinions on any subject. The
Public Service Act provides : “ After the passing
of this act no officer in the Public Service shall be
dismissed therefrom or suffer any other penalty in
respect thereof except for the causes and in the
manner set forth in this Act.” No part of the Act
says anything about anybody’s political opinions,
therefore if the M‘Lean Government carries out its
threat some fine large bills for damages may be
expected.
