POLITICAL
POINTS
A Sydney weekly states :
The anti-Billites still have a kick—and a strong one at
that—left in them.
Quite so. They are going to kick the bucket
violently.
Candidates for billets on the permanent
committee to distribute the N.S.W. Patriotic
Fund are already pushing to the front, and over a
dozen sharp-witted individuals are racing for the
secretaryship, which will presumably carry a good
salary. In such cases the secretary is often the
largest beneficiary.
Townsville (Q.) Bulletin states that out
of 969 persons arrested last year in its town 150
could neither read nor write, 768 could read only,
or read and write very imperfectly, 45 could read
Und write well, and only six had a superior educa-
tion. A terrible record of ignorance for a land
which professes to have a compulsory education
system.
“H.”: London Times has a Sydney
correspondent, who evidently writes under orders,
like Miss Shaw, to give a reflex of English ideas.
With some ability, and at considerable length,
he keeps worrying at the idea that Australia must
contribute £2,000,000 a year to the support of the
British navy, instead of a paltry £126,000. This
is the sort of thing that will put loyalty to the
pinch.
This is the logic of Government grants in
Queensland
Firstly. The sugar industry in this district will never
flourish unless we have a central mill. Secondly. A
central mill is no earthly use unless Government also
builds us a tram-line to our port. Thirdly. The tram-line
is no use unless a wharf be built at its seaward end.
Fourthly. What use is the_wharf unless the approaches
be deepened ?
And so on ad infinitum.
Ex-P.M.G. Duffy startled Yic. Assembly
last week by rising to ask a trivial little question
re the firing of grass by engine sparks. It
was the first question he had asked in ten years,
said Duffy, and in the excitement of the historic
moment he forgot that he had merely given notice
of what he intended to say and was not actually
saying it. On learning that the House wanted a
verbal statement of his question, Duffy pulled
himself together and duly did what he hadn’t done
for 10 years.
London Morn ing
Leader, under the head-
ing—“ Sad End to a Use-
ful Life,” tells of the death
of an aged Folkestone
(Eng.) magnate, father of
Mrs. Applebee, murdered
a year or two ago in the
Transvaal. It states that
it was never discovered
whether the crime was
committed by Kaffirs or
whites, and adds bitterly
that the Transvaal Legis-
lature refused to grant any
satisfaction o r redress.
This sort of thing sounds
pretty awful, and when the
average person reads it
the callousness and ineffi-
ciency of the Boer Govern-
ment seem to stick out like
the fire-box of a locomotive.
And yet the Queensland
Parliament failed to give
any satisfaction or redress
over the three awful mur-
ders of British subjects at
Gatton —in fact, there have
been hundreds of undis-
covered murders perpe-
trated in Australia, and
none of the Legislatures
has ever given satisfaction
for a single one of them.
And supposing the English-
man, Nuffield, had been
treated in the Transvaal
as he was in N.S.W., on the same absolute
lack of evidence, what a shriek of rage there
would have been about, the Boer hatred of
England, and the corruption of Boer judges and
of Boer justice !
A Queenslander Writes :
Had the late session of Q. Parliament continued much
longer the Labor-party might have burst up. Glassey
was unreliable ; Brown—the best all-round man in the
party—could hardly drag his creaking bones to Parlia-
ment House ; Lesina and Givens were nightly making
asses of themselves, while the sober-minded members of
the party showed visible signs of despair and disgust.
Worst of all, Dawson was utterly worn out. The ex-
Premier is getting into a pathetic state of mental and
physical weariness brought on by keeping himself at
high pressure through all-night sittings without sufficient
solid tucker or rest. Moreover, Dawson has not received-
the cordial and loyal support he deserved from some
prominent members of his party, who left the worn-out
leader to do all the hard toil during ‘the Barron Falls, Sea-
forth Estate, and other stinkful debates. He has friends
and sympathisers in plenty, but friendship and sympathy
in Queensland mostly consist of invitations to “come
and have another.” Dawson very rarely takes another.
If he did take another it, on top < of his other troubles,
would probably break him up altogether.
The Forrest crowd recently made an effort
to work up an Anti-Separation League on the
W.A. Goldfields, by way of counterblast to the
Separation movement. It only got 28 members,
and they refuse to allow their names to be pub-
lished, presumably lest public scorn should fall on
them like a tree, and crush them. A wiser
Government than Bigjohn’s would not have thus
exposed its weakness.
The discredited Continuous Government of
Bananaland, having exhausted the old revolution-
ary and socialistic bogies wherewith it was accus-
tomed to scare the electors from voting Labor, has
now fallen back on the Disloyalty-accusation as a
last resource. According to the Courier, a change
of Government in Queensland will be fatal to the
Empire, the welfare of which is somehow indis-
solubly connected with the Administration which
has perpetrated so many stinkful jobs.
Bad as N.S.W. municipal law is on the
face of it, it is made worse when the courts begin
to interpret its provisions. A recent interpreta-
tion of one clause limits the right to vote to the
actual person who pays the rates. Hitherto, if
the owner paid the rates in the occupier’s name
the latter could vote, but this interpretation,
which is said to have the approval of the Full
Court, knocks most occupiers out, and narrows
the municipal suffrage so much that a shadow
could hardly crawl through the Act sideways.
Premier John Forrest, of W.A., had a
chance to size up his popularity in his own province
on his recent journey eastward. At Albany,
which Bigjohn is doing his best to ruin by his
scheme for shifting the mail-steamer business to
Fremantle, right at the back-door of Perth (where
brother Alick’s property is), one hotel-keeper
refused to have his premises polluted by Bigjohn’s
presence. Also a cabman, being called upon to
shift the great man back to the pier, refused with
some considerable blanky to have his cab and
horse thus contaminated; and the people burnt
Forrest in effigy during his stay.
The well-equipped viticultural college at
Rutherglen (Vic.) had, at a recent date, only two
scholars. Which circumstance emphasises the
fact that, despite the persistent howl for rural
education, the rural population has no desire for
anything out of the old happy-go-lucky, rule-of-
thumb order of things that did very well for its
father and its grandfather. It doesn’t want science
at any price. It wants to sit down and smoke,
and try to strike the winner of the Benambra
Stakes. And as the State can’t round the popu-
lation into its colleges with a stockwhip, all the
State can do is to sit still and consider how much
too previous it has been.
The Queensland folks boast that their
educational system is “ free, secular, and com-
pulsory.” The boast is entirely at variance with
fact. The Queensland schools are not free, inas-
much as the local public has to contribute one-fifth
of the cost of buildings. Neither is education secular,
for a Parliamentary motion has just sanctioned the
spending of State scholarship funds on the de-
nominational colleges. Neither is the Q. system
compulsory, for no youngster in Bananaland was
ever yet compelled to attend school by an attend-
ance officer. Which knocks the legs from under a
triple fallacy. The freedom doesn’t matter so very
much, but the ‘ ‘ secular and compulsory ” are
things of real importance.
HE PREFERRED TREASURE ON EARTH.
HEAD OF THE FIRM (who always likes to take an
interest in the welfare of those under him) to
OFFICE-BOY: “ Well, Charles, I’m glad to hear
you won the guinea prize for running at your
Sunday-school picnic! ”
OFFICE-BOY (with some disgust ): “ Yes ; but would
yer believe it? the mean blokes, instead of the
cash, gave me a guinea Bible !”
S.A. democrats, in view of the approach-
ing Council elections, are widely publishing
Sudden Solomon’s recent statement that the
Commonwealth Bill provides a “model for a free
and enlightened people.” The Liberals only ask
for a reduction of the present qualification from
£2O to £l5, as advocated by the Holder Govt,,
and they want to know what the Tories object to,
considering that the Commonwealth Bill, the
“model” aforesaid, provides for adult suffrage for
both Houses!

Deakin lefc down his friend Syme badly
over the appointment to go to England on the
Federation business. Day after day the Age was
barracking for a union of the Liberal party under
Deakin’s leadership. All the time Deakin said
not a word, although he had accepted M‘Lean’s
offer and was packing up his trunks. Some say
that Deakin should have given Syme the wink not
to go on writing him up as a possible leader, but
the eye that Deakin winks with is fixed on the
Commonwealth Parliament now.
Snake-yarn from S.A. Critic :
Significant figures in the Transvaal'Patriotic Fund sub-
scriptions to date : The Register, £2213 4s. ; Advertiser,
£213 18s. lid. Which again shows that the Democrat
never puts his hand in his pocket.
Well, if he did, he very likely would find nothing,
the Fatman’s hand having been there.already.
It is almost incredible that J. C. Neild’s
famous report on Old Age Pensions, which
wrecked the Reid Ministry, and was intended to
be paid for out of the Treasury to the tune of some
£7OO or more, contains not a line relating to
the Maoriland Act; yet Mfioriland is the one State
that has adopted a thorough-working old-age
pension system.
The discussion of the status and emolu-
ments of State Governors at the Premiers’ Con-
ference will not carry the question to a conclusion.
It is essentially a question for people and Parlia-
ment to determine, and Premiers, no matter what
their opinions, count for only one vote each in the
matter. Canada elects its State Governors, which
should be a valuable hint.
The Cow-Shaking Government of N.S.W.
has now become a dealer in anonymous letters, an
evesdropper and listener at key-holes, and a
general bearer of small tales and gossip. Its
latest proceeding has been to hold an official in-
quiry into charges of “disloyalty” laid against a
South Coast teacher by an anonymous correspon-
dent. The accusations were found to be wholly
groundless. The average citizen, however loyal
he may be, and however much he may admire the
loyalty of his Cow-Shaking Government, will soon
begin to despise the undignified busybodies who
fuss around with anonymous communications in
this fashion.
Horrible passages in a letter from Private
Hickey of the N.S.W. Contingent, published in
Maitland (N.S.W.) paper:
At Belmont the Boers buried their dead by throwing a
few stones on their bodies, leaving legs and arms exposed.
Some of our chaps put bits of biscuit in their mouths,
pinned the British colors on them, cut their fingers off if
they had a ring worth while ; and one chap having a bad
pair of boots carne across a dead Boer with a good pair,
and quietly exchanged.
The Bulletin devoutly hopes that these yams
about Australian soldiers thus grossly insulting
their dead enemies will be promptly disproved.
No story yet told about the Boers, even by their
worst enemy, sounds more unpleasant than the
above, and if it isn’t true the “loyal ” paper that
publishes it is a disgrace to this country. Will
the military authorities hold a strict inquiry
into these charges when the Contingent comes
back, and clear Australia from the accusation of
being a land of savages ?

The official list of arrivals and departures
in Westralia for ’99 shows that the province lost
887 men by excess of departures, and gained 940
women by excess of arrivals, making the total in-
crease only 53. In 1898 the increase from this
source was 3953; it was 22,598 in 1898, and
35,949 in 1897. In fact, 1899 was the worst year
since 1888. Evidently the great boom is dead,
and Westralia must needs sober down to dull,
commonplace toil like its neighbors, and depend:
for its growth on the common, ordinary plain in-
fant, with occasional twins to push things along.
In apology for the stinginess of local sur-
face-patriots, Melb. Argus sneers at Sydney
Bushmen’s Fund, as an outcome of wealthy men’s
generosity. Fact is that the moneyed Victorians
who have gassed so much are scarcely represented'
in the various funds; even Mayor M‘Eaeham,
with his brand-new title, hasn’t done anything
astonishing in the way of cash. The best test of
patriotism would be a man-to-man collection in.
Legislative Council, Assembly, and City Corpora-
tion ; if the first-mentioned body couldn’t pan out
£20,000 at least it should be put under the public
ban and curse for rank disloyalty.
The “ loyal ” papers of Australia continue
to print letters from soldiers in South Africa
containing weird charges of brutality against.
British and Australian soldiers, and the loyal
public reads them without, apparently, even
realising that they are weird, and without a
word of protest. For instance, this is from Mel-
bourne Argus
Some of the Boers are glad to be taken, because they
don’t want to fight, but one of the generals said to his
officers : “You are taking too many prisoners. I hope
you understand me.” This means no quarter for the
Boers.
If The Bulletin even hinted that a British
general had given orders, directly Or indirectly, to
introduce the methods of African savagery into a
war between white races, it would have been
cursed by the loyal push from one end of Austra-
lia to another as a Boer emissary or something of
the sort. Yet the loyal Argus prints this shud-
dersome slander without turning a hair. And so
far not a voice has been raised in protest. Has
Britain no friends in Australia ?
A strange and dreadful loyalist, signing
h’mself “ John Bull,” writes to Rockhampton (Q.)
Morning Bulletin to groan about the attitude of
the Worker and of this paper. He says among
other things:
They have always depended on the Power they curse for
sustenance, and now they bite the hand that gives.
The frantic Jingo has got things upside-down in
his haste. No democratic paper in this country
gets any sustenance from the Power in question,
and the “ hand that gives ” is too economical to
give a penny. But the present Bulletin writer
is pleased to say that the British Government has
depended on him for sustenance to some extent,
and his hand has given it a good deal of money.
At least, in earlier days, when he lived in Eng-
land, that Government had him for over £lOO in
stamp-duty in one lump, and it didn’t seem in the
least grateful. It actually tried to make the bill
more. After sustaining the British Government
in this liberal fashion, he claims the right to criti-
cise his dependent whenever he feels like it.
FREE SPEECH.
The right to say the pretty thing
That pleases fool, and pimp, and king.
The speech is free whate’er befall;
The soul is tongue-tied, that is all.


SOME
PIANO
PONT’S
DON’T choose .an old second-
hand piano because it is pre-
sumably cheap, however
strongly tempted to do so. It
will be the dearest you can
buy, because it will always
need expensive repairs.
DON’T expect your child to
become even a moderately good
player if you give him or her
an instrument with a worn-out
mechanism to practise upon•
you will expect the impossible.
DON’T be misled by the
attractive advertisement of the
“ newspaper widow ” or the
kindly disposed person in dis-
tressed circumstances who have
a piano to dispose of.
DON’T choose a piano with
a poor, thin, wiry tone that
becomes worse and worse in
the coui-se of time. When
buying remember
these DONT’S.

Victorian politicians comment in a very
uncomplimentary style on Chamberlain’s despatch
recommending the Australian Governments to
favor the Eastern Extension Co.’s proposed new
cable in opposition to the Pacific State-owned
cable. This is not the first time Chamberlain has
used his official position in the interests of British
capitalists at the expense of Australia. He did
the same over the New Guinea land grab.
One of the loyal gentry, who raised a
company of Yeomanry for Boer-hunting, was The
Bulletin’s old, unhonored acquaintance, Bob
Sutton. But Bob’s little co. appears to have been
wound-up, “owing to difficulties with the War
Office.” _R. S. Sevier, the modern yeoman, has
been raising difficulties ever since this journal first
knew him. When he was seeking the bubble
reputation as Bob Sutton, the “English book-
maker” (of Australia) he brought trouble to him-
self and Lord Deerhurst by plugging the nose of
that son of a belted earl in a Melbourne pub.
Also, he troubled the pretty little Australian wife
who divorced him. Then he went back to his
native land and married the Marquis of Ailes-
bury’s sister, and somehow the lady’s name got
into the law reports owing to difficulties with her
previous sweetheart and some old love letters, and
presently R. S. Sevier, Esq,, started a long series
of difficulties with his creditors. Furthermore,
Lord Deerhurst aforesaid got into difficulty with
his creditors in consequence, perhaps, of the nose-
plugginghe had received from fatal Bob in days of
old. Everything that is touched by such as
Sutton turns to trouble seemingly. If he persists
in associating himself with Patriotism and all that
sort of thing, the Empire had better prepare for
the worst.
Higgins, M.L.A. (Vic.), is trying to dig
out the details of an unsavoury military scandal.
Some time ago Victoria wasted its substance in
re-arming its troops with a hopelessly obsolete
rifle —one which the shocked British authorities
promptly took from them when they and the
venerable blunderbusses trotted ashore at the
Cape. The weapon was selected by an alleged
committee of experts in London, and it now
transpires that some members of the committee
were shareholders in the concern which worked off
this dead stock on a trusting province.
War-fever seems still to rage high over in
Dunedin, Maoriland. Every week or so a meet-
ing is held, and Sandy heaves his braw self upon
the platform to declare that the Contingent Fund
has now readied the sum of something-or-other
thousand pounds seven shillings and sixpence half-
penny. (That half-penny is direfully Scotch.)
Then the community shakes itself by the hand for
being so generous, entirely oblivious of the psycho-
logical fact that when a community gives largely
to one form of charity it simply means that other
forms of charity receive less than their share.
When Tammas gives his sixpence to the conting-
ent, it doesn’t mean a whisky the less to Domnas,
it means that Tammas contrives not to see the
plate next Sawbath when collections are being
made for the Distressed Washerwoman’s Fund, or
some other thing to that effect. The lady who
shakes her tambourine at street corners is be-
coming an institution and receives favorable men-
tion ; but the Maoriland platform-orator who
praised the matrons of his district because they
“ went on the streets in aid of the Contingent
Fund,” will suddenly find himself dead some day,
and never know the reason why.

The essence of Lord Charles Beresford’s
book on China:—•
There is not a single spot or direction in China to which
one can point and say, “ Here is improvement, promise,
reform. The Emperor’s hasty but well-meant edicts of
reform cost him his influence. The progressive mandarins
have all been thrust from office. Corruption is the atmos-
phere in which official China lives and moves. An inter-
view with the Empress Dowager costs £lOOO for the chief
eunuch who is a sham eunuch! A degraded viceroy
pays £20,000 for his reinstatement. The Chinaman in
charge of the great arsenal at Tientsin receives a salary of
£2l a month. The arsenals at Canton and Nanking are
making gingalls cannon Oft. long fired from men’s
shoulders. Everywhere “enormous sums of money are
bemg expended upon war material that in most cases is
absolutely useless.” All executive and administrative
posts are still given to men who pass examinations in the
Chinese classics. Many troops are regularly drilled with
bows and arrows. One General met by Lord Charles re-
ceives pay for 10,000 men. He has actually 800,-and on
inspection day he fills up his ranks with coolies hired at
"f: * ™ e inspector being, of course, bribed to say
that he has found the army in good order. The Grand
Uanal is dry for many miles during four months of the
year. Piracy is rampant and increasing. Whole districts
—indeed, provinces—are devastated by rebellion, which
with mP ena l ® overnmen t is utterly powerless to cope

The Desert Duel.
Two and two—they lay them down
Underneath the nighest trees;
Scant of breath and glad of death,
Worn of hoof and weak of knees;
Grim the Fates above them frown,
While the dumb brutes lay them down.
Grim the Fates and fierce the sky !
Life and Drought, with sword and shield
Near and far, in ancient war,
Fight the fight where Life must yield—
Vanquished yield, or turn and fly •
So the dumb brutes sink and die.
A wind goes by—Wherefore, O Wind,
Ihe slaughter-sword, the red assault?
iloaiismr steals, and silent wheels
mu j brutal sun in heaven’s vault:
lhe dull-red desert, scarred and lined
With arid creeks, is dumb and blind.
RODERIC QUINN.

With the holiday season coming on, and num-
bers of people either going themselves or sending their
families to the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest has begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables—plate,
jewellery, papers, &c.—at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street (hack of Paling’s), and business there is proportion-
ately brisk. We would recommend Bulletin readers to
call'and inspect the massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upon reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very moder-
ate rates.*

move to larger premises. Clients are requested to address
orders to 150 Clarence-street, instead of 486 Kent-street,
as heretofore.*

WRITE FOR CATALOGUE
SPORTING NOTIONS.
■ : The bowling in the recent interprovincial
and Australian XI. v. Rest of Australia cricket-matches, at
Sydney was only of very ordinary merit, and there’s no
doubt a few good new bowlers will need to be raked up
before the next British team arrives. New left-handers
Saunders and M Beth, who are understood in a vajme
kind of way to have done great work, come out of the
interprovincial match with the very poor respective
records of 5 for 220 and 6 for 164. Hugh Trumble’s
average, despite excellent bowling in the second innings,
is 6 for 197 ; and Mary Ann Noble, with all his awful care
and everlasting off-theory, only gathered in 9 for 157—and
it’s the best average of the match. In collecting 6 for 90
against the Aus. XI. first innings Saunders put up a good
effort, and he s no doubt the better of the two left-handers,
but there’s too much sameness in his trundling. Howell
and Jones have gone off, Pye bowls “ tripe,” and Laver
and Hopkins only wheel up very ordinary stuff. Wanted,
a good slow break bowler.
**‘ * •
“ W.”r With the exception of, perhaps,
Trumper, Noble is undoubtedly the greatest batsman in
N.S.W. Though not the dashing bat he was, he is sound
and a real trier. His recent 155 against Vic. won that
match for N.S.W., and his 94, at the expense of the Rest
of Australia, alone saved the Australian Eleven first
innings total from looking ridiculous.
* * * *
Stucky, the sturdy little smiter from East
Melbourne, is already a big favorite with the Sydney
cricket crowd. Although hardly up to the minimum
volunteer height, he is as plucky as a bull-dog ant, and
cracks everything hard. In hi's last three innings on
Sydney Cricket Ground he has knocked up 54, 53 and 88
—all by free, slashing batting.
• * • •
“ W.”: In heaping up 73 for the Rest of Aus-
tralia v. Australian XI., on Saturday, “ Grunnny ” Graham
batted right up to his. form of a few years back. He
calmly walked out of his crease. as of old and made his
own length for the .well-pitched ones, and pulled the full
or short ones to the leg-fence. With the exception of
Graham himself, occasionally, there are no batsmen like
the Graham of old now.
• • • »
Few Australian cricketers have been given more
nick-names by the crowd than huge stonewaller Alick
Mackenzie. “ Helpless Alick,” “ Old Flatfoot” (he shares
that one with Premier Lyne), “ The Corpse,” and “ Feet ”
are only some of ’em. The crowd have also been struck
by “Mary Ann” Noble’s overgrown understandings.
When Hill was fielding in the outfield on Sat. one of the
push pointed to said Mary Ann and asked who he was,
and Clem informed him. “ I thought it was ’im by his
flamin’ great hoof,” answered the pushite calmly.
* * * *
A Croweater writes : “ Not much doubt that the
best team wins the Sheffield shield this season. N.S.W.
has made 2541 runs lor 60 wickets, against her opponents
1765 for 73 wickets. N.S. Wales’ batting has been won-
derfully consistent in every engagement, and her bowling
is quite equal to that of Victoria, although the latter pro-
vince’s eleven has surpassed in the fielding. S.A. suffers
from want of young players, F. Hack being the only
junior tried who looks like permanently remaining in big
matches. The shield-matches leave a doubt as to which is
the best all-round player in Australia, Noble or Trumble.
Noble’s fine batting-record, and the fact that he is
the best point in Australia, perhaps place him ahead
of the big yictorian. The Sheffield shield has been
going for seven years. ' Fourteen players have made over
1000 runs in Shield 'matches, and it is significant
that Reedmah, the-consistent S.A. player, is the only one
of the number who has not been to England. Noble
heads the batting with an average of 55 for 26 completed,
having made 1447 runs, but Iredale, who is third, has
made 2120 runs. He is the only Australian who has made
over 2000 in Sheffield contests. Turner is at the top of
the bowling with 73 wickets, average 19 each, but Trum-
ble has captured 131 and M’Kibbin 137, each averaging 20
runs per wicket. The Sheffield contests for the seven
years show what a splendid man Hugh Trumble has been
to Australia. Good old Giffen has made 1952 runs and
taken 171 wickets.”
* * * *
“ B.”: Went to see a cricket match (Balranald v.
Oxley) at Oxley t’other-day. Drove 65 miles ; temperature
116 in water-bag. Play commenced noon, and adjourned
at 12.30 in consequence of terrific dust. Resumed play
when temporary lull'took place-.' During afternoon
players entirely invisible to occupants of bush pavilion,
and scorers had to stand alongside batsmen to record
strikes. Leather lost in whirlwinds several times. Wind
increased; stumps drawn 4.30 owing to light being
obscured by dust. Banquet in bush pub. in evening, dust
awful. Three dust-storms during night. Drove back 65
miles in dust next day. Lost the road through specially
violent dust-storm. Temperature 117 in water-bag.

At Warwick Farm, on Saturday, backers got
back some of their Randwick losses, but only a little.
Only one real first favorite got home, Ferryman, a leggy
bay Gozo gelding, who went out at 5 to 4 for the Farm
Stakes, and won with his head in the air. The chestnut
Gibraltar gelding, The Watchdog, had no trouble in
gathering in the Flying. Handicap for “ Bill ” Forrester,
from Belle Isle and Amourette. The latter started first
choice, but got her backers their cash later on by lumping
11.2 to the van in the Welter. For the Stewards’ Mile the
consistent California and inconsistent Undaunted, both
well-backed, dead-heated after a great battle over the last
100yds., the favorite, Rock of Ages, getting lost in'the
crush. The two Carbine mares, Carberra and Fortune,
were the Warwick Handicap favorites, and they were the
two last to pass the judge. The sturdy brown Fairy
Prince, won by a head from Bombshell, Eulong a neck
away.

“Tote”: Early last week betting shops were
rushed for Warwick Farm double—Saraswati and War
God—and about £lOOO taken. Report said money was for
owner Bill Forrester. Later Saraswati developed a cold.
Then the other horse from the same stable, Watchdog,
was coupled with War God for £5OO. Going to
the. course by train' on Saturday, .War God got
down in his box and suffered injuries that made it
impossible for him to start. Forrester delayed the
elimination of Saraswati .so long that he was fined
£5 for late scratching. The Watchdog won the first leg
Of the double, and, all being well, War God’s chance of
landing the other end would have looked rosy. Writer
hasn’t nihch sympathy with Forrester. War God has been
wound-up for a long time, and, on his track form, might
have got away with any one or more of the Summer plums
and started at a remunerative price, but he was always
among the eleventh-hour scratchings, thus greasing to the
full that very much fatted sow, the double layer.

Texas Jack sports on Brisbane Cricket Ground,
Saturday, attracted 4000 people. Series of horses versus
cycle races were very interesting. Jack had tbe use of
four nags, while crack Q. cyclist, Mick Healy, was allowed
six pacemakers—J. Walne among them. Horse won the
mile easily in 2.12. Same result occurred in 2 miles event.
Time 4.4s—Healy 4 l-ssec. slower. Buck-jumping com-
petition supplied a lot of fun. One fractious brute plunged
fair on to his head and pegged out straight away. All con-
testants rode well. Vifevers got the money, but Weaver
and King seemed to be as good. Later, in 3 miles race,
Texas Jack’s prad ran into and smashed a bench on the
side, lacerating his stifle badly. If such a programme
could always be made sure of Spanish bull-figlits wouldn’t
be in it: .
• • * •
M. bruiser Otto Cribb has a match on with
Ned Burden at Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney) to-night
(Saturday). This brings Otto a rung or two higher up the
pug-ladder than he has yet risen. Burden, two years
since, was in the running for the lightweight champion-
ship of the continent. Challenges from him to Herb
M’Kell and others who claimed the title were religiously
ignored. Few know that Cribb can fight at the light-
weight limit (10st.). Recently old-man Jim Barron
hurled defiance at all Australians in that division, and
Cribb at once accepted, so that it is likely they’ll come to-
gether shortly. Otto, after this week, will have engage-
ments pending with. 12st. M‘Coll, of Armidale (N.S.W.),
and an unknown at St. Peters (Sydney). He says he’d fight
twice a week if the clubs would stand it.

Sydney pug-clubs (Golden Gate and Metropoli-
tan) are at outs and don't bruise in the same yard.
Youngest of the two (Metrop.) has seized upon same
nights (Wednesday and Saturday) as the opposition show,
and started with an offer of £4O for 8.7 novice men.
Golden Gate followed suit. Ere this appears in type the
fun will be at its height. Boxers are sure to benefit con-
siderably by this cut-throat business, and get a bigger and
fairer whack of the dollars raked in. Opposition’s the
life of trade, saith the trader whose commodity is stoush
* * * *
N. champion 7-stoner, Bert Weekes, had
another soft snap at Metropolitan A.C. (Sydney) last
week. He and Fitzpatrick fought for a fair purse .and a
side wager. Fitz. wasn’t in it at any period, though he
tried hard and took a lot of assault and battery. At the
close of the ninth round the tatter was aimed heaven-
wards on his account, which is our new patent way of
■saying that Fitz. skied the rag, full up to the brim of
standing before an opponent who was all legs and wings,
and being concussed without a hope of doing any con-
cussion himself. Bert never let Fitz. notch a point all
through, and yet several in the crowd called it a good
fight—yes, a good fight of the Napier-Hegarty description,
•one man doing all the fighting.
* * * *
“ Pug.”: Be recent Napier-Tim Hegarty boxing
fiasco, at Sydney Opera House. Said now that Napier’s
second (Mick Dunn) advised him to keep away from the
Melbourne crack during the first 10 rounds—hence
Hughie’s poor showing. Probably-it would have been
better had Mick Dunn advised his man to keep away from
Hegarty altogether. Many a pug. has lost a big fight
through altering his methods on the advice of seconds.
How would a cricketer, a rower, or a swimmer get on if
he changed his style of doing the thing, at the last
moment, on somebody’s advice? Griffo fought in a
manner peculiarly his own ; fancy him adopting different
tactics at the suggestion of a second 1 Black Peter Jack-
son never got pointers in his corner—he wouldn’t have
listened to them. “I fight my own way,” said Peter,
“ and don’t want any other man’s ideas.”

“ Ware-Hawk ”: Looks as if there’s something
that wants explaining in connection with the Felix-
Doherty negotiations for a second fight. Black Felix’s
black manager, Billy M’Clain, took his £5O deposit down
t’other day, though Doherty had covered it. Up to then
things seemed to shape well for another meeting.
Mr. M’Clain finds he is too busily engaged with
his professional duties to give the ring much attention.
He had his “ professional duties,” as a member of the
M’Adoo troupe, to look after before, yet the ring was
given a deal of attention, and the various Australasian
sporting papers deluged with high falutin’ cartels over
M’Clain’s signature, as “ manager for the present heavy-
weight champion of the provinces.” M’Clain thought he
had unearthed a marvel in something we had known all
about for years, but didn’t place extra much value upon.
Wonder if ink-hued Peter will really be taken to America,
to meet world-champion Jeffries for that fabulous purse
■and side wager.
* * * •
Crack lightweight pug, “Cocker” Tweedie,
and Jim Gaffney are liberally slangwhanging each other
■over a match that didn’t take place. Gaffney says Tweedie
wanted to square him to “throw it”—the consideration
being 60 per cent, of the purse—and -though he (Gaffney)
pretended to agree, he nevertheless made up his mind to
win—a task which, to his way of thinking, was akin to
“falling off a log,” for Tweedie couldn’t “ brush a bee
from a bucket.” Latter retorts that Gaffney is doing the
“ crawfish act” ; that he wants to get out, and invented the
squaring yarn to that end. Tweedie, whose 12 months in
Yankeedom have converted him into an out-and-out
down-easter, gets back with the statement that Gaffney
has “ cold feet,” is a confirmed “ quitter,' and a
“ squeaker ” of the most pronounced type, and would
sooner creep into a rat-hole and starve than fight.
* * * *
Sydney boxer, lanky, tomato-haired Jim Richard-
son, who tacks 44 0 f Honolulu” on to his name, raised
Cain in a fight at Gateshead (Eng.), latter end of Dec.
He met Jack Thompson in a go, the best of 20 rounds,
before a record house. Jim had a parlous time of it right
from the jump ; nothing but pure grit kept him up. In
the sixth three minutes the referee stopped proceedings
and declared Thompson the winner. Thompson went up
to shake hands wLh the vanquished bruiser, but Jim
wasn’t taking any. He rushed at the referee with blood
in his eye and some of Thompson’s gore on his hands, but
the crowd came between them. .Then the Australian
made a bolt for Thompson, who got home first and
laid his man out.- Jim assumed the, perpendicular
install ter, and was just about to in u P°n
all and sundry with a chair, which he brandished
menacingly when the police took a hand and matters
quietened down. Writer remembers Richardson fight-
ing a savage battle with Pheasant at the old Darlinghurst
Skating Rink (Sydney) eight or nine years ago.' The pair
went for each other like catamounts, and once fell head-
long out of the ring, carrying posts and ropes with them.
Several people in the vicinity had their clothes badly be-
spattered with blood. Richardson won. Prior to leaving
for ’Ome Jim challenged all Australia, but failed to con-
nect. ’He tried South Africa without success, and since
arriving “ down under ” has had several battles, but won
none—albeit he fought a great fight each time.

Kid M‘Coy showed how good he is by clean

every time.
• * * •
“Old Timer”: Obit. Marquis of Queensberry
(cabled from ’Ome last Friday). Late files tell that he was
found insensible in bed, at the Raleigh Club, London,
Dec. 18, and carried home in a critical state. At Oxford,
deceased made a name for.himself by getting all over his
contemporary lightweight undergrr.ds. with the gloves.
By-and-by, being a liberal patron of the bruising indus-
try, a new lot of boxing rules had his name appended to
them as a compliment. They were no great shakes, how-
ever, and have been altered and added to until the original
code has almost passed out of existence. The House of
Lords once kicked against the Marquis (John Sholto
Douglas) sitting in its gilded halls ; he blamed Rosebery
for the slight, and publicly challenged him to a thumping
match. Exposure of Oscar Wilde’s objectionable pro-
clivities was brought about through an action Oscar had
against Queensberry, who used language about him on a
post-card, or something of. the kind. Writer remembers
seeing the Marquis, at Foley’s gymnasium (Sydney), years
ago, and didn’t think much of him physically or mentally.
He Was built on wiry lines, and seemed then something
over 40.
* • » *
The belated “ Curse of Deeming ”: Alf. Sped-
ding, well-known as a Carlton footballer, died last week
in Melbourne, after five years of lingering consumption.
Spedding lived next door to Deeming’s historic cottage,
in Andrew-street, Windsor, and tendered material evi-
dence at the trial.
*** * . .
Bulletin tip, last week, re interprovincial
yacht-race between Sam Hordern’s White Wings and M.L.
crack Rainbow panned-out right. When a Bulletin tip
pane out right this paper gets, into a state of beastly arro-
gance. Rainbow won the rubber easily. Second race
was sailed on January 31. Breeze, moderate from S.S.E.
at start, backed round to \E., and fell away gradually as
afternoon wore on. This suited the M.L. boat to perfec-
tion. Race was twice round Manly course, Sydney Har-
bor (about 22 miles). White Wings stuck to it well in
first round, but the wind was too light for her
afterwards, and she dropped back till Rainbow crossed
the line 7min. 52sec. to the good. Archie Logan handled
the Aucklander, and Fred Doran the Sydney yacht. Each
had a leg in at this stage. Last of the series was sailed
Thursday, over same course. A light East Nor’-Easter
favored Rainbow, and she won with 12min. Bsec. in hand.
Same two men were at the helms. In light weather
White Wings, with her comparatively small area of
canvas, is no match for the M.L. yacht, though given a
stiff breeze there would always be a great race between
tlie pair.

P.A.Y.O. (Sydney) General Handicap, Satur-
day, was deprived of all interest through a disaster on the
starting-line. Defender, M.L. crack Rainbow, and Chin-
nery’s new yacht, Rona, collided. Latter two lost their
bowsprits. Rainbow got much the worst of the deal, as
in addition to the injury named her mainsail was damaged
and topsail torn beyond repair. Rainbow’s port-rail also'
carried away. Beyond losing her bowsprit, Rona sustained
no injury, and Defender—the innocent cause of all the
trouble —got away unscathed and engaged in the race
while the others hove-to to get rid of wreckage. Isea
won by 32sec., while Aoma finished lmin. 27sec. inside
what White Wings had to allow her. Course was round
Manly buoy and Watson’s Bay pile-lightr—about 17 miles.
Conditions were best and best. Saturday’s was to have
been Rainbow’s last test with local boats of her class.

‘ ‘ Bias Sydney bowling pennant matches have
reached the semi-final stage. St. Leonards, Balmain and
Redfern were put out on Saturday last. City, Petersham

* •- * •
The 300yds. swimming supremacy of N.S.W.
brought out nine competitors at Farmer’s Domain Baths
(Sydney) on Saturday. Balmain champion Craig (holder
of Australasian 220yds. premiership) won by a foot after a
hard tussle with young Dick Cavill, who fought out every
inch of the way. At 75yds. from home Craig was three
to the good, but Dick laid his ears back and strained
every muscle till they were level. Neck and neck the
two rounded into the last stretch (30yds.), and it seemed
that something must snap. In the end Craig won by a
foot. Bond (half-mile champion .of N.S.W. and quarter-
mile top-notcHer of Victoria) was third—lsyds. off—while
plugger Harry Smith filled fourth position. Time (3 min.
48 1-5 sec.) is 1 3-5 sec. outside world’s amateur record,
created by Australian Freddy Lane over same course in
February last year ; and 1 4-5 sec. speedier than profes-
sional champion of the world Nuttall’s best. Craig—still
in his teens—is only about three years older than Cavill.
Writer thinks that if Dick had made his effort a bit sooner
he would very nearly have reversed the result.
* * * *
Be The Bulletin par. (20/l/’00) attributing
Dick Cavill’s defeat in the furlong championship of Aus-
tralasia, previous Saturday’, to staleness. Writer rises to
say that that statement was correct. Proof came last
Saturday in the 300yds. premiership. Cavill stuck to
Craig over the whole journey, and only went under by a
foot in wonderfully fast time. Bond was 15yds. off third.
In the furlong race Craig led easily from start to finish
and won with 10yds. margin between himself and Cavill,
who only finished 3yds in front of Bond.
International 3-miles scratch race at N.S.W.
League’s acetylene-gas meeting on Sydney Cricket Ground,
Monday night, was won in great style by Bob Walne (Q.).
Tucker streaked away at the start and captured a couple
of lap-prizes before being displaced ; then Carpenter
notched a like performance. Entering the final quarter
all were going well, but 150 yards from home Walne shot
out like a rocket and won, pulling up, by 10yds. Megson
(N.S.W.) barely beat Gordon (Vie.), between whom and
Morgan it was as near a tie as could be. Time sm. o6 2 sec.
* * * *,
Adelaide Oval cycling track, alleged to be the
best in Australia, and now nearly completed, will be
opened in March. As a result, S.A Government, which
laid down an expensive track on Adelaide Jubilee Oval,
will fall in very badly, S.A. Cycling League having agree
to race on the new track exclusively.
* * * *
“C.M.G.”: How is it half-a-dozen professional
cyclists* are not killed in the course of every big event?
The smash is inevitable. I have never been at a meeting
that did not provide at least one promiscuous scattering
of men and machines, and have seen seven batches come
down in one afternoon ; yet not once has a, man of them
been damaged as seriously as the ordinary citizen is when
he falls over a barrel-hoop in his back yard, or spills him-
self into the wood-heap. At the Melb. A.N.A. sports on
Friday night, when a push was going Sheol for hides in a
lightning heat, ker-smash went a batch of competitors at
a bad bend, and for half a second the air was full of
crumpled riders and crippled machines and tyres and
spokes, and men were bouncing off the asphalt like a
cloud of astonished grasshoppers. And not one man had
to be returned to his parents on a shutter. Next morn-
ing’s papers had a story of a placid rider who ran cautiously
into a dog, and broke his arm and concussed his brain on
the spot. It would seem that the true art of riding on a
bicycle is the art of falling off. -
Old John Roberts—he was J. Roberts, jun.,
until his father died a while ago —looks likely to retain
the English billiard championship for another year. At
latest dates he had been giving 8000 points in 21,000 to
his challengers, and beating them one after another. On
“ form ” he is still apparently superior to young Dawson,
who met him on level terms last year and was altogether
outplayed at the business end of the match.
Since the George-street (Sydney) tram started
operations the Chinese Monte Carlo at the north end,
near Circular Quay, has slumped. The cheerful lallikm
who erstwhile stole in and risked half-crowns at the
games he did not understand, and marked tickets which
came to nothing, took a dislike to the trams (reason not
known, for the workings of the bell-bottomed intellect
are beyond ordinary comprehension) and transferred his
custom to Goulburn-street. Then came a police raid,
and the two combined convinced John, or, as he is
becoming more generally known, Pat, that Chinatown
was played out, and he mostly shifted to Goulburn-street
also, and there are now a number of cheery premises to
let. * *
* • * *
Bob Fitzsimmons was not long in finding out
the difference between a winning and a defeated man.
Bob asked Julian, his brother-in-law and manager, to re-
serve a box for him at a theatre in which Julian had an in-
terest, but the box was not reserved, and the pair
quarrelled and parted. Had Fitz, when in the heyday of
his glory, wished for the whole theatre it would probably
have been his for the asking. The Fitzsimmons-Julian
household was the most extraordinary on earth. Fitz was
divorced from the wife he took over from Sydney, and
married Julian’s sister, a plump and pleasing party of the
variety stage, whereupon Julian married Fitz’s divorced
wife ; and the marvellous conglomeration travelled toge-
ther for years.
* * * »
“ W.’s” opinion:—Recent successes of new
bowlers, M’Betn and Saunders, in interprovincial cricket
make one wonder what would happen if a second Charlie
Turner were let loose in a present-day match. Both
these trundlers toss up, left-handed, a common, medium-
paced straight ball, and batsmen are so unused to straight
bowling in these days of everlasting alleged off-theory
that they promptly get bowled. When the newness
wears off them, and they learn to bowl said off-theory,
hitters like Worrall and Hopkins will knock them out of
the sct>ring-book. “ Off-theory” bowling is becoming the
curse of Australian cricket. In the days of Turner and
“Giff.,” when it was good, off-theory was fairly effec-
tive, but very few batsmen get out off it nowadays for the
simple reason that 9 out of every 10 batsmen “pass”
everything pitched on the off, assuming that it is
treacherous off-theory. Occasionally the tenth has ago
at it, and as most of our present bowlers’ idea of off-
theory is any kind of muck pitched a couple of feet out-
side the wicket, he generally piles up a score ; but no
amount of illustration will persuade the average cricketer
to “ deal out ”to it. The amusing part is that most bats-
men bang to the fence lots of straight bowling, and re-
ligiously leave alone exactly the same stuff if it is a little
to the off. Yet it stands to reason that it’s easier to keep
a ball down when you know it won’t hit the wicket than
it is when you know if you miss it you’re out —but the
average cricketer can’t reason.
••* • •
George Giffen’s double record of 10,000 runs and
1000 wickets in first-class cricket places him right above
all other Australians. Who is likely to rival him ?
Trumble has a chance now that he has become a good,
solid batsman, and the only other man in view’ at present
is Noble. Murdoch stopped at 7000 runs (he didn’t bowl
at ail), and the only other Australian batsmen who have
made over 5000 runs in first-class cricket are A. Banner-
man, Lyons, Blackham, M’Donnell, and “Joe” Trott.
In bowling only one other Australian has captured 1000
wickets —viz., O. Turner, and he owed his success to the
wet English wickets, whereas Giffen was at his best on
our ow'n hard pitches. In all probability Australia will
not for a long time have another all-round champion of
Giffen’s calibre. Apropos, Giffen, in his best days, W’ent
through heavy training every morning with Indian clubs,
accompanied by brisk walking and boxing exercise. At
the latter pastime he was one of the best amateurs in
Adelaide. His performance of taking eight wickets for
280 out of N.S. Wales’ total of 803, this season, shows what
stamina Giffen still retains.

“Toka : Not generally known that the great
Carbine had a narrow escape from destruction before his
career had well commenced. He had won a race as a
two-year-old, and a crowd had gathered round him when
the spin was over. Carbine, being young and unwise,
chewed the bell-topper of one of the bystanders, who was
proceeding to drive his gamp into the horse’s eye when
.he was removed. He was removed about eight feet with
a fist. The assailant proved to be an incipient maniac,
and becoming less incipient as time went on he has now a
permanent residence in an asylum.

Memoriam notice from Hobart Mercury
(IS/l/’00): —“ NUGGET.—The late owners of the pony
Nugget regret to inform their many friends that he died
on January 2 at his owners’ late residence, corner Barrack
and Collins Streets, of sunstroke. A faithful worker to
the last.” After all, why not? Writer has known many
horses that deserved a kindly send-off when they went
where the good horse goes—and very few men.
Lindeman’s Wines.
The name of Lindeman has become indissolubly
linked with the Australian wine trade, and when
a good article is wanted Lindeman is a name on
which to rely. Increasing trade has necessitated
Mr. Lindeman establishing his offioe and cellars
on the ground floor and basement of Queen Vic-
toria Markets, George-street, Sydney. The
spaciousness of the cellars, and the amount of the
stock is a revelation to anyone who is not familiar
with the extent of Mr. Lindeman’s business.
400,000 gallons of wine, the greater portion fully
matured, is a modest computation of the stock.
The excellence of the wines is a fact granted by
every connoisseur. Among others, Mr. William
Hudson, the well-known London expert, pro-
nounces Lindeman’s to be equal to the best
European wines; and a chemical analysis by the
N.S.W. Govt, analyst of wines representing
the whole stock, proved them to be absolutely
pure, and comparing favorably with the best French
and German brands. Mr. Lindeman’s wines have
fained distinction wherever exhibited ; notably at
'aris, Bordeaux, London, and Calcutta; while
the four national prizes offered by the N.S. Wales
Government for the best dry red, best sweet red,
best dry white, and best sweet white wines were
secured by Lindenian’s. Mr. Lindeman occupied
the Exchange cellars for over forty years, and it
was there that he laid the foundation of his pre-
sent immense business.

Mr. Randolph Bedford has issued a con-
venient and valuable map of the Chillagoe mines
(North Queensland) which conveys a lot of infor-
mation about the lay of that enormous specula-
tion, and will be of great value to intending in-
vestors. All shareholders, also people who help
to carry on the angry Chillagoe correspondence in
the papers, should get it.
Mr. Alfred R. Moulton, late conductor of
the Princess Theatre orchestra, and better known
as the composer of the immensely-successful opera,
“ Lalamine,” is being tendered a big farewell con-
cert, at the Melb. Town Hall, on Saturday even-
ing, 17th inst. Mr. Alfred R. Moulton is proceed-
ing to America shortly, where he intends producing
his operas, so Melbourne will lose a musician of
uncommon merit.*
One of the “ difficulties ” standing in the
way of the (N.S.W.) Medical Practitioners’ Bill
passed away at Coonamble last week, aged 86
years. Edward Connor Cornelius’ name does
not appear among the legally-qualified surgeons.
Yet for nearly half a century he practised success-
fully “out back” on the Castlereagh. A kindly
old gentlemen, he will long be kept in memory for
his charity and good deeds.

In view of the present outbreak of military
fury, Messrs. Fairfax and Roberts, of 23 Hunter-
street, Sydney, vice-regal jewellers, have struck
an idea. They are appealing to the excited fancy
of the moment by issuing a dainty gold brooch of
five bars of gold bearing the notes of tne “Advance”
—the notes that called on the Soldiers of the
Queen to storm Talana Hill and Elands Laagte.
When the war is oyer and all the Boers are killed,
and the Absent-minded Beggar in distant years
takes the emblem to his uncle who dwells under
the shadow of the three globes, the notes will serve
as a soul-stirring appeal to that relative to advance
a little more than usual on the shecurity,*

Editor Bulletin.— l see “ Ilaresfoot,” in The Bulletin’s
last issue, refers to the old discussion Concerning my
father’s drama, the “Dead Heart,” and Dickens’ “Tale
of Two'Cities.” The subject Was thoroughly threshed
out when Sir Henry Irving produced Watts Phillips’
drama at the London Lyceum, when it was conclusively
proved that my father’s play was written before the novel
of “ The Two Cities.” Ben Webster, of the Adelphi
Theatre, read the drama to Dickens at Gads Hill as the
promising work of a young author. Dickens liked the
play. Webster, as was his custom with unknown authors,
shelved the dratua. When he heard Madame Celeste
(who was his partner and had quarrelled with him) was
going to produce a version of the novel, he looked out the
MS. of “ Dead Heart” (this was in ’59) and produced it.
My father’s play was a success, but the dramatised version
of “ The Two Cities ” was not, though I believe Madame
Celeste was very fine as Madame DCfarge in this version
—the part of the play. Neither was indebted to Dumas’
“ Chevalier de la Alaison Rouge.” It was not then the
age of translations, and I doubt if Dickens had read
Dumas’ work. My father found both episodes— i.e., a
man taking another’s place on the scaffold and also being
buried in the Bastille for 18 years—in Carlyle’s “French
Revolution.” He often spoke on the subject, as, to his
deep regret, it caused a coldness between himself and
Dickens.—Faithfully yours, Roland Watts Phillips.
Lyceum Theatre, Sydney. [The Bulletin adds an irrele-
vant P.S. to the effect that Miss Watts Phillips is almost
the only lady it knows whose handwriting isn’t an anguish
to the compositor. The average lady’s manuscript is one
reason why so few compositors are saved.]

A famous and almost-forgotten divorce was that of
Ellen Terry from the now veteran artist G. F. Watts. A
magnificent portrait of her still hangs in his great bronze-
ornamented studio ; but visitors are warned to pass it
without reference, lest annoyance should be caused to the
“ modern Velasquez ” and his young second experiment
in matrimony.
“ V.” : S.M. Herald, in a notice of Mr. R. Stewart, now
treasurer at Sydney Royal, says that as a, member of a
company leasing Melb. Royal from 1867 to 1877 he intro-
duced G. V. Brooke, &c. As Brooke was drowned in the
Bay of Biscay in Jan., 1866, it must have been the trage-
dian’s ghost the company engaged.
“ P.A.” : About prophets. Visited Mackay lately, where
Melba squandered about £30,000 worth of voice on as
unsympathetic audiences as could be found in all the
Land of Mysterious Murder. One old resident told me she
was “ pooty good.” People always applauded her songs
if the weather wasn’t too ’ot, ’cos she was a local cocky’s
wife and sung for nix. Sometimes they laughed at her
squeak}' voice and “them funny little quivers” in it.
Then I met the man who “ discovered ” her. He told me
so himself. He said to her: “ Look here, Mrs. Armstrong,
if I ’ad that voice of yourn I’d make a fortune.” “ How ? ”
says she. “ W’y, I’d go ’Ome and study ’bout three
years," says I; “ then I’d make my deboot and paralayze
'em. And she just done what 1 tole her.” At Geelong, a
few years ago, I heard Marie Elster give a magnificent
rendering of “ Sing, Sweet Bird,” to a crowded house, and
only half-a-dozen hand-claps broke the painful silence.
Charles Harding, who, at the age of 60, electrified a few
London audiences with the remains of the finest male
voice Australia ever produced, kept a wood-and-coal yard
in Auckland, and wasted a life there that could have been
spent in a triumphant career in the old world ; he was only
considered “ not too bad ” in that “ musical ” city.
Simonsen “ discovered ” him at the age of 40. ' On the
other hand, Jack Edwards was considered a perfect won-
der. When he sang “ Let me like a Soldier Fall ” in an
upstairs pub. parlor in Queen-street people would stand
open-mouthed to listen, and the ’bus traffic stopped.
When he came over to storm Sydney he secured a billet
in the chorus, and died after some years’ service, having
never got more than eight bars in advance of the crowd.
I told the story to a very famous English vocalist in the
same hotel parlor some years after, and played for him
while he sang his best. After each song he ran to the
window to see if the traffic was disturbed, but nary a ’bus
stopped except to set down a woman with a market basket.
“ C.M.G.’’: There are two parties fiercely disputing as
to the cause of the S. African war, and twenty different
opinions are prevalent, all wrong. I alone'appear to have
realised the serious fact that tw’o popular songs are en-
tirely responsible for the whole iniquity. Had “ Sons of
the Sea ” and “ Soldiers of the Queen ” never been written
the British would have been satisfied with the measure
of reform offered by Uncle Paul of Pretoria, but those
shrieksome ditties had been sung into every British in-
stinct, and every patriot and jingo was raving mad to
realise them —to demonstrate the truth of the philosophy
they breathed. “ Let me make the songs of a nation, let
who will make its wars.”
Hubert O'Grady, Irish “ author-actor-manager,” died
recently. Years ago, the film imported O’Grady, and lie
played a brief season at Melbourne Royal as the comic
cynical villain in one of his own bad dramas. The season
terminated with a speech from the “ star,” intended to
bring blushes to the cheek of the local press. O'Grady
was a most unreasonable visitor. He took his hatred for
Australia back to England with him, and fired bits of ani-
mosity across the ocean at our vile uncultured country
until his stock of adjectives gave out.
“Cockaigne”: Miss Nellie Stewart was never so chag-
rined as on last Boxing Night, at Drury Lane. She was
engaged for Jack, in the pantomime of the “ Giant
Killer,” and duly attended to dress for the performance,
but a cold with which she had been struggling made it
absolutely impossible for her to go on. Therefore the
part had to be given to a chit named Lowell, the under-
study, and she made a hit in it, while poor Nell spent the
festive evening weepfully in her sumptuous drawing-
room.
Demise: Piccolomini, the little prlma donna—the Patti,
as it were, of a few years before Patti. Piccolomini was
nobly born, as the butcher’s apprentice says in “ The
Power and the Glory.” Her father was a nobleman, her
uncle a Cardinal, and the family has yielded two Popes.
Her Traviata was an overwhelming London success.
Queen Victoria went as far as she could towards forbid-
ding this opera. In the hot time of the war for Italian
liberation, the Florentine populace started to taKe
the horses out of Piccolomini’s carriage, to tug it along
after the opera, but she screeched, “No! Go ana ngnt
for your country 1 ” Which was a better advt. than
t’other. Like most' prirna donnas, Piccolomini came to
poverty.

Striking how hard-up the Englishman is for decent
patriotic songs when he does feel like throwing up his hat
and bawling in the streets after pub.-closing time. The
recent wave of Jingoism spent itself mostly in more or
less awful renderings of “ Soldiers of the Queen ” and
“ Sons of the Sea,” two music-hall ditties of the deepest
dye. Now and then “Rule Britannia” would bob up,
but that about finished the repertoire, and the programme
would have to be started all over again. Now, an Irish-
man or a Scotchman could bellow away all night without
ever repeating himself. When did England last have a
decent patriotic song of its own—a land war-song especi-
ally ? What use is “ Britannia rules the Waves ” when the
fight is with an inland state and an ironclad can’t climb a
kopje ?
“ J.A.C.”: When in Italy I heard a story of Rossini
which I don’t think has ever been printed. One Ronzi
called on Rossini, and, announcing himself as both singer
and composer, proposed that he should first give the
master a specimen of his vocal abilities, and then play one
of his own compositions, in order to give Rossini a chance
of advising him as to which branch of the art he should
pursue. Accordingly he sang, and Rossini, when he had
finished, said, “ Beautiful, beautiful! ” Ronzi, delighted,
then sat down to the piano and played. “ Excellent,
excellent! ” was the comment this time ; but not another
syllable was forthcoming. “ But, caro maestro, you have
not yet told me which I should choose,” said Ronzi.
“ Well,” Said Rossini, after a pause of apparent delibera-
ti6n, “ if I were you I should try dancing ! ”
It was a small, disreputable co., calling itself a vaude-
ville combination, and it consisted of two managers, the
boss and his wife (or the boss and her husband) and two
played-out corner-men. The company could do a few
deplorable songs, and certain melancholydances, but the
great draw was a damaged cinematograph, the films of
which were mostly holes. The cinemat. got the large
headlines on the bills, and when the-co. struck a small
bush township it was the machine that drew in about 60
people all told. The tunes were run off, and then the
male manager got into his little caboose in the gallery,
and began to grind out his splendid living pictures. Ten
minutes later a deputation from the audience below
plucked him forth. “Say, mister,” said the spokesman,
“we want our money back.” “Money back!” gasped
the proprietor. “Why?” “Call that a sinnymatter-
graff ! ” said the representative man. “ Why, ’tain’t
nothin’ but an old magic lantern 1 ”
Melb. papers gush over the great successes of an Aus-
tralian actress in a far land, and hint at their vast appre-
ciation of her cleverness when on the local boards. Yet,
in point of fact, the young lady merely kicked her heels
in the dusk away in the back parts of the Melbourne
ballet, and never by any chance came within half-a-mile
of the footlights. And the papers never asked that she
should be brought further to the front.
A one-time very bad actor, now a very bad travelling
photographer, called at Murphy’s humpy and stayed until
next morning. Murphy had never seen a camera before,
and felt a bit scared at the look of it. When the photo-
fiend had the family grouped and was fixing the black
cloth, Murphy and the rest decided that he was going to
shoot, and they fled in all directions into the bush. Only
hunger made them return home, and by that time Johnny
and his weapon had departed.
Wilson Barrett seriously threatens to produce himself
in “ Henry the Fifth” when he takes his next autumn
turn at the Lyceum. It is a sacrilegious scheme. No
soulful Australian or American who has seen the two
actors would willingly permit Barrett to play the monar-
vcbial part which George Rignold practically “ created.”
The Bulletin, anyway, will always think of Handsome
George—and he was very handsome in his blonde wig and
tin crown—as Henry Vee by the Grace of God.

Robert Young, who recently “ knocked ’em ” at a
Melb. Liedertafel smoke concert with some musical
sketches in the Corney Grain style, was Clerk of Petty
Sessions at Hobart for some years. In ’9l’, when he went
’Ome for a holiday, Young made two matinde appearances
at the London Tivoli, and was offered an engagement
which he couldn’t accept. Now that he is a fullblown
pro. he finds Australia has little or no use for drawing-
room entertainers. Better to be a Sessions clerk than a
pianoforte funny-man.
During his tour through Maoriland, N.S.W, ex-Premier
Reid called in at a limelight show given at (Queenstown,
Otago, in aid of the fund for helping to deprive a small
nation of its independence. First picture represented a
badly-flooded town. “ This,” said the showman, “is
Sydney under water.” “ Nonsense,” said an exasperated
voice from the audience, “ that’s Brisbane. Sydney was
never under water.” The showman turned the limelight
on the voice and saw a large round head and a dazzling
reflection where the light rebounded from an eyeglass.
Then he went on with rather less confidence. A pond
with some ducks appeared, and the flurried entertainer
stated at random that it was Sydney Harbor. Here the
head and the dazzling eyeglass rose to say something,
but couldn’t find anything large enough to fit the
occasion, and two short legs trotted disgustedly out.
At Adelaide Royal, “Called to Arms,” staged to big
houses at contingent departure time, included as one of
the characters President Kruger, who, amid the execra-
tion of the gods, advised his generals to shoot the
prisoners and wounded, and hoist the white flag when-
ever necessary. Good old British fair-play 1
Dear Bulletin. —Quite a number of Aus-
tralian Cabinet Ministers have intimated their
intention of dismissing any State-school teacher
or other public servant who expresses opinions on
certain matters which do not coincide with the
Minister’s own. I don’t know about the other
provinces, but I desire to remind N.S.W. Educa-
tion Minister Perry that under the Electoral Act
it is a felony to intimidate, dismiss, bribe, or
punish any employe for his political opinions or
votes. Felony isn’t a nice thing, and there are
unpleasant consequences about it.—S.B.
Cardinal Moran’s announcement that he
is against the Boers because of their intense
Protestantism, and his wild whooping of the
Catholic party to help to down the Protestant
republic on religious grounds, was the sort of
mistake that a wiser man would not have fallen
into. And the R.C. Bishop of Perth dropped
into the same tactless error. The Boers are
probably the most hardshell nation of Protestants
on earth. Their State is an ideal one from the
Orange standpoint—a State where no Roman
Catholic can fill a higher billet in the public
service than that of policeman; and this public
sooling-on of the green dog is calculated to make
the other dog—the yellow one—feel sour and cross.

Ricciotti Garibaldi who has offered volun-
teers from Italy to fight for Britain in South
Africa, was a familiar figure in Melbourne in
the latter seventies. He lived at Brighton in
those days, and was a great friend of Marcus
Clarke. Garibaldi’s ambition was then to raise
poultry. He had a fowl-farm at Brighton and
hatched chickens, and sold them to Jenkins,
poulterer, in Swanston-street. A chemist friend
advised him to use an invention of his for heating
incubators. It turned out to be an explosive in-
vention, and the son of the great Red Shirt found
himself all of a sudden blown through the roof of
his hatching house. When he descended he was
smothered with eggs, and his splendid beard and
moustache had disappeared during the episode.
He mentioned his misfortune to Marcus Clarke in
confidence, and declared that it served him right,
for it was not a soldier’s duty to look after the in-
cubation of hen eggs. At one time, he had a
small billet hunting up small boys who didn’t go
to school, and he narrowly escaped being a sub-
urban town-clerk.
Amusements.

ANOTHER RADICAL CURE.
To Convince Sceptical Readers or Unbelievers
X:M:iT.A.TO:R,S COPY BUT BIVAL.
Dyspepsia and Neuralgia Promptly Cured.
The Case ov
MRS. ELIZABETH PHIPPS.
(Reported for “ The Redfern Times.”)
Within gunshot of Redfern Railway
terminus our reporter found the residence
of Mrs. Elizabeth Phipps, at 41S£ Elizabeth-
street, Surry Hills. This lady was at home,
and, in answer to enquiry, freely gave
an account of her recovery from a serious
case of ansemia and distressing neuralgia.
“My general health has not been good
since 1 was a child,” said Mrs. Phipps. “ I
was not strong, and some months ago I got
into an exceptionally low state. My diges-
tion became impaired ; I lost my appetite,
and had no taste for food I had, at times,
a sharp pain between the shoulders and in
the left side, but my most distressing
symptoms were neuralgic pains that, coming
up my neck, spread all over my head and
face with unbearable pain. At last this
pain became so serious that it affected my
eyes; they became weak and dazed. This
was followed by a nervous, trembling pain
in the arms, especially the right arm, so
that I thought I was going to be paralysed.
My husband tried to laugh me out of my
fears. I noticed that the neuralgia pains
were worse in hot weather, and always came
on towards sundown, and, continuing in the
night, robbed me of sleep. Towards
morning these pains would ease, but only to
return again the next night. Sometimes,
when I tried to lie down, suffering from this
terrible neuralgia, my throat would seem to
swell, my breathing became difficult, and I
felt as though someone was trying to
smother me. This feeling was so distressing
that even if I could have gone to sleep I
felt my breathing would have ceased.
Naturally, I was distressed, and in very
low spirits.”
“ What remedies did you try, Mrs.
Phipps ? ” enquired the reporter.
“I took a mixture from the chemist’s
and advertised preparations, but they were
far from improving my general health, and
very soon my neuralgic pains returned as
badly as before.”
“ What did you try next?”
“By accident I came across one of the
pamphlets of Clements Tonic, and, on read-
ing the testimonials, I found the names of
several persons I knew This gave me
confidence. I knew what was said was
genuine, and I became convinced that
Clements Tonic would suit my case and
cure me. This is how I came to purchase
the first bottle.”
“ And what wasthe result, Mrs. Phipps ? ”
“After taking the fourth dose, that is,
on the second day—for I took Clements
Tonic three times a day—l noticed a decided
change for the better, and as I continued
to take it the improvement was perceptible
and constant. I know it was Clements
Tonic that did me good, for, owing to our
changing house and being very busy, I
neglected for a short time to take Clements
Tonic, and I found that during that time
my cure was retarded. The first and most
welcome sign I had that Clements Tonic
was doing me good was the return of my
appetite. I could cat my meals ; and, after
that, all my other pains went away ; or,
rather, they did not come again, as they
used to do, pai ticularly the terrible pains in
my face and head that had come every night
so regularly and made sleep impossible.
Only last week I recommended a friend of
mine, who is in a very low state of health
from overwork, to take Clements Tonic,
and already it is doing her good.”
“Have you any objection, Mrs. Phipps,
to your experience being made public ?”
“Not at all. You can freely add my
name to the numbers of those I know wno
cannot speak too well of Clements Tonic.
It is a grand medicine.” *, v

SOAP MAKERS,
BY

TO
