AND TO
His Royal Highness
The Prince
of Wales,
Dorothy’s Prince.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dorothy dwelt in Dingotown,
Hard by the Lachlan’s banks of brown.
Never a lad
Dorothy had,
Never a lover to ride long miles
Merely to bask in her warm red smiles.
She was so proud
Sweethearts were cowed,
Horsebreakers e’en of wide renown
Left her at peace in Dingotown.
Once they were thick as bees that hum
Over the flow’rs when Spring has come ;
Never a day
Journeyed away
Bringing no horse with a rider bold,
Ready to offer his heart of gold.
Dorothy laughed,
Dorothy chaffed,
Said that she’d rather have stumps of gum,
Singing her epithalamium.
Dorothy dwelt in Dingotown,
Dreaming alone when the sun went down
Soft were her eyes,
Soft were the skies,
Sweet was the thrill in the dusky air,
Sweet was the thrill in her heart, and fain
Fairer than flame,
Visions that came,
Visions of love and the orange-crown—
Dorothy’s dowerlin Dingotown.
Dorothy’s delicate shell-like ear
Ever was tuned the sounds to hear—
Beat, beat, and beat,
Down the long street,
Clanging of horse-hoofs that should evince
Coming at last of her Fairy Prince ;
Over the ridge,
Over the bridge,
Riding, swift-riding, her Cavalier,
Prince of her Dreams, her Prince drew near.
Dorothy stood at her door one eve,
Watching the City of Make-Believe,
Column-and spire,
Sink with the Are,
Dying upon the horizon’s rim,
Fading out softly into the dim
Dusk of the night—
l. V Then came a light
Sourfu that the still dense air did cleave—
Beat' wild hoofs —in her dream to weave
“ Horse comes a-gallopin’ hard as hell,”
So they remarked in the Bush Hotel,
Quaintly and curt,
“ Rider is hurt —
Can’t hold his horse in. Bet you a crown !
Here is his spill-mark. God ! he’s down ! ”
Swift as a white
Ghost in the night
Dorothy sped—as folk still tell—
Straight to the place where the horseman fell.
Dorothy’s sire was sour and swift,
Sudden-tongued lover got sudden shrift,
But when a chap
Dropped in his lap
Speechless and stunned—well, what could he do ?
Just made a guest of him. So would you.
Dorothy too —
Dorothy knew,
Knew that her Prince by his charger biffed
Into her arms had come as a gift.
Dorothy’s Dad is kind and free,
Better companion could not be,
Pills up his glass,
Calls me an ass,
Laughs himself hoarse —I never wince— ■
Laughs himself sore at Dorothy’s Prince.
Good grey old man,
Laugh while you can,
Laugh till you’re black—for it won’t hurt me !
. . . Dorothy’s Dad is mine, you see.
CREEVE ROE.
The Demoralisation of Quigley.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The story of Quigley is the story of a man with a
weak chm who gave way to drink ; it is either a
temperance lecture or a psychological study,
according to the mental bias of the reader.
Quigley has a queer sort of interest for me because
I watched Ids beery slide into the pit. (I write
“pit ” with a small “p” of malice prepense—as I
would write Quigley with a small “ q ” but that it
would look eccentric.)
He was a young man when I knew him first,
and he had a rather pretty wife—a very good wife,
too, she would have been, if Quigley hadn’t had a
weak chin, or hadn’t given way to drink. He was
a handy young fellow —could canvass, collect, deal
and gather in the needful in divers ways and life
opened out before him like a smooth and level
road before a touring biker. Perhaps it was the
very facility with which he made a living that
caused Quigley to lose interest in the game so
early. A little struggling at the outset might
have stiffened his character a bit. Anyhow, he
soon added to his other gifts the fatal one of caus-
ing long beers to vanish apparently by magic.
The exercise of this talent soon took up so much
of his time that there remained very little to
devote to the sordid business of earning money,
which, besides, grew ever more, and more distaste-
ful. Pretty soon Quigley’s horizon was more than
three parts obscured by the rising of a long-
sleever in front of it. .
Those were days when I thought it possible to
reform a “drunk.” But I might as well have
attempted to hold a pint of water in my two hands
as hold Quigley out of the beery pit into which he
gravitated. .
Mrs. Quigley found time to have two pretty
little girls while Quigley was engaged in his long-
beer magic. She was a woman to whom resigna-
tion comes as a sort of second nature. She took
Quigley as a matter of course, as if she had never
hoped for anything better. And she took the
things that kind people gave her and the remarks
that unkind people made in the same undemon-
strative way as she accepted Quigley. She was
not withered or prematurely aged by the misery
and squalor of her life; never looking ahead, she
was not disheartened by the blank outlook. It
seemed as if nothing could shake her limp serenity
or rouse her from her invincible calm. Her look
might not be hopeful, but neither was it despair-
ing or even weary. It was indifferent; and she pre-
served her appearance of a pretty girl with nothing
in her. Hers was the abject resignation of a weak,
colorless nature placed in miserable surroundings.
I have known one or two ladies of comfortable
position who would have been exactly like Mrs.
Quigley in her circumstances. She was one of
those impalpable creatures which an avalanche is
powerless to injure. It was in vain that life
crushed her as it always crushes the weak; she
emerged unconscious of the pressure to which she
had been subjected. It was like pouring a ton of
coals on top of a ghost.
Quigley himself was unfortunate enough to have
the beginnings of a brain over his weak chin, and
also to possess nerves or something which punished
him horribly whenever they got a chance. These
nerves or brain-cells, or whatever they were,
assumed a sort of lordship over him and purported
to be his conscience. But in time Australian beer
sapped their power and eventually brought them
down from their high estate. I had hopes of
Quigley as long as he wore that abashed look
which told me that his supposed conscience was
still active ; but I felt that the last bit of blue sky
on his horizon would be soon shut out by the beer
pewter unless something happened.
Something did happen, and my hopes rose high.
(I was very callow then, and could not raise any
Mephistophelian enjoyment over Quigley’s sordid
slide.)
Quigley had a cousin, who looked rarely upon
the beer when it frothed, neither did he gaze very
hard at any strong waters —but the loveliness of
woman found great favor in his eye. He saw. the
Quigley household sinking deeper and deeper into
the mire of poverty, and in the goodness of his
heart he came to the rescue. The hash-houses
where he had sojourned for many years knew his
face no more, and Mrs. Quigley accepted him as
a boarder, in the same meek, unmoved way as she
accepted all things else.
Quigley, who now absolved himself entirely
from the obligation of working for his beer, found
no fault with the arrangement; but ere long the
tail-end of his self-respect rose up in flabby pro-
test. But my fond hopes fell to zero when, one
day, I saw my weak-chinned friend emerging
from the “Morning Star” with the. foam of a
recent “long-un” clinging to his bristly beard,
and his cousin following him, a cigar in his teeth,
and a more than usually hideous squint in his
close-set eyes. Esau had sold something more
precious than his birth-right for —a glass of beer.
I turned away in sickness of disgust, and carried
that fell squint in my memory for many a day.
His was a peculiar face—he had. two large front
teeth that seemed to rest upon his lower hp, and
his squint was wonderful to behold. A look .01
power supplied all that was needed to make him
irresistible to women of the class of Mrs. Quigley.
That lady presently blessed her beery husband
with a son, but that gentleman rejoiced not—nor
did he join in the smiles of the neighbors when
the little innocent, who already squinted awfully,
developed two large front teeth that rested on his
chubby little lower lip. Mrs. Quigley accepted
the awkward resemblance, and said nothing.
Quigley, after much cogitation, came to the con-
clusion that it behoved him to kick his sup-
planter out and resume possession of his house-
hold. As he needed moral support, he confided
in me, and I aided and abetted him in his design.
I kept him sober all day, and then, having forti-
fied him with three specially long glasses, led him
to his dwelling and waited.
Presently there was a confused sound of scuffling
and profanity, and then a form shot out on to the
pavement. It was Quigley’s.
After this he continued to eke out a precarious
existence on the occasional and uncertain beer.
Another little stranger appeared, with the squint
and later on the teeth, but it made no difference
to Quigley. Sometimes he slept in the gutter,
sometimes he was allowed to repose in the fowl-
house through the kindness of his cousin. On
special occasions, his wife let him into a back room,
where he wept beery tears over his miserable lot.
There was only one deeper circle into which he
could descend—the depth in which he would not
even know that his lot was miserable. _
Quigley was spared that last depth. _ And just
here, I grieve to say, the moral of this bald and
squalid tale goes awry as far as he is concerned
but then real life is seldom either moral or artistic.
An old and despised relative died in rags and
wretchedness, and the banknotes and deposit re-
ceipts that he left went to Quigley, as next of kin,
just as that miserable pariah was about to touch
bottom in the pit which is supposed to be bottom-
With an income of £2OO a year, Quigley has be-
come less beery, but considerably more revengeful.
His revenge, however, is of the passive order.
Comfortably housed in a pleasant suburb, his two
pretty little girls keep him partially sober till they
retire to their innocent cots. Then, assisted by
bottled beer, he gloats over the parlous case of his
cousin, who, with only £lOO a year very hardly
earned, and an increasing family of squint-eyed
children with large front teeth, snares a cramped
residence, in a narrow street, with a large, pale,
lymphatic woman, no longer attractive and rapidly
going into flesh. I saw. Quigley the other day.
He is beery still —exceedingly beery, but on
a year he carries it off with a F abstain an aplomb
most impressive; and on the whole he will pro-
bably die in a much stronger odor of sanctity
than his sober cousin.
L. COLONNA.
“ Melb.” : Melb. Public Library trustees
have just issued an octavo, which a lot of outsiders
would like to read. I presume it will be sold
cheap. In 1853 Governor Latrobe collected, from
58 Victorian squatting pioneers, little autobio-
graphic sketches of their work in this region, his
intention being to write a history of Victoria. In
1872, he sent all these memos, to James Graham,
M.L.C., who gave them to the Public Library,
where they have been embalmed ever since.
Going through the book, in which they are now
published (dated 1899), I find that the contribution
of Robertson, of Colac, is much the best. He
mentions the great sagacity of a sheep-dog. The
blacks speared the shepherd and drove off 700
sheep, out of 1500. The dog tried to round-up the
remaining 800, and take them home. However,
he could only manage 300, which he brought back
safely, late at night, to Gibson’s station. In the
morning, he led Gibson over to the spot where the
shepherd lay dead.

The man in the picture is It. Crawford, of
Smeaton Road, Clunes, Victoria. He is seventy-
five years of age. He looks and talks like a vigor-
ous man. He IS vigorous for his age.
The title of this article was suggested by a con-
versation recently held with Mr. Crawford, in
which he talked'of kidney trouble, and its effects
on the back. He gave an account of his own
experience with this trouble, which will bear
repetition for the benefit it may bring to others
who have found themselves in a similar condition.
. “An aching pain in the small of my back was a
perpetual torment to me for years,” said Mr.
Crawford. “My back was so bad I could not rise
after stooping, except with the greatest difficulty.
I had other signs of kidney disease, as the kidney
secretions were unnatural and my sleep was un-
settled. I want to say to you that I have obtained
relief by the use of Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills, which cleared up the kidney secretions and
improved my back; and I want to recommend
this medicine as a good kidney medicine not only
to any old people of my age, but to any and all
who have any signs of kidney trouble. You are at
liberty to publish these facts if you wish. They
are true and may help others.”

In re Billy Keats.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At 6 a.m. on Creation Day the Lord made out his
lists —
A brake to the rain, and a spur to the sun, and a
governor for the mists 1
A white suit He gave to the Polar bear to help him
live in the snow,
And a spring to the heel of the kangaroo wheresoever
they go ;
And He gave unto man a conscience to be king of all
his acts,
And a saving sense of proportion to help him mea-
sure facts —
To show him the way of his duty—in the ice or
thro ’ the fire —
To die in the glare of the limelight—or to struggle
for life in the mire ;
Duty for some in the battle—duty for some in the
house —
Duty for some to be militant man—duty for some to
be mouse,
And nothing esteems He so highly in the way of the
foulest crime
As the man who spits on his duty for the passing
praise of the time.
So over Billy Keats’ bones, I carve of these end-
less laws—
“ Man’s duty is ever man’s duty, with or without
applause.”
• * * *
Fruit pulp to the topmost storey—tinplates to the
basement floor —
And men who reeked fruit odors sang down the
shoot for more;
The middle of Apricot Week, and the fruit still
fresh from the tree,
And scores of tons yet waiting to be pulped for
oversea.
At the tinsmiths’ bench, crouched under the roof,
in the charcoal fume and haze,
The solderers closed the eternal jam through the
everlasting days;
The soldering iron was never cold —the spirit ever
flowed —
The only outside sounds they heard were the
hawkers’ cries from the road ;
The daily burn from the charcoal fire, and the
stink of the endless fruit,
And fortnightly fights at the fortnight’s pay with
the chance of the sack to boot;
Nine-pence a gross for closing (one hundred and,
forty-four)
Nine-pence, and find your solder, and thank God
that they find the store;
Twelve gross a day is a day for a man, working
early and late,
Two cans a minute tots in the day seventeen
hundred and twenty-eight,
Home at dusk if you’re decent, and glad to be
home with your kids ;
Away from the sickening fruit smells, away from
the soldered lids;
Soak in a pub, if you’re careless, and come to the
bench next day
With the fumes of the beer in your head-piece
docking a gross from your pay.
* * * *
Apricot Week had ended —but never for Billy
Keats
Was the work change more than a change of cans,
or the contents a change of sweets;
Fourteen years as boy and man he had soldered
and closed the can
Of fourteen damson seasons to sweeten his fellow
man;
As boy and youth he had held his bench and now
as the father of five
He soldered and closed at ninepence a gross to
keeps his folks alive;
Spirits of salts had corned his hands so that his
skin did die;
The work’s dead level kippered him —the brazier
smoked him dry;
And he cried aloud for something new as the tor-
tured pray for death
Cried for aught but the solder steel, with a prayer
in every breath.
• » * *
A brand new barber’s clerk came up with some
new subscription sheets—
“ This is the line, and the sum’s your choice that
you’ll ante, Billy Keats ;
Five bob for the Patriotic Fund as loyal workmen
should,”
And he mouthed of “ Empire” and “Glory,” and
Keats, he listened like wood.
But he promised to give the subscription, (which
meant at the ruling rate,
Nearly seven gross of closing—nearly a thousand
and eight) ;
But the loose-mouthed talk of “ Empire ” and
“ Glory ” had caught him fast,
And he soldered his cans in a day-dream—he had
found something new at last.
He saw thro’ the fumes of the charcoal a little hill
in a plain,
And himself all horsed and booted, charge the hill
through the leaden rain ;
On his housings were crimson purfles—in his soul
was a Viking deed —
(A deed that proves frustraneous —when your foe
is of,similar breed).
There were Boers that he shot, and Boers that he
lanced, and Boers he killed with his sword;
And afterwards —virgins, and bands of brass, and a
little nod from a lord.
And he hated the solder and salts more than ever
he’d loathed them before,
And he hated the stench of the raw, ripe fruit in
the bins on the sorting floor ;
And the squalid street in Richmond, and the
Saturday haggling for pay,
And the gross he closed that was missing, and the
awful average day.
He’d go with tne next contingent—he was sick
and tired of the life—
He’d go to find glory and change—and what would
become of the wife ?
• • • •
He thought it over and over that night as he lay
by her side,
And she slept the sleep of the weary (0, quickly-
faded bride !)
She would fret, of course, when he’d gone, but
she’d somehow manage to live,
(A man can always make answer, rest to his con-
science to give).
They’d be right, for Kate was plucky—(“dear
young old girl of mine),
I love ’em, but oh ! the glory, the stir, and the
change, and the shine !
Sick am lof the benches —I must have change or
die —
The change to the clean, sweet troopship, and the
plain with the sun in the eye.”
And then his old self to him spake, crying out
against this sin,
And said, “ Leave the war to its makers and let
the greater win—
God hath given thee thy duty, and it is straight
and clean, *
And your guerdon and glory are both of the best—
a love neither selfish nor mean.
What has Australia to do with the fight of the
foreign man’s ?
Australia has no quarrel—stick to your soldered
cans.
God made a blue wall to this land—a wall to this
great whito isle—
To keep the old world factions out—to weaken the
old world guile.
War shall not venture to our coasts, if but our
men are free—
The Lord hath given us bulwarks in three thou-
sand leagues of sea.
What if thy soul is [sick of the awful deadness of
. life,
Hath she no wish for a change, thy stale and
monotonous wife ?
Marriage for women like these is duller than can
be for man—
Bearing and suckling and weeping—to realise sex
and her ban,
You’ve one true mate in all the world, and that
true mate is here.
Keep her young by your kindness—a wrinkle is
more than a year.
She smothers her wants in affection—she chides
_ her desire for a change.
Think you the creek is forgotten—the murmurous
creek in the range;
That she thinks not of glens at the Bern tree, nor
the plan of your courting that day,
Nor the stooks at the Horsham harvest, nor the
fields of the new-moon hay.
The change that thou seest is fair, but fairer are
love and duty —
Thy war is against the wolf that destroys the
children’s beauty.
And all. the fine, purpling velvet, and the pomp
and the ruddy gold,
Shall they be your wife’s mangild ?—no, never a
, coin nor fold,
None will give the children a crust nor make thy
widow a queen
(Because of thy kingly deeds), when thy grass puts
, on its green.
Relief funds are not fathers, and charity is not a
sire,
And the public dole to the widow cannot quench
; her desire.
Here is your duty—your war—stand by it and to
it —a man
Gan find equal glory in killing another, or solder-
ing a can.
It is only the way that you do it, for glory lies in
. degree,
To. be the Most Thorough Dustman or the
• Highest Berg at Sea.
But the man’s Desire spake up and said, “ My
soul cries out for change—
Give me the swirl of a flooded stream, or the sight
of the sun on a range ;
I go, and none can stay me—Love warms not dead
Desire;
Gold can not bribe the granite—wood can not calm
the fire !”
Then the man’s old self in anger cried, “So soon
do you forget
With your careless doffing of garments, and your
careless denial of debt.
The woman is thine and of thee—the children thy
bone and blood ;
And you, the bee on the petal; she the fading
bloom of the bud.
Go to the hollow glory, and the change that will
slake your drouth,
And the loose-lipped brag of Kipling who killed
poetry with his mouth,
Haste to your newest pastures—leave the wife a
navvy’s farewell ;
And send your babes to the gutter—and your soul
to a first-class Hell.”
• • * •
Apricot Week sees Billy Keats no more by his
closing bench—
He went to fight without a cause—and he left his
leg in a trench—
And his soul left him in hospital—(no brass band
in the scene) — ' v_ ■ . -
And while his widow slowly starves his grave puts
on its green—
And over his virescent mound I carve of the
primal laws—
“ Man’s duty is ever man’s duty”—with or with-
out applause.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Bridge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We drove from Dunedin to Waitati in a drag, and
it was generally admitted that Dalrymple was the
life and soul of the party. He and I remained at
the hotel to attend to the horses and get some
additional provisions, while the others proceeded
across the railway bridge to the beach beneath the
cliffs.
Dalrymple and I followed soon afterwards each
laden with a couple of picnic baskets. The bridge
is 70 feet long and 45 feet above the water. The
rails are laid on ironbark girders, and for the
benefit of surfacemen and platelayers a twelve-inch
plank is laid between the rails.
Dalrymple took the bridge first, but had not
gone far before he was seized with panic. His
knees trembled, and as he looked back atme his face
was deathly pale. Then he crouched down with
eyes riveted on the water —paralysed with tfear !
I relieved him of his baskets and got him back to
terra firma. It was a considerable time before I
could induce him to make another attempt, but at
last, by allowing him to hold onto my shoulders, I
got him over the bridge. I well remember how he
trembled, and how his heart beat as we shuffled
across the plank.
We walked along the railway-line in silence.
Only once did he speak. “How did the girls
cross ? ” he asked hoarsely.
“Just as you did,” I said, “ holding someone
by the shoulders.”
“What a coward you must think me!” Dal-
rymple said, looking at me with wistful eyes.
“Oh,” I said, “nervousness is nob necessarily
cowardice! ”
We soon joined the party on the beach, and
Dalrymple’s depression was accounted for by ,a
headache. In a shamefaced way he remained
with the girls, and I saw very little of him during
the day.
Before starting for home, we decided to have a
swim in the channel through which the sea flows in
over an expanse of sand and mud flat. But as the
tide was now ebbing, the current was much stronger
than we had expected, and almost before we
had realised what had happened young Morgan
was carried into the open water. _ We heard his
cry for help, and some of us were in the act of fol-
lowing when Dalrymple rushed up.
“ Stop ! ” he shouted, “ It is my right to go ! ”
and, plunging off the steep, shelving sand, he
swam towards the boy.
The current took them out rapidly, until we
could only get an occasional glimpse of their
heads. They were together, however, and
Dalrymple was evidently supporting the boy.
Presently, seeing that they drifted in a curving
course towards a sandbank about a mile from
where we stood, we ran in that direction, and
arrived in time to pull them out of the breakers.
We soon revived Morgan, but Dalrymple was
dead.

PASTEURISED
BUTTER • • •
The same remarks that apply to milk apply equally to butter. Pasteurised butter is made from the very finest
cream, consequently the butter has a fine aroma that only Pasteurisation can give. There are a few, we are
pleased to say, only a few, idiotic folks who will not be convinced of the value of the process ; but it is satisfactory
to know that these are getting less daily. The difference in price between Pasteurised, beautifully-flavored butter and the
ordinary make is only |d. per lb. It would pay consumers well to give six times this difference. Heads of households should insist
that their grocer supplies Pasteurised butter, and if he will not, send your Orders to US, and we will deliver it at your doors.
Good butter such as we have described is appreciated always, whereas at afternoon teas or for ordinary use bad butter is dear
at any price. The famous “ Berry ” brand Pasteurised butter
can be obtained only from this Company.
